Defending Real Disjunction: A Four-Part Reply to Objections
Author gives a four-part answer defending the logical properties of real disjunction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the four-part defense of a real disjunction and explain how each objection is rebutted by appealing to the constancy of mutual opposition.

Core Idea
The mutual opposition in a real disjunction persists regardless of assuming one disjunct.

This passage presents a detailed response to four objections that challenge the logical properties of a real disjunction (al-munfasila al-haqiqiyya). The author defends the core idea that the opposition (al-ʿinād) between the two disjuncts remains constant, even after one is assumed. By working through each part of the reply, you will see how the author uses careful analysis of implication and composite negation to uphold the exclusive nature of real disjunction. This lesson will guide you through the logical moves in each of the four answers.

Guided Path
1
Locate the four parts of the response
The author marks four points with letters (alif, ba, ta, tha). Identify where each begins and summarize the objection being addressed based on the response. This helps you see the structure of the dialectical defense.
Each part corresponds to a distinct logical challenge.
2
Extract the definition of real disjunction
From the first answer, note the definition: a real disjunction involves mutual opposition such that the existence of one disjunct entails the non-existence of the other in all cases. Underline 'العناد' as the key relation.
This definition is the foundation of the entire defense.
3
Trace the role of opposition in the first and second answers
In the first answer, the author argues that even after assuming one disjunct (e.g., 'it is even'), the opposition remains: if it is even, it cannot be odd. In the second answer, the opposition is used to establish the implication between evenness and non-oddness. Write out the logical relationship: even → not odd, odd → not even.
The constancy of opposition is the key rebuttal to the idea that the disjunction disappears.
4
Analyze the third and fourth answers on implication and composites
The opponent uses composite negation (e.g., denying the composite 'even and odd') to attack the implication. The author responds by distinguishing the composites and showing that negation of one composite does not break the implication, because the opposition (ʿinād) remains actual. In the fourth answer, he argues that if a thing implies two others, it implies each individually—this resolves an apparent contradiction.
These answers demonstrate refined logical analysis using the notion of composites.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب [1] فنقول في الأول سلمنا أن إنتاجه مبني على ذلك لكن لم قلتم بأن ذلك لا يكون باقيًا بعد قولنا إنه زوج أو إنه فرد بل يكون باقيا؛ إذ المفهوم منه هو العناد بينهما على وجه يلزم من تحقق هذا انتفاء ذلك وبالعكس؛ وذلك يكون باقيا على تقدير أن يكون زوجا حتى إذا لم يكن التقدير وهو كونه زوجا يلزم أن يكون فردا . ولا يقال إنه إذا كان باقيًا على هذا التقدير يلزم على هذا التقدير أنه إذا لم يكن زوجا يكون فردا؛ وذلك في حيز المنع فإن كونه زوجًا مما يستلزم نقيض كونه فردا على جميع التقادير. وكذلك كونه فردًا لنقيض كونه زوجًا، وبالعكس في كل واحد منهما أيضًا وإلا لا تكون المنفصلة حقيقية. ولو كان كذلك لكان من اللوازم أن يكون فردًا على هذا التقدير إذا لم يكن زوجا وإلا لا يكون كونه فردًا من لوازم عدم كونه زوجًا على جميع التقادير. [ب] وفي الثاني نقول: سلمنا بأن المحال لازم عند الاستثناء سواء كان التردد باقيًا أو لا يكون باقيًا لكن لا يلزم منه انتفاء كونه رفعا في نفس الأمر ؛ وذلك لا يكون مانعا عن اللزوم. وأما انتفاء ذلك المجموع وهو الزوجية مع عدم الفردية فكما أنه يلزم من انتفائه انتفاء ذلك المجموع؛ فكذلك يلزم من انتفائه انتفاء هذا المجموع أيضًا وهو الزوجية مع الفردية بل يلزم من انتفائه انتفاء المجموع المركب من هذه الثلاثة. وانتفاء المجموع مما يحقق اللزوم بين الجزء الأول وعدم ما عداه؛ وإنه مما يحقق اللزوم بين كونه زوجا وعدم كونه فردًا لاستحالة انتفاء ما عداه بانتفاء اللا فردية. وإن قيل كيف هو وانتفاء المجموع الأول مما يحقق هذا فنقول كما أن انتفاء ذلك المجموع مما يحققه؛ فانتفاء المجموع الثاني مما ينافيه. ولأن اللزوم فيما نحن فيه باعتبار العناد بين الزوجية والفردية وإنه مما يحقق اللزوم بين الزوجية واللا فردية لا بين الزوجية والفردية فانتفاء المجموع وإن كان مؤثرًا فيما ذكرتم مطلقا فلا نسلم كونه مؤثرًا في هذه الصورة بل لا يكون مؤثرا؛ ولا يلزم أن يكون الملزوم منافيًا للازم. [ت] وفي الثالث نقول: ما ذكرتم يدل على انتفاء أحد المجموعين لكن انتفاء أحد المجموعين لا يكون مانعا عن اللزوم فإن المانع عن اللزوم يكون مانعا عن العناد فيما نحن فيه؛ والعناد متحقق؛ إذ الكلام فيه. ولأن الكلام فيما يكون مستلزما على جميع التقادير كما مر . ولو كان كذلك لكان انتفاء المجموع مجرَّدًا عن المانعة. [ث] وفي الرابع نقول: ما ذكرتم من المجموع لا يكون منتفيًا، أو الانتفاء لا يكون مانعا وإلا لا يكون العناد متحققاً بين الزوجية واللا زوجية. ولأن ما ذكرتم من الدليل كما يدل على انتفاء ذلك المجموع فكذلك يدل على انتفاء ذلك المجموع وهو كون الزوجية مستلزمة لهذا دون ذلك. وإن قيل هذا في حيز التعارض فنقول إنما يكون كذلك إذا كان كونها مستلزمة لكل واحد منهما منافيًا لكونها مستلزمة لما ذكرناه وهو عدم الفردية بل لا يكون منافيًا. فإن الشيء إذا كان مستلزمًا للشيء ولغيره كان مستلزمًا لذلك الغير لا محالة؛ وقد مرّ الكلام فيه؛
As for the answer: we say regarding the first point: we grant that its production is based on that, but why do you say that that does not remain after we say 'it is even' or 'it is odd'? Rather it remains, because what is understood from it is the opposition between them such that the realization of this entails the negation of that, and vice versa; and that remains on the assumption that it is even, so that if the assumption (that it is even) were not made, it would entail that it is odd. And it cannot be said that if it remains on this assumption, then on this assumption it entails that if it is not even, it is odd; that is subject to denial, because its being even entails the negation of its being odd in all cases. Likewise its being odd (entails) the negation of its being even, and vice versa in each of them as well; otherwise it would not be a real disjunction. And if it were so, then it would be among the necessary consequences that it is odd on this assumption if it is not even; otherwise its being odd would not be a necessary consequence of its not being even in all cases. [B] And regarding the second point: we say: we grant that the impossible is necessary upon the exception, whether the disjunction remains or does not remain, but that does not entail the negation of its being a removal in reality; and that does not prevent the implication. As for the negation of that composite—namely evenness with lack of oddness—just as its negation entails the negation of that composite, so too its negation entails the negation of this composite as well—namely evenness with oddness—rather its negation entails the negation of the composite composed of these three. And the negation of the composite actualizes the implication between the first part and the absence of what is other than it; and it actualizes the implication between its being even and its not being odd, because it is impossible for what is other than it to be negated by the negation of non-oddness. And if it is said: how so? The negation of the first composite actualizes this? We say: just as the negation of that composite actualizes it, so the negation of the second composite contradicts it. And because the implication in our case is by virtue of the opposition between evenness and oddness, and it actualizes the implication between evenness and non-oddness, not between evenness and oddness, so the negation of the composite, even if it is effective in what you mentioned absolutely, we do not grant that it is effective in this case; rather it is not effective; and the necessary condition does not have to be incompatible with the necessary consequence. [T] And regarding the third point: we say: what you mentioned indicates the negation of one of the two composites, but the negation of one of the two composites does not prevent implication, because that which prevents implication prevents opposition in our case; and opposition is actual; for speech is about it. And because speech is about that which is necessary in all cases, as previously. And if it were so, the negation of the composite would be free from the preventing factor. [Th] And regarding the fourth point: we say: what you mentioned of the composite is not negated, or the negation does not prevent; otherwise opposition would not be actual between evenness and non-evenness. And because what you mentioned of the proof, just as it indicates the negation of that composite, so too it indicates the negation of that composite—namely that evenness is necessary for this and not that. And if it is said: this is in the realm of contradiction, we say: it is only so if its being necessary for each of them is incompatible with its being necessary for what we mentioned—namely the lack of oddness—rather it is not incompatible. For if something is necessary for a thing and for another, it is necessarily necessary for that other; and the speech about it has passed.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage is a detailed dialectical response to objections concerning the nature of real disjunction. The author addresses four points (marked with letters alif, ba, ta, tha).

In the first point, the author argues that when we say 'it is even or it is odd', the opposition (عناد) between the two disjuncts remains even after assuming one of them. The opponent claimed that the disjunction disappears once we assume 'it is even', but the author insists that the logical relation still holds: the assumption of evenness entails the denial of oddness in all cases, and vice versa. If it didn't, it wouldn't be a real disjunction.

In the second point, the author discusses the implication between evenness and non-oddness. He accepts that an impossibility is necessary when we make an exception (استثناء), but disputes that this negates the relation. He analyzes composites of terms and shows how the negation of one composite affirms the implication between evenness and non-oddness, not between evenness and oddness.

In the third point, the author responds to the claim that the negation of one composite prevents implication. He argues that the negation of one composite does not prevent implication because the opposition (عناد) is still actual; the preventing factor would negate the opposition itself, which is not the case.

In the fourth point, the author addresses the opponent's proof that a composite is negated. He shows that even if a composite is negated, it does not prevent the implication, because opposition between evenness and non-evenness remains actual. He further argues that if something implies both A and B, it necessarily implies each individually, so there is no contradiction.

This passage is highly technical, using examples of evenness (زوجية) and oddness (فردية) to illustrate the logical properties of real disjunction. The author maintains that the core relation of mutual exclusion (عناد) is constant, and the opponent's objections do not undermine it.

Key Terms
المنفصلة الحقيقية (al-munfasila al-haqiqiyya): A disjunction where the two parts are mutually exclusive in all cases, such that affirming one entails negating the other and vice versa. [Note: No significant shift; this term is standard across later logic texts.]
العناد (al-'inād): The mutual opposition between two disjuncts such that the existence of one necessitates the non-existence of the other. [Note: In some earlier texts, 'inād may refer to any conflict, but here it specifically denotes the logical relation in real disjunction.]
الاستثناء (al-istithnā'): The logical operation of affirming one disjunct in a disjunctive syllogism to deduce the negation of the other. [Note: Stable across the tradition; used in the context of disjunctive syllogism (qiyās istithnā'ī).]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key feature of a real disjunction (al-munfasila al-haqiqiyya)?
A) The two disjuncts can both be true at the same time.
B) There is mutual opposition such that affirming one entails negating the other in all cases.
C) Negating one disjunct does not produce anything.
D) The disjunction only applies to temporal matters.

Key Takeaway
The author demonstrates that the mutual opposition (ʿinād) in a real disjunction is preserved even when one of the disjuncts is assumed, thereby refuting objections that would weaken the logical force of exclusive disjunctions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the following statement: 'The Qur'an is either created or uncreated.' Explain why this is a real disjunction. What are the two disjuncts? Show that affirming one entails negating the other in all cases.
?
In a hypothetical debate, an opponent argues that if we assume the Qur'an is created, the disjunction disappears. Using the author's first answer, construct a response that defends the persistence of the opposition.

Exclusive Disjunction (Māni'a min al-Jam') and Its Inference Rules
Author distinguishes non-real disjunction (exclusive) and states its inference rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the exclusive disjunction, state its two inference patterns, and explain why denying a disjunct yields no conclusion.

Core Idea
Exclusive disjunction allows only one valid inference: affirm one to deny the other.

In the previous lesson, we studied real (exhaustive) disjunctions, which are used in scientific demonstration. Now we turn to non-real disjunctions, specifically the exclusive type (al-māni'a min al-jam'). This type is weaker because both disjuncts can be false, which restricts the inferences we can make. The passage explains its rules and notes that it is not used in the sciences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition
Read the author's definition: exclusive disjunction prevents both parts from being true together, but allows both to be false. This is unlike real disjunction which also prevents both false.
The term 'māni'a min al-jam' literally means 'preventer of combination'.
2
Identify the example
The example is 'this thing is either a tree or a stone'. Note that these two categories cannot overlap (nothing can be both tree and stone), but something could be neither (e.g., a rock).
3
Rule for affirming one disjunct
If you affirm one disjunct (e.g., 'it is a tree'), you can conclude the negation of the other ('so it is not a stone'), because they cannot combine.
This is the only valid inference pattern in exclusive disjunction.
4
Rule for denying one disjunct
If you deny one disjunct (e.g., 'it is not a tree'), you cannot conclude the other ('it is a stone'), because both could be false simultaneously (e.g., it could be a rock).
This is the key difference from real disjunction: in real disjunction, denying one forces the other.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا من الأقيسة الاستثنائية ما يكون مشتملا على المنفصلة الحقيقية. فأما إذا لم يكن مشتملا عليها بل يكون مشتملا على غير الحقيقية نحو المانعة من الجمع فذلك لا يستعمل في العلوم وهو مثل قولنا هذا الشيء إما أن يكون شجرًا وإما أن يكون حجرًا، فاستثناء عين أيهما كان ينتج نقيض الباقي لاستحالة الاجتماع بينهما. فأما استثناء النقيض فذلك لا ينتج أصلا لصحة ارتفاعهما معًا؛ فلو قلت لكنه شجر يلزم أن لا يكون حجرًا. وكذلك إذا قلت لكنه حجر يلزم أن لا يكون شجرا. فأما
Thus from the exceptional syllogisms are those that include the real disjunction. As for those that do not include it but instead include a non-real disjunction, like the exclusive type, that is not used in the sciences; it is like our saying 'this thing is either a tree or a stone.' Affirming either one of them produces the negation of the other, because it is impossible for them to combine. As for affirming the negation, that produces nothing at all, because it is possible for both to be false simultaneously. So if you say 'but it is a tree,' it follows that it is not a stone. Similarly if you say 'but it is a stone,' it follows that it is not a tree. As for...
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage defines the non-real disjunction, specifically the exclusive type (المانعة من الجمع), and explains its inference rules.

The author begins by stating that among the exceptional syllogisms (الأقيسة الاستثنائية) are those containing a real disjunction (المنفصلة الحقيقية), which were discussed earlier. Now he turns to those containing a non-real disjunction, such as the exclusive disjunction where both parts cannot be true together but can both be false. He gives the example 'this thing is either a tree or a stone'—it is impossible for something to be both a tree and a stone simultaneously, so the two disjuncts are mutually exclusive in terms of truth (they cannot both be true).

He then explains the inference rules: If you affirm one disjunct (e.g., 'it is a tree'), it produces the negation of the other ('it is not a stone'), because they cannot coexist. Similarly, affirming 'it is a stone' yields 'it is not a tree'. However, if you negate one disjunct (e.g., 'it is not a tree'), that does not produce any conclusion, because both could be false (something might be neither tree nor stone, e.g., a rock). In other words, from the denial of one disjunct, you cannot infer the affirmation of the other.

The author notes that this type of disjunction is not used in the sciences (العلوم), implying that real disjunction is the proper one for demonstrative reasoning. This passage is foundational for understanding the difference between real and exclusive disjunction in Islamic logic.

Key Terms
المانعة من الجمع
استثناء النقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In an exclusive disjunction (مانعة من الجمع) like 'this is either a tree or a stone', what follows if you affirm 'it is a tree'?
A) It is a stone.
B) It is not a stone.
C) Nothing follows.
D) It is both a tree and a stone.

Key Takeaway
Exclusive disjunction yields a conclusion only when one disjunct is affirmed; negating a disjunct leaves the conclusion uncertain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The Syllogism
beginner

Two Types of Disjunctive Syllogisms: Exclusive and Exhaustive
Author defines two types of disjunctive conditionals and their rules

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between man'at al-jam' and man'at al-khuluw disjunctions and identify the valid inference operation for each type.

Core Idea
Knowing which disjunction type you have tells you whether to affirm or negate a disjunct to get a valid conclusion.

Not every disjunctive syllogism works the same way. The author explains two types: exclusive (man'at al-jam'), where both parts cannot be true together, and exhaustive (man'at al-khuluw), where both parts cannot be false together. Understanding the difference is crucial for using disjunctive reasoning correctly in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunction type
Check whether the disjunction is exclusive (man'at al-jam') – the two parts cannot both be true – or exhaustive (man'at al-khuluw) – the two parts cannot both be false. In the passage, the first example 'either a tree or a stone' is exclusive; the second 'either not a stone or an inanimate object' is exhaustive.
Use key context clues: if both can be false but not both true, it's man'at al-jam'; if both can be true but not both false, it's man'at al-khuluw.
2
Apply the rule for exclusive (man'at al-jam')
For man'at al-jam', affirming one part (istithna' al-'ayn) yields the negation of the other. The author says: 'If you say it is a tree, it necessitates it is not a stone.' But if you negate one part, no conclusion follows because both can be false (e.g., it could be a man).
This rule is like the exclusive OR in modern logic.
3
Apply the rule for exhaustive (man'at al-khuluw)
For man'at al-khuluw, negating one part (istithna' al-naqid) yields the affirmation of the other. The author says: 'If you say it is not not-a-stone, it necessitates it is an inanimate object.' But affirming one part yields no conclusion because both can be true (e.g., an inanimate object that is not a stone).
This rule mirrors the inclusive OR when negating one disjunct.
4
Contrast with the true disjunctive
The true disjunctive (al-munfasila al-haqiqiyya) combines both properties: both exclusive and exhaustive. That allows full deduction: affirming one gives negation of the other, and negating one gives affirmation of the other. This type is used in scientific demonstrations.
The passage warns that ambiguities can arise in non‑true disjunctives, so careful reflection (ta'ammul) is required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا من الأقيسة الاستثنائية ما يكون مشتملا على المنفصلة الحقيقية. فأما إذا لم يكن مشتملا عليها بل يكون مشتملا على غير الحقيقية نحو المانعة من الجمع فذلك لا يستعمل في العلوم وهو مثل قولنا هذا الشيء إما أن يكون شجرًا وإما أن يكون حجرًا، فاستثناء عين أيهما كان ينتج نقيض الباقي لاستحالة الاجتماع بينهما. فأما استثناء النقيض فذلك لا ينتج أصلا لصحة ارتفاعهما معًا؛ فلو قلت لكنه شجر يلزم أن لا يكون حجرًا. وكذلك إذا قلت لكنه حجر يلزم أن لا يكون شجرا. فأما إذا قلت لكنه ليس بشجر فلا يلزم أن يكون حجرًا أو لا حجرًا. فكذلك إذا قلت لكنه ليس بحجر فلا يلزم أن يكون شجرًا أو ليس بشجر: فإن من الأشياء ما ليس بشجر ولا بحجر أيضًا نحو الإنسان والفرس وغير ذلك. وعلى هذا إذا كانت الأجزاء ثلاثة أو أربعة مثلا . وأما إذا كان مشتملا على المانعة من الخلوّ كما في قولنا هذا إما لا حجر وإما جماد فاستثناء نقيض أيهما كان ينتج عين الآخر لاستحالة ارتفاعهما معًا. فأما استثناء عين أيهما كان فذلك لا ينتج ; فلو قلت لكنه ليس بلاحجر فلزم أن يكون جمادًا، ولو قلت لكنه ليس بجماد يلزم أن لا يكون حجرًا. فأما إذا قلت لكنه ليس بحجر فلا يلزم أن يكون جمادًا أو لا جمادًا، وكذلك إذا قلت لكنه جماد فإنه يلزم أن يكون حجرا أو لا حجرًا لصحة اجتماعهما معا؛ فهذا القسم يشبه المتصلات الحقيقية من حيث استثناء النقيض، وذلك يشبهها من حيث استثناء العين لما أن رفع أحد جزأي الشرطية أو وضعه ينتج رفع الآخر أو وضعه. وقد يورد فيهما من الشبه ما يورد في الحقيقية كلا أو بعضًا يعرف بالتأمل.
These are the exceptional syllogisms that include the true disjunctive. As for when they do not include it but include a non-true one, like the exclusive [man'at al-jam'], that is not used in the sciences. It is like our saying: this thing is either a tree or a stone. Then the affirmation of either yields the negation of the other due to the impossibility of their coexistence. As for the affirmation of the negation, that does not yield anything at all because both can be false together. So if you say 'but it is a tree', it necessitates that it is not a stone. Likewise if you say 'but it is a stone', it necessitates that it is not a tree. As for if you say 'but it is not a tree', it does not necessitate that it is a stone or not a stone. Similarly, if you say 'but it is not a stone', it does not necessitate that it is a tree or not a tree. For some things are neither tree nor stone, like man and horse and others. And likewise if the parts are three or four, for example. As for when it includes the exhaustive [man'at al-khuluw], as in our saying: this is either not a stone or an inanimate object. Then the affirmation of the negation of either yields the affirmation of the other due to the impossibility of both being false together. As for the affirmation of either, that does not yield. So if you say 'but it is not not-a-stone', it necessitates that it is an inanimate object. And if you say 'but it is not an inanimate object', it necessitates that it is not a stone. As for if you say 'but it is not a stone', it does not necessitate that it is an inanimate object or not an inanimate object. Likewise, if you say 'but it is an inanimate object', it necessitates that it is a stone or not a stone, due to the possibility of both being true together. Thus this type resembles true conditionals in terms of affirming the negation, and that type resembles them in terms of affirming the positive, because affirming one part of the conditional or negating it yields the negation or affirmation of the other. And there may be introduced in both of them ambiguities as are introduced in the true, in whole or in part, known by reflection.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on disjunctive syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i munfasil). The author begins by distinguishing the 'true disjunctive' (al-munfasila al-haqiqiyya) from non-true ones. The true disjunctive is the one that is both exclusive and exhaustive (mani'at al-jam' wa al-khuluw), but here he discusses the two types separately: exclusive (man'at al-jam') and exhaustive (man'at al-khuluw).

First, he explains the exclusive type (man'at al-jam'), where the two disjuncts cannot both be true but can both be false. An example: 'This thing is either a tree or a stone.' Since it cannot be both, affirming one yields the negation of the other. However, negating one does not yield the affirmation of the other because both can be false (e.g., it could be a man). He then notes that this pattern extends to three or more parts.

Second, he explains the exhaustive type (man'at al-khuluw), where the two disjuncts cannot both be false but can both be true. An example: 'This is either not a stone or an inanimate object.' The two cannot be false together (everything is either not a stone or inanimate). Here, negating one yields the affirmation of the other. But affirming one does not yield a certain result because both can be true simultaneously.

The author then compares these two types to conditional syllogisms, noting that man'at al-khuluw resembles conditionals when negating one part leads to affirming the other, while man'at al-jam' resembles them when affirming one part leads to negating the other. Finally, he warns that ambiguities can arise in both, requiring careful reflection.

The examples used are from common natural objects (tree, stone, man, horse) which are neutral but acceptable in the Islamic context as they do not involve prohibited analogies.

Key Terms
منفصلة حقيقية
مانعة الجمع
مانعة الخلو
استثناء العين
استثناء النقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a man'at al-jam' disjunction, if the disjunction is 'This action is either wajib or haram' (assuming it cannot be both), which inference is valid?
A) If it is wajib, then it is not haram.
B) If it is not wajib, then it is haram.
C) If it is not haram, then it is wajib.
D) Both B and C are valid.

Key Takeaway
The two non-true disjunctives, man'at al-jam' and man'at al-khuluw, have opposite valid inference patterns: affirming one disjunct works for exclusive, negating one disjunct works for exhaustive.

Disjunctives Depend on Conditionals
Author argues that disjunctives rely on conditionals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why every disjunctive syllogism depends on an underlying conditional and identify the role of luzum (implication) in demonstrative reasoning.

Core Idea
Disjunctive syllogisms hide a conditional; true demonstration rests on necessary implication.

The author claims that whatever disjunctives occur in logic are only completed by conditionals. When you reason with a disjunctive proposition, you are implicitly assuming a conditional link. This reveals that the foundation of demonstration is not contradiction but necessary implication (luzum). Understanding this helps you evaluate arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's claim about disjunctives
The author states that disjunctives are only completed by conditionals. Read the passage and locate where he says 'disjunctives are only completed by conditionals'.
This is the thesis of the passage.
2
Trace the reduction to conditionals
When you say 'either A or B' and then deny one, you are really thinking 'if not A then B' (or a similar conditional). The disjunctive syllogism only works because of that unstated conditional.
The author says you have 'implied a conditional in your mind'.
3
Distinguish implication (luzum) from contradiction ('inan)
The author says 'the first real demonstration is by way of implication (luzum)', not by contradiction. Contradiction only works indirectly because it depends on implication (e.g., the denial of one implies the other).
luzum is the core; 'inan is secondary.
4
Apply to accidental conditionals
Even in an accidental conditional like 'Either Zayd is writing or 'Amr did not give permission', the disjunctive argument still relies on an underlying conditional. The reduction holds regardless of whether the connection is necessary or accidental.
The example shows that the form is what matters.

In summary, whatever disjunctives occur in this chapter are only completed by conditionals. For if you say 'it is not free from this and this, and if it is not this then it is that, or if it is that then it is not this', you have inevitably implied a conditional in your mind and an exception from it, even if not stated explicitly. How could it be otherwise? The required [conclusion] must be a necessary implication from the statement, and the contradictory is not actually necessary for what it contradicts; rather, either its negation follows from it, or it follows from the negation of what it contradicts. Therefore the first real demonstration is the one that is in itself by way of implication. As for the way of contradiction, that is by mediation of what implies it from the implication. Then the conditional may be accidental in exceptional syllogisms, as for example it is said: either Zayd is writing or 'Amr did not give him permission to write. For affirming either in this example yields the negation of the other due to the absence
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage is a meta-logical reflection on the relationship between disjunctive and conditional syllogisms. The author argues that all disjunctive arguments rely on an underlying conditional (mutasil) implication. Whenever one says 'either A or B' and then uses a disjunctive syllogism, the reasoning is only valid because one tacitly assumes a conditional: 'if not A then B' (or vice versa). The true demonstration, he asserts, is based on implication (luzum) rather than on contradiction ('inan). Contradiction only works because it relies on the implication that the denial of one implies the other. He gives an example of an accidental conditional: 'Either Zayd is writing or 'Amr did not give him permission' — here, the disjunction is not genuine, but the logical form still produces a valid inference if one part is affirmed. This reinforces the point that the conditional is the fundamental logical structure.

Disjunctives and Accidental Conditionals
Author explains that disjunctives rely on conjunctives and introduces accidental conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify accidental conditionals and determine valid inferences in disjunctive syllogisms.

Core Idea
Every disjunctive rests on a conjunctive; accidental ones need caution.

In this passage, the author reveals a foundational insight: all disjunctive conditionals used in reasoning implicitly depend on conjunctive conditionals. He then introduces the concept of accidental conditionals, where the connection between parts is coincidental rather than essential. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating the validity of arguments in Islamic logic, especially when dealing with real-world examples where the relationship may be temporal or contingent.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the reliance on conjunctives
The author states that every disjunctive syllogism implicitly involves a conjunctive conditional. For example, the statement 'Either this or that, and not this, so it is that' contains the hidden conjunctive 'If not this, then that'.
This is a key meta-logical insight: disjunctives are not primitive but reducible.
2
Distinguish essential from accidental conditionals
Essential conditionals have a necessary connection (e.g., 'If the sun has risen, it is daytime'). Accidental conditionals have a coincidental connection (e.g., 'Either Zayd is writing or 'Amr did not give permission').
The author emphasizes that accidental conditionals require special handling in inference.
3
Analyze an accidental disjunctive example
Take 'Either Zayd is writing or 'Amr did not give permission'. The author says: excluding the affirmative of one part yields the contradictory of the other. For instance, if Zayd is not writing, then 'Amr must have given permission (contradictory of 'did not give'). However, excluding the negative of a part yields nothing because both can be false.
This is because the disjunction is exclusive (mani'at al-jam) but both can be false.
4
Apply the conditions for productive disjunctives
The author states that for a disjunctive to be productive in an exceptive syllogism, it must be affirmative and universal. Negative and particular disjunctives are not productive because the time of exclusion may differ from the time of opposition.
This is a critical condition for valid inference in Islamic logic.
5
Consider temporal specification
If the time is specified (e.g., 'at this moment'), even a particular disjunctive can be productive. This is because the ambiguity of time is removed.
The author leaves this as a nuanced exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة فما يكون من المنفصلات في هذا الباب فذلك إنما يتم بالمتّصلات; فإنك إذا قلتَ لا يخلو عن هذا وهذا، وهذا ليس ذا فهو ذاك أو هو ذاك فليس ذا، فقد أضمرت في نفسك اتصالا لا محالة واستثناء منه وإن لم يصرّح به. وكيف والمطلوب يجب أن يكون لازما من القول والمعاند ليس بالفعل لازما لما يعانده بل إما أن يلزمه نقيضه وإما أن يلزم لنقيض معانده; فإذن البيان الحقيقي الأول هو الذي لنفسه من طريق اللزوم. وأما من طريق العناد فذلك بتوسط ما يلزمه من اللزوم. ثم الشرطية قد تكون اتفاقية في الأقيسة الاستثنائية كما يقال مثلا إما أن يكون زيد يكتب أو لم يأذن له عمرو في الكتابة; فإن استثناء عين أيهما كان في هذا المثال ينتج نقيض الآخر لعدم الاجتماع بينهما اتفاقا. فأما استثناء نقيض أيهما كان فلا ينتج لصحة ارتفاعهما [معا]. / وعلى هذا في الغير من الصور. ثمَّ ما يكون من الشرائط فذلك أن تكون المنفصلة موجبة كلية لما أن السالبة منها لا تفيد لما مر . والجزئية كذلك لاحتمال أن يكون زمان الاستثناء غير زمان العناد. اللهم إلا أن يكون الزمان معينا كما في المخصوصة ; وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون منتجا لعدم احتمال أن يكون هذا في زمان، وذلك في زمان والله أعلم.
In general, whatever disjunctives occur in this chapter are completed by conjunctives; for if you say 'It is not free from this and that, and this is not that, so it is that, or it is that so it is not that', you have implied in yourself a connection without a doubt and an exclusion from it even if not stated explicitly. How so? The required must be necessary from the statement, and the opponent is not actually necessary for what opposes it, rather either its contradictory is necessary for it or its contradictory is necessary for the contradictory of what opposes it. So the true primary demonstration is that which is in itself by way of necessity. As for the way of opposition, that is mediated by what is necessary for it. Then a conditional may be accidental in exceptive syllogisms, as for example it is said 'Either Zayd is writing or 'Amr did not give him permission to write'; for the exclusion of the affirmative of either one in this example yields the contradictory of the other because they cannot be together coincidentally. As for the exclusion of the negative of either one, it yields nothing because both can be false together. And so on in other forms. Then, of the conditions is that the disjunctive must be affirmative universal because the negative does not benefit due to what has passed. And the particular likewise because it is possible that the time of the exclusion is other than the time of opposition. Unless the time is specified as in the determinate; then it would be productive because there is no possibility that this is in one time and that in another. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage explains several important points about disjunctive conditionals in the context of exceptive syllogisms. First, the author states that all disjunctives in this chapter (i.e., those used in logical reasoning) are ultimately completed by conjunctives (muttasilat). Even if you do not explicitly state the conjunctive conditional, it is implicitly assumed in your mind. For example, when you say 'Either this or that, and not this, so it is that', you are effectively thinking 'If not this, then that' (a conjunctive). The author then discusses the nature of logical necessity: the sought conclusion must follow necessarily from the premises, and when dealing with opposition (al-ʿinād), the inference may go through the contradictory of the opposite. The true primary demonstration is based on necessary implication, not opposition. Next, the author introduces accidental (ittifāqiyya) conditionals, where the conjunction or disjunction is not based on essential connection but on coincidence. He gives an example: 'Either Zayd is writing or 'Amr did not give him permission to write'. In such cases, the exclusion of the affirmative of one part yields the contradictory of the other, but exclusion of the negative does not yield anything because both can be false coincidentally. Finally, the author notes conditions for the disjunctive to be productive: it must be affirmative and universal in scope. A particular disjunctive is not productive because the time of the exclusion might differ from the time of the opposition, unless the time is specified (as in determinate propositions).

Key Takeaway
Disjunctives implicitly rely on conjunctives, and accidental conditionals require careful handling because not all inferences are valid.

Exceptive Syllogisms Rely on Categorical Foundations
Author argues exceptive syllogisms depend on categoricals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why exceptive syllogisms require categorical syllogisms for completion and identify the role of qiyas al-khalf.

Core Idea
Every exceptive syllogism is completed by a categorical syllogism.

This passage establishes a key insight: exceptive syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i), whether conditional or disjunctive, are not self-sufficient. They depend on categorical syllogisms (qiyas iqtirani) to be fully valid. The author cites Ibn Sina and uses reductio ad absurdum as an example. Understanding this dependence is crucial for constructing sound arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
Find the author's main thesis: that the exceptive syllogism is only completed by the categorical syllogism. He quotes Ibn Sina to support this.
2
Understand the example of qiyas al-khalf
The author mentions reductio ad absurdum (qiyas al-khalf) as a type of exceptive syllogism that depends on categorical syllogisms. Recognize that even proofs by contradiction require categorical reasoning.
Qiyas al-khalf assumes the opposite of the conclusion and derives a contradiction, which then requires categorical logic to expose the contradiction.
3
Connect disjunctive to conjunctive
The author asserts that disjunctive exceptive syllogisms implicitly involve conjunctive conditionals. Trace this dependency: a disjunction (either A or B) is understood through the conditional 'if not A then B'.
4
Trace the dependency chain
Note that the conjunctive form where the negation of the consequent is excepted (modus tollens) is clarified by the form where the affirmation of the antecedent is excepted (modus ponens), and that latter form itself requires a categorical syllogism.
This shows a hierarchy of dependence, all rooted in categorical syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : أما القياس الاستثنائي فإنه إنما يتم بالاقتراني كما قال الشيخ في الشفاء: إن كل قول قياسي منتج منه حملي فإنه يتم بأحد الأشكال التي هي الحمليّات، فقياس الخلف مثلا إنما يتم بالقياسات الاقترانية والشرطية الاستثنائية (). والقياس الشرطي قد وضح من أجزائه أنه لا يفيد كما ينبغي إلا بالاقترانيات، وإذ الكلام في أنالوطيقا القديمة [First Analitics] إنما هو في القياس المنتج الحملي فيكون المراد بالاقتراني فيه والحملي واحدا(۲). وقد اتضح لك أن الاستثنائي المنفصل إنما يستثنى فيه لاستشعار المتّصل، وأن المتصل الذي يستثنى فيه نقيض التالي يتبين بالذي المستثنى فيه عين المقدم. فإذا وضح أن المستثنى فيه عين المقدم لا يفيد إلا بقياس اقتراني بأن ذلك في سائر القياسات الشرطية والحملية. وأحسن ما يذكر في إبانة ذلك هو أنه لو كان المقدم بينا لكان لازمه بينا; وكان إبانته بالقياس محالا; إذ القياس يبين الخفي ولولا أن المقدم غير بين لما ألحق به لفظ الشرط كما في قولنا إن كان كذا فكذا كذا فإنه مما يقتضي وقوع الشكل في المقدم فلو كان المقدم بينا بنفسه كان ذلك الإلحاق ضايعا فإنه لا يكون
Section: As for the exceptional syllogism, it is only completed by the categorical, as the Shaykh said in al-Shifa': 'Every syllogistic statement that produces a categorical conclusion is completed by one of the figures which are categorical.' For example, the reductio ad absurdum syllogism is only completed by categorical syllogisms and hypothetical-exceptive ones. And the hypothetical syllogism has been clarified from its parts that it does not benefit as it should except by categoricals. And since the discussion in the First Analytics is only about the syllogism that produces a categorical conclusion, therefore what is meant by 'categorical' there and 'categorical' is one. And it has become clear to you that the disjunctive exceptive syllogism is only exceptive due to the awareness of the conjunctive, and that the conjunctive in which the negative of the consequent is excepted is clarified by that in which the affirmative of the antecedent is excepted. So when it is clear that that in which the affirmative of the antecedent is excepted does not benefit except through a categorical syllogism, that applies to all other hypothetical and categorical syllogisms. And the best thing to mention in clarifying that is that if the antecedent were self-evident, its consequent would be self-evident; and its clarification by syllogism would be impossible, because the syllogism clarifies the hidden, and were the antecedent not hidden, the word 'if' would not be attached to it, as in our saying 'If it is such, then it is such', which requires that there be a figure in the antecedent. So if the antecedent were self-evident in itself, that attachment would be wasted, because it would not be...
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage is a section heading and discussion on the relationship between exceptive syllogisms (qiyās istithnāʾī) and categorical syllogisms (qiyās iqtirānī). The author begins by asserting that the exceptive syllogism is only completed by the categorical. He cites Ibn Sina (al-Shaykh) from al-Shifa', stating that every syllogism producing a categorical conclusion is completed by one of the categorical figures. This includes reductio ad absurdum (qiyās al-khalf). The author notes that the hypothetical syllogism (sharṭī) is also only fully beneficial when supplemented by categoricals. He then references Aristotle's Prior Analytics (Anālūṭīqā al-Qadīma), where the discussion is about syllogisms producing categorical conclusions, so 'categorical' (iqtirānī) and 'categorical' (ḥamlī) mean the same. The author then explains that the disjunctive exceptive syllogism works only because it implicitly involves a conjunctive conditional. Furthermore, the conjunctive where the negation of the consequent is excepted depends on the one where the affirmation of the antecedent is excepted. And that latter type (affirmation of antecedent) only benefits through a categorical syllogism, and this principle extends to all other types. The best proof, he says, is that if the antecedent were self-evident (bayyin), its consequent would also be self-evident, so no syllogism would be needed. But since we use the word 'if', it indicates the antecedent is not self-evident but requires a figure (syllogistic form) to be clarified. Thus, the syllogism reveals the hidden. This demonstrates the foundational role of categorical syllogism in all logical deduction.

القياس الاقتراني
قياس الخلف

Key Takeaway
Exceptional syllogisms (istithna'i) are not autonomous; they require categorical syllogisms (iqtirani) to be valid and complete.

The Self-Evidence Argument for Categorical Syllogisms
Author uses self-evidence to argue for categorical dependence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's argument that the use of 'if' in conditionals indicates a need for categorical syllogisms to clarify the antecedent.

Core Idea
The conditional 'if' proves the antecedent is not self-evident, necessitating a categorical syllogism.

In the second part of the passage, the author presents a powerful argument to ground the dependence of exceptive syllogisms on categorical ones. He argues that if the antecedent of a conditional were self-evident, its consequent would also be self-evident, making a syllogism unnecessary. The very use of 'if' shows the antecedent is hidden and requires a categorical syllogism to be clarified. This philosophical insight reinforces the priority of categorical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional argument
The author presents a conditional: 'If the antecedent were self-evident, its consequent would be self-evident.' Recognize this as a hypothetical statement.
2
Note the implied premise
The consequent is not self-evident because we need a syllogism to clarify it. Therefore, by modus tollens, the antecedent is not self-evident.
This is a valid conditional syllogism (modus tollens).
3
Connect to the use of 'if'
The author argues that the word 'if' (law or in) is only used when the antecedent is not obvious. Thus, the conditional sentence signals that the antecedent requires a figure (syllogism) to be proven.
4
Conclude the role of categorical syllogism
Since the antecedent is hidden, it must be clarified by a categorical syllogism. This shows that even the conditional syllogism itself depends on a categorical foundation.
The categorical syllogism is the tool that makes the hidden antecedent evident.

فصل : أما القياس الاستثنائي فإنه إنما يتم بالاقتراني كما قال الشيخ في الشفاء: إن كل قول قياسي منتج منه حملي فإنه يتم بأحد الأشكال التي هي الحمليّات، فقياس الخلف مثلا إنما يتم بالقياسات الاقترانية والشرطية الاستثنائية (). والقياس الشرطي قد وضح من أجزائه أنه لا يفيد كما ينبغي إلا بالاقترانيات، وإذ الكلام في أنالوطيقا القديمة [First Analitics] إنما هو في القياس المنتج الحملي فيكون المراد بالاقتراني فيه والحملي واحدا(۲). وقد اتضح لك أن الاستثنائي المنفصل إنما يستثنى فيه لاستشعار المتّصل، وأن المتصل الذي يستثنى فيه نقيض التالي يتبين بالذي المستثنى فيه عين المقدم. فإذا وضح أن المستثنى فيه عين المقدم لا يفيد إلا بقياس اقتراني بأن ذلك في سائر القياسات الشرطية والحملية. وأحسن ما يذكر في إبانة ذلك هو أنه لو كان المقدم بينا لكان لازمه بينا; وكان إبانته بالقياس محالا; إذ القياس يبين الخفي ولولا أن المقدم غير بين لما ألحق به لفظ الشرط كما في قولنا إن كان كذا فكذا كذا فإنه مما يقتضي وقوع الشكل في المقدم فلو كان المقدم بينا بنفسه كان ذلك الإلحاق ضايعا فإنه لا يكون
Section: As for the exceptional syllogism, it is only completed by the categorical, as the Shaykh said in al-Shifa': 'Every syllogistic statement that produces a categorical conclusion is completed by one of the figures which are categorical.' For example, the reductio ad absurdum syllogism is only completed by categorical syllogisms and hypothetical-exceptive ones. And the hypothetical syllogism has been clarified from its parts that it does not benefit as it should except by categoricals. And since the discussion in the First Analytics is only about the syllogism that produces a categorical conclusion, therefore what is meant by 'categorical' there and 'categorical' is one. And it has become clear to you that the disjunctive exceptive syllogism is only exceptive due to the awareness of the conjunctive, and that the conjunctive in which the negative of the consequent is excepted is clarified by that in which the affirmative of the antecedent is excepted. So when it is clear that that in which the affirmative of the antecedent is excepted does not benefit except through a categorical syllogism, that applies to all other hypothetical and categorical syllogisms. And the best thing to mention in clarifying that is that if the antecedent were self-evident, its consequent would be self-evident; and its clarification by syllogism would be impossible, because the syllogism clarifies the hidden, and were the antecedent not hidden, the word 'if' would not be attached to it, as in our saying 'If it is such, then it is such', which requires that there be a figure in the antecedent. So if the antecedent were self-evident in itself, that attachment would be wasted, because it would not be...
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage is a section heading and discussion on the relationship between exceptive syllogisms (qiyās istithnāʾī) and categorical syllogisms (qiyās iqtirānī). The author begins by asserting that the exceptive syllogism is only completed by the categorical. He cites Ibn Sina (al-Shaykh) from al-Shifa', stating that every syllogism producing a categorical conclusion is completed by one of the categorical figures. This includes reductio ad absurdum (qiyās al-khalf). The author notes that the hypothetical syllogism (sharṭī) is also only fully beneficial when supplemented by categoricals. He then references Aristotle's Prior Analytics (Anālūṭīqā al-Qadīma), where the discussion is about syllogisms producing categorical conclusions, so 'categorical' (iqtirānī) and 'categorical' (ḥamlī) mean the same. The author then explains that the disjunctive exceptive syllogism works only because it implicitly involves a conjunctive conditional. Furthermore, the conjunctive where the negation of the consequent is excepted depends on the one where the affirmation of the antecedent is excepted. And that latter type (affirmation of antecedent) only benefits through a categorical syllogism, and this principle extends to all other types. The best proof, he says, is that if the antecedent were self-evident (bayyin), its consequent would also be self-evident, so no syllogism would be needed. But since we use the word 'if', it indicates the antecedent is not self-evident but requires a figure (syllogistic form) to be clarified. Thus, the syllogism reveals the hidden. This demonstrates the foundational role of categorical syllogism in all logical deduction.

Core Conditions for Valid Disjunctive Syllogism
Author explains conditions for productive disjunctive syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two necessary conditions (universal affirmative and exclusivity) for a valid disjunctive syllogism and explain why a particular or negative disjunction fails.

Core Idea
A disjunctive syllogism yields a conclusion only if the disjunction is exclusive and universal.

Not every 'either-or' argument is valid in Islamic logic. The author lays out strict conditions: the disjunction must be universal affirmative and the disjuncts must be mutually exclusive in the same timeframe. Without these, the syllogism collapses. This lesson unpacks these conditions using fiqh examples.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the disjunction type
Identify whether the disjunction is inclusive (allows both true or both false) or exclusive (prevents both true and both false). The author states that excepting the contradictory of either disjunct yields no conclusion when both can be false (inclusive).
Inclusive disjunction: 'Either X or Y (or both)' is not sufficient.
2
Check universality and affirmation
The disjunction must be universal affirmative. A negative disjunction ('Not: either X or Y') is useless. A particular disjunction ('Some are either X or Y') fails because the time of exception may not match the time of exclusivity.
Universal: applies to all instances. Affirmative: asserts the disjunction exists.
3
Test with an Islamic legal example
Apply the conditions: 'Every contract is either valid (sahih) or void (batil). This contract is not valid, therefore it is void.' This works because the disjunction is universal, affirmative, and exclusive. If we say 'Some contracts are either valid or void', the conclusion may fail because the time of judgment might differ.
In fiqh, contracts are judged at the moment of contract formation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاجتماع بينهما اتفاقا. فأما استثناء نقيض أيهما كان فلا ينتج لصحة ارتفاعهما معا. وعلى هذا في الغير من الصور. ثم ما يكون من الشرائط فذلك أن تكون المنفصلة موجبة كلية لما أن السالبة منها لا تفيد لما مر. والجزئية كذلك لاحتمال أن يكون زمان الاستثناء غير زمان العناد. اللهم إلا أن يكون الزمان معينا كما في المخصوصة؛ وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون منتجا لعدم احتمال أن يكون هذا في زمان، وذلك في زمان والله أعلم.
The conjunction between them [the two disjuncts] is agreed upon. As for excepting the contradictory of whichever of them, it does not yield a conclusion because their mutual negation is valid. And likewise for other forms. Then among the conditions is that the disjunction be universal affirmative, because the negative does not benefit as previously mentioned. And the particular is likewise due to the possibility that the time of the exception is not the time of mutual exclusivity. Unless the time is specified, as in the particularized case; then it necessarily yields a conclusion because there is no possibility that this is in one time and that in another. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage discusses the conditions under which a disjunctive (munfasil) syllogism is productive. The author begins by stating that if one excepts the contradictory of either disjunct (i.e., asserts one disjunct's truth), no conclusion follows because both can be false simultaneously—this occurs when the disjunction is inclusive (allowing both falsehood). The same applies to other forms. Then the condition is given: the disjunctive proposition must be universal affirmative; a negative disjunction is useless because it does not assert a relation that can be exploited. A particular disjunction is also problematic because the time of the exception might not coincide with the time of mutual exclusivity. However, if the time is specified (e.g., 'at this moment'), then the syllogism becomes productive because there is no ambiguity about the time frame. This aligns with the idea that a valid disjunctive syllogism requires exclusivity (mani'at al-jam' wa al-khuluw) and universality, with no temporal variance. For example, 'Either this act is wajib or it is haram at this very moment; it is not wajib, therefore it is haram' would be valid.

Key Terms
المنفصلة
زمان العناد

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the disjunction may be negative.
B) Because the time of exception might not match the time of mutual exclusivity.
C) Because the disjunction is universal affirmative.
D) Because the contradictory exception is always invalid.

Key Takeaway
A disjunctive syllogism is valid only if the disjunction is universal affirmative and the disjuncts are mutually exclusive in the same timeframe.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Temporal Exception: When a Particular Disjunction Works
Author explains exception when time is specified for particular disjunction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition under which a particular disjunctive syllogism becomes productive (specified time) and construct a valid example from Islamic law.

Core Idea
Specifying the time removes temporal ambiguity, making a particular disjunction productive.

The author notes a crucial exception: if the time is explicitly specified (al-makhsusa), a particular disjunction yields a valid conclusion. This is because the temporal ambiguity is eliminated. In Islamic legal reasoning, this often arises when rulings depend on specific moments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of disjunction
Recognize that the disjunction is particular (juz'iyya) and not universal. For example, 'Some actions are either halal or haram' is particular.
Particular means the disjunction applies to some individuals, not all.
2
Check if time is specified
The author says 'unless the time is specified, as in al-makhsusa'. This means the disjunction is qualified by a definite time, e.g., 'at this moment' or 'during the morning prayer'.
The specification makes the time of exception coincide with the time of exclusivity.
3
Validate with an Islamic example
Example: 'This act at the time of Fajr prayer is either halal or haram. It is not halal at that time, therefore it is haram.' This is valid because the time bounds the disjunction, ensuring exclusivity at that moment.
In fiqh, rulings often change with time (e.g., eating before Fajr is halal, after Fajr is haram during fasting).

Critique of Ibn Sina on Conditional Syllogisms
Author critiques Ibn Sina on conditional syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate Ibn Sina's argument that conditional syllogisms depend on categorical syllogisms, and articulate the author's critique regarding the evidentness of antecedents and implications.

Core Idea
Conditional syllogisms need not have non-evident antecedents; implication can be clarified independently.

In this passage, the author presents Ibn Sina's view from al-Shifa that conditional syllogisms are completed only through categorical syllogisms, because the antecedent must be non-evident for the syllogism to be useful. The author then offers a trenchant critique, distinguishing between being 'evident in itself' and 'evident by implication.' Understanding this critique sharpens our grasp of how conditional reasoning works and how it relates to categorical forms.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ibn Sina's view
Locate the claim that conditional syllogisms (al-qiyās al-istithnā'ī) are only completed by categorical syllogisms (al-iqtirānī). What does Ibn Sina say about the role of the antecedent?
The passage starts with 'القياس الاستثنائي فإنه إنما يتم بالاقتراني'.
2
Locate his supporting reasoning
Find the argument that if the antecedent were evident, its consequent would also be evident, making the syllogism pointless. Why does Ibn Sina think the 'if' clause implies hiddenness?
The phrase 'لو كان المقدم بينا لكان لازمه بينا' captures this.
3
Extract the author's counterexample
How does the author use the first mood of the first figure (Barbara) to challenge Ibn Sina? Show that its premises are evident in themselves yet the syllogism is still productive.
The author says: 'الضرب الأول من الشكل الأول' and explains that the conclusion is evident by implication.
4
Understand the key distinction
What is the difference between 'evident in itself' (bayyin bi-nafsihi) and 'evident by implication' (bayyin bi-l-luzūm)? How does this distinction save the syllogism?
The author stresses: 'فرق بين قولنا إنه بين بنفسه وبين قولنا إنه بين لزومه عن بين بنفسه'.

فصل: أما القياس الاستثنائي فإنه إنما يتم بالاقتراني كما قال الشيخ في الشفاء: إن كل قول قياسي منتج منه حملي فإنه يتم بأحد الأشكال التي هي الحمليات، فقياس الخلف مثلا إنما يتم بالقياسات الاقترانية والشرطية الاستثنائية. والقياس الشرطي قد وضح من أجزائه أنه لا يفيد كما ينبغي إلا بالاقترانيات، وإذ الكلام في أنالوطيقا القديمة إنما هو في القياس المنتج الحملي فيكون المراد بالاقتراني فيه والحملي واحدا. وقد اتضح لك أن الاستثنائي المنفصل إنما يستثنى فيه لاستشعار المتصل، وأن المتصل الذي يستثنى فيه نقيض التالي يتبين بالذي المستثنى فيه عين المقدم. فإذا وضح أن المستثنى فيه عين المقدم لا يفيد إلا بقياس الشيء البين بينا فيعلم بتوسطه ذلك اللازم. وفرق بين قولنا إنه بين بنفسه وبين قولنا إنه بين لزومه عن بين بنفسه؛ فاللازم لا بد وأن يكون بين اللازم أو ينتهي آخر الأمر إلى لازم بين اللزوم وإلا لكانت الأشياء كلها بينة بأنفسها. فيلزم أن لا يكون الضرب الأول من الشكل الأول منتجا؛ وذلك لأن قياساته تنحل إلى مقدمتين بينتين بأنفسهما ثم النتيجة بين اللزوم منهما. فنسبة القياس إلى النتيجة نسبة التالي إلى المقدم؛ فيكون المقدم أمرا بينا كما في قولنا إن كل ج ب وكل ب أ؛ أو التالي بين اللزوم عنه فيجب قولنا كل ج أ بينا. وكذلك جميع النتائج الثواني إلى غير النهاية. وأيضا ليس يجب أن يكون التالي بين اللزوم فإنه ربما كان غير بين اللزوم؛ فيتبين لزومه. فإذا صار بين اللزوم بحجة ثم استثنى المقدم بعينه للتالي بعينه فصار قياسا مفيدا فيمكن إذن يكون المقدم بينا بنفسه؛ واللزوم لا يكون بينا؛ فيتبين. وما يجب ذكره في هذا الموضع هو أن كل ما يتعلق من الأمور تعلقا بينا بأمر واحد بين كان خطور المتعلق به بالبال معينا في وقوف الذهن على صحة التالي اللازم؛ فيكون إذا كان أب بينا وكان لزوم ج د له بينا فمتى خطر أب بالبال لم يحتج إلى أن يستعمل قياس بالفعل في إلزام التالي البتة. فأما إذا لم يكن بينا فحينئذ يحتاج إلى أن يجرد النظر في أجزائه كما ينبغي. وكذلك حال القياس الاقتراني إذا صار مقدمًا فإنه ينعکس بیان مقدماته عن استئناف الاستثناء؛ فالقياس مقدم شرطي والتالي هو لزوم النتيجة فبحسب ذلك ليس تحتاج النفس إذا كان اللزوم بينا إلى أن يرجع.
Chapter: As for the conditional syllogism, it is only completed by the categorical, as the Shaykh said in al-Shifa: Every syllogistic statement producing a categorical conclusion is completed by one of the figures which are the categoricals. The reductio ad absurdum, for example, is completed by categorical and conditional syllogisms. The conditional syllogism has been clarified from its parts that it does not benefit as it should except by categoricals. And since the discourse in the Prior Analytics is only about the syllogism producing a categorical conclusion, then what is meant by 'categorical' and 'categorical syllogism' there is one. It has become clear to you that the disjunctive conditional is only excepted due to a hint of the conjunctive, and that the conjunctive in which the contradictory of the consequent is excepted is clarified by that in which the same antecedent is excepted. So if it is clear that that in which the same antecedent is excepted does not benefit except by a categorical syllogism, that applies to all conditional and categorical syllogisms. The best thing mentioned to explain this is that if the antecedent were evident, its consequent would be evident; and its clarification by syllogism would be impossible, because the syllogism clarifies the hidden. If the antecedent were not hidden, the term 'if' would not be attached to it, as in our statement: 'If it is such, then such and such', which necessitates the occurrence of a form in the antecedent. If the antecedent were evident in itself, that attachment would be wasted, because it would not be placed in its proper place. In summary, this explanation is among those that require consideration, because the antecedent does not necessarily have to be doubted or not doubted in all forms. Nor does a thing necessarily have to be evident in itself if it is a necessary consequent of what is evident in itself; for a thing may be evident in itself and have a necessary consequent that is not evident in itself, but its implication for that evident thing is evident, so that consequent is known through that evident thing. There is a difference between saying 'it is evident in itself' and saying 'its implication from what is evident in itself is evident.' The consequent must either be evident in itself or ultimately lead to a consequent whose implication is evident; otherwise all things would be evident in themselves. Consequently, the first mood of the first figure would not be productive; because its syllogisms are resolved into two premises evident in themselves, then the conclusion is evident in its implication from them. Thus the relation of the syllogism to the conclusion is the relation of the consequent to the antecedent; so the antecedent is something evident, as in our statement: 'Every J is B and every B is A'; or the consequent is evident in its implication from it, so we must say 'Every J is A' is evident. And likewise all secondary conclusions ad infinitum. Moreover, it is not necessary that the consequent be evident in implication; for it may not be evident in implication, so its implication is clarified. When it becomes evident in implication by an argument, then the same antecedent is excepted for the same consequent, so it becomes a useful syllogism. Thus it is possible that the antecedent is evident in itself and the implication is not evident, so it is clarified. What must be mentioned here is that everything that is evidently related to one evident thing, its occurrence in the mind is a help in the mind's stopping at the correctness of the necessary consequent. So if 'AB' is evident and the implication of 'CD' to it is evident, then whenever 'AB' occurs to the mind, it does not need to actually use a syllogism to force the consequent at all. But if it is not evident, then it needs to analyze its parts as appropriate. Similarly, the case of the categorical syllogism when it becomes a premise: its clarification of its premises returns from the renewal of the exception. So the syllogism is a conditional premise, and the consequent is the implication of the conclusion; accordingly, the soul does not need to return if the implication is evident.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage begins a new section ('Fasl') dedicated to the relationship between conditional (istithnā'ī) and categorical (iqtirānī) syllogisms. The author first reports the view of Ibn Sina (the Shaykh) from al-Shifa: every syllogism producing a categorical conclusion—whether directly or via conditional forms—depends on one of the categorical figures. Even the reductio argument and conditional syllogisms rely on categorical syllogisms. The chapter then presents Ibn Sina's supporting argument: If the antecedent were evident in itself, its consequent would also be evident, making a syllogism pointless; thus the antecedent must be non-evident for the conditional to be useful. The author immediately critiques this reasoning. He points out that the antecedent need not be doubted in all cases; a thing can be evident in itself while its consequent is not. Moreover, the first mood of the first figure—universal affirmative—would be problematic if Ibn Sina's reasoning were followed, because its premises are evident in themselves but the conclusion follows by evident implication, not being directly evident. The author distinguishes between being 'evident in itself' and being 'evident in its implication from an evident premise.' He argues that even if the antecedent is evident, its implication might not be, and a syllogism can then clarify that implication. For example, if it is evident that 'Every A is B' and 'Every B is C', the implication that 'Every A is C' may be clarified through the syllogism. Thus, Ibn Sina's objection fails. The author concludes by noting that if the implication (luzūm) is evident, the mind does not need to reconstruct the syllogism; it directly grasps the conclusion. This detailed critique refines the understanding of how conditional and categorical syllogisms interact, emphasizing the role of evidentness and implication.

Key Terms
القياس الاستثنائي
القياس الاقتراني
المقدم
اللزوم
بين بنفسه

B) Because the conclusion is evident in itself, not by implication.
C) Because the conclusion is evident by implication from the premises, which is distinct from being evident in itself.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between 'evident in itself' and 'evident by implication' is crucial for understanding the role of syllogisms: a syllogism can clarify an implication even when its premises are self-evident.

The Syllogism

The Condition for Beneficial Implicit Premises
Author distinguishes two types of separation in syllogistic statements and elucidates the role of the implicit major premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether leaving a major premise implicit in a categorical syllogism is epistemically beneficial by applying the mental presence condition derived from the author's analysis.

Core Idea
An implicit premise only benefits if the listener already has it in mind.

In this passage, the author refines the standard account of syllogistic reasoning by examining the epistemological condition of implicit premises. While earlier logicians allowed leaving self-evident premises unstated, the author goes deeper: he distinguishes two types of 'separation' (faṣl) in discourse and argues that even a self-evident major premise is only beneficial if it is mentally present at the moment of inference. Without this mental presence, the syllogism fails to convey knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of faṣl
The author says the separation in discourse is of two kinds. Find these in the Arabic passage. One type involves the premise being already fully considered within what was said, so it does not need to be rementioned. The other type involves the premise being implicitly understood, but if expressed it would occur twice. Identify these in the source.
Look for 'أحدهما فصل يكون الاستثناء عنه على سبيل أنه قد فرغ من إخطاره بالبال في ضمن ما قيل' and 'والثاني أن يكون استثناء النفس عن التوقف عليه'.
2
Extract the condition for beneficial implicity
The author states: 'إنما ينفع هذا الإضمار إذا خطر الكبرى بالبال خارجًا عن خطور الصغرى بالبال متصلاً زمانه بزمانه'. Translate and explain: the implicit major premise is only beneficial if it occurs in the mind of the listener, separate from the minor premise's occurrence, and contiguous in time.
This is the key epistemological condition.
3
Test the condition with a fiqh example
Consider the syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden. Nabidh is an intoxicant. Therefore nabidh is forbidden.' If the major premise 'every intoxicant is forbidden' is left implicit because it is well-known, does it benefit? It only benefits if the listener already mentally affirms that premise at the time of hearing the minor. If not, the argument is not persuasive.
4
Distinguish self-evident and non-self-evident major premises
The author notes that if the major premise is self-evident (bayyina bi-nafsihā), it can be left implicit because it occurs in the mind naturally. If not, the listener may demand it. Show this distinction from the passage.
The passage says: 'فإن كان بينا بنفسه استغنى بخطوره بالبال في اتباع النتيجة للمقدمة عن التصريح به وإن لم يكن بينا بنفسه طالب به المخاطب'.

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (tahqīq) of the theory of syllogistic reasoning, specifically addressing the nature of the implicit (muḍmar) premise. The author begins by analyzing the case where both premises are not necessarily self-evident: if the conjunction of the two premises is not fixed in the mind, the conclusion does not follow automatically. The discussion then shifts to the case where the major premise is self-evident (bayyina bi-nafsihā). The author argues that using such a premise in a syllogism is actually a 'separation' (faṣl) in the sense that it need not be explicitly mentioned because it is already present in the mind. He distinguishes two types of faṣl in discourse: one where the premise is already fully considered within the speech, and another where it is implicitly understood but would have occurred twice if expressed. The key point is that the implicit premise is beneficial only if it actually occurs in the mind at the appropriate time. The author then illustrates with the conditional form: in a conditional syllogism like 'if every b is a', the truth of the antecedent brings the consequent to mind only if the implication is known. Throughout, the author emphasizes the epistemological condition that the implicit premise must be mentally present for the inference to be valid. This analysis refines earlier accounts by highlighting the mental aspect and the distinction between explicit and implicit use of premises.

The Syllogism

Conditional Syllogisms Require Doubt in the Antecedent
Author refutes objection that conditional syllogisms are useless when antecedent is known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given conditional premise is suitable for use in a conditional syllogism by checking if its antecedent is doubted.

Core Idea
A conditional syllogism is only informative when the antecedent is uncertain.

An objector claims that conditional syllogisms are useless when the antecedent is already known, giving the example that we already know humans are animals before learning they laugh. The author refutes this by clarifying that a genuine conditional syllogism requires the antecedent to be doubted (mashkuk). If the antecedent is certain, the conditional premise is either disconnected or requires additional categorical proof. This lesson unpacks that condition and shows how to identify when a conditional syllogism is properly applied.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector says: 'Every human is laughing, and every laughing is animal; therefore every human is animal.' But the conclusion (man is animal) is known before the premise that humans laugh. So the syllogism seems useless. Note that this is a categorical syllogism, not a conditional one, but the objector uses it to challenge conditional reasoning.
The author later explains that this example is not a genuine conditional syllogism.
2
Locate the author's key condition
The author states: 'the conditional premise is not subject to exception of its antecedent unless its antecedent is doubted.' Find this exact phrase in the Arabic. This is the central criterion for a valid conditional syllogism.
The Arabic: 'فإذن لا تكون المقدّمة المتّصلة متعرضة للاستثناء من مقدمها ما لم يكن مقدمها مشكوكا'
3
Analyze the author's rationale
The author explains that if the antecedent is not doubted, the conditional is either disconnected (i.e., the implication is not genuine) or its consequent is self-evident, making the conditional unnecessary. If the consequent is not evident, the conditional connection itself must be proven via a categorical syllogism.
Thus, a conditional with a known antecedent fails to provide new information.
4
Apply the condition to a new example
Consider the conditional 'If man is an animal, then he is a body.' The antecedent 'man is animal' is known with certainty. According to the author, this conditional is not useful for istithna because the antecedent is not doubted. Instead, one would simply use a categorical syllogism: 'Every animal is a body; man is an animal; therefore man is a body.'
This shows that conditional syllogisms are reserved for cases where the antecedent is uncertain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قائلا لو قال كل إنسان ضحاك صدق، وإذا قال وكل ضحاك حيوان صدق ولكن هذا غير مفيد فإنه علم أن يكون إنسان حيوان قبل أن علم أنه ضحاك. فيجب أن يفهم قول المعلم الأوّل على هذه الصورة. ولا يظن أنه يرى أن بين اللزوم عن بين الصدق أو أن المقدَّم لا يكون موضوعًا مقدما وهو غير مشكوك فيه حتى إذا لم يكن المقدم مشكوكا فيه لم تكون القضية متّصلة. وإن قول القائل إن كان الإنسان حيوانا فهما جسم إما أن يكون أمرًا مشكوكًا في مقدمه أو قولا غير متصل بل معناه أن استعمال ما لم يكن مشكوكًا في مقدمه بأن يستثنى مقدمه إذا كان تاليه بين اللزوم أو بان لزومه لشيء، أو هو بين بنفسه هو أمر غير قياس أي غير مطابق بدلالته على المحتاج إليه. فإن لم يكن التالي بين اللزوم فهو أبعد؛ فيحتاج لا محالة في إثباته إلى قياس اقتراني ينتهي إليه آخر الأمر ليكون الاتصال بينا. فإذن لا تكون المقدّمة المتّصلة متعرضة للاستثناء من مقدمها ما لم يكن مقدمها مشكوكا
For if a speaker says 'Every human is laughing: true', and if he says 'And every laughing is animal: true', but this is not useful because he knew that man is an animal before he knew that he is laughing. So the statement of the first teacher must be understood in this way. And one should not think that he holds that the implication is from truth to truth, or that the antecedent is not put forward while it is not doubted, so that if the antecedent is not doubted, the proposition is not conditional. And the statement of the speaker 'If man is an animal then he is a body' either is a matter doubted in its antecedent or is a disconnected statement. Rather, its meaning is that using what is not doubted in its antecedent by taking its antecedent as an exception, if its consequent is evident in implication, or its implication to something is clear, or it is self-evident, is a non-syllogistic matter, i.e., not corresponding in its indication to what is needed. If the consequent is not evident in implication, it is more remote; then inevitably in its proof it needs a categorical syllogism that ultimately leads to it so that the connection is evident. So then the conditional premise is not subject to exception of its antecedent unless its antecedent is doubted.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection raised against the use of conditional syllogisms. The objector gives an example: 'Every human is laughing, and every laughing is animal, therefore every human is animal.' But the conclusion (man is animal) is already known before the premise that humans laugh, so the syllogism seems useless. The author responds by insisting that such statements must be understood as the 'first teacher' (Aristotle) intended: the antecedent in a conditional must be doubted (mashkuk). If the antecedent is not doubted, the proposition is not truly conditional. The author then analyzes the conditional 'If man is an animal then he is a body.' This conditional either has a doubted antecedent or is disconnected. If the antecedent is not doubted and the consequent is clear, using it as a conditional is not syllogistically beneficial. If the consequent is not clear, it requires a categorical syllogism to establish the connection. Therefore, a conditional premise is only useful for inference when its antecedent is uncertain; otherwise it is either redundant or requires additional proof.

اللزوم
المقدم

Key Takeaway
A conditional syllogism is only useful when the antecedent is uncertain; otherwise, the reasoning is either redundant or requires a categorical syllogism.
