When Are Conditional Syllogisms Useful?
Author critiques conditional syllogisms and reduces them to conjunctive form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a conditional syllogism is genuinely beneficial and explain why all useful syllogisms reduce to conjunctive syllogisms.

Core Idea
Conditional syllogisms are useful only when the antecedent is unknown.

The author examines the epistemic value of conditional syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i). He argues that they only convey new knowledge when the antecedent is not already known. Moreover, even then, they ultimately depend on a conjunctive syllogism for their validity. This lesson guides you through his critique and reduction thesis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's main claim
The author argues that conditional syllogisms are only beneficial when the antecedent is doubtful to the hearer. If the antecedent is already known, the syllogism adds nothing new and is superfluous.
2
Understand the condition for utility
The crucial condition is that the antecedent must be 'mashkuk' (doubtful) and the connection (ittisal) between antecedent and consequent must be evident either in itself or through a conjunctive syllogism.
If the antecedent is not doubtful, the conditional is not a genuine connected statement.
3
Examine the example
The author gives the example: 'Every human is a laughing being; every laughing being is an animal.' This does not teach that a human is an animal because that conclusion is already known. Thus the conditional form is useless here.
This illustrates that the conditional syllogism does not produce new knowledge when the antecedent is not in doubt.
4
Trace the reduction to conjunctive syllogisms
The author argues that even when a conditional syllogism is useful, its antecedent is proven either by another conditional or by a conjunctive syllogism. Ultimately, the chain ends in a conjunctive syllogism, so all beneficial syllogisms reduce to the conjunctive form.
The foundation of logical proof is the conjunctive syllogism (qiyas iqtirani).
5
See how reductio ad absurdum also requires conjunctive syllogisms
The author anticipates an objection about conditional syllogisms used in reductio (al-khalf). He replies that the implication between antecedent and consequent is not self-evident but is established by a conjunctive syllogism.
This reinforces the primacy of conjunctive syllogisms.

للاستثناء من مقدمها ما لم يكن مقدمها مشكوكا فيه، والتالي بين اللزوم والاتصال بنفسه أو بما يحججه؛ فقد بان أن القياسات الخلفية والوضعية المتصلة فإن الفائدة في استعمالها على صورة قياسية إنما يكون إذا الأمر إلى قياسات استثنائية مشكوك في مقدماتها. وإنها تتبين بقياسات اقترانية. وإن تبين في أول الأمر بقياسات اقترانية فذلك أوضح ؛ فيكون جميع القياسات المفيدة استعمالها على صورة قياسية ترجع إلى الاقترانيات. ولا يبعد أن يرجع إلى استثنائية لا تحتاج إلى استعمالها على صورة القياس لكن ذلك في القليل. فإن قلت فيما نقول في المقدّمة الشرطية التي مقدمها قياس اقتراني فكيف يتبين مقدمها بقياس اقتراني فنقول إنه بنفسه قياس اقتراني؛ وغرضنا أن الشيء الذي يتبين بالاستثنائي فإنه يتعلق بقياس اقتراني في البيان والغرض حاصل في هذه الصورة. وإن لم يكن كذلك بان بقياس غيره على شيء يتبين أن استثنائية التالي الذي هو النتيجة من المقدم الذي هو القياس ليس على سبيل بيان أمر قياسي عن قياس مفيد. لكن لقائل أن يقول في الاستثنائي الذي في الخلف؛ فنقول إن ذلك لا يكون بين المقدم بين لزوم التالي للمقدم. وكيف يكون بين المقدم ومقدمه هو الذي يراد إبطاله، وكيف يكون بين لزوم التالي وإنما يتبين ذلك بقياس اقتراني، وهذه كلها تعرف بالتأمل في الأمثلة القياسية.
The exception [i.e., the conditional syllogism with exception of the antecedent] must either not convey anything new to the mind or convey by repeating what is already obtained, except that the time of its occurrence to the mind is not the time of uttering the exception, as was the case with uttering the major premise when the major premise was occurring to the mind in what dispenses with utterance. Thus this exception does not convey something essential in the connection to the goal, but rather something whose assent is prior. And what is prior in assent, the indication of it by speech does not correspond to the time of need, so it is superfluous both in terms of speech and in terms of conveying, so it does not convey with the exception of it. Hence it is clear that using such premises in the form of a syllogism is laborious. What is obligatory is to use it in the way we said: 'Whenever such-and-such is, such-and-such is', and not 'Whenever such-and-such is, such-and-such is' in the form of a syllogism; then it would have a syllogistic benefit. For if someone says 'Every human is a laughing being' it is true, and if he says 'And every laughing being is an animal' it is true, but this is not useful because he knows that human is an animal before he knows that it is a laughing being. So one must understand the statement of the First Teacher in this form. And one should not think that he holds that there is between implication and between truth... or that the antecedent cannot be a subject presented while it is not doubtful, such that if the antecedent were not doubtful, the conditional proposition would not be connected. And the statement of someone: 'If human is an animal, then both are a body' is either something whose antecedent is doubtful, or a disconnected statement, but its meaning is that using what is not doubtful in its antecedent by excepting its antecedent, if its consequent is evident in implication or its implication to something is evident, or it is evident in itself, is a non-syllogistic matter, i.e., not corresponding in its indication to what is needed. For if the consequent is not evident in implication, it is farther; then inevitably one needs in its proof a conjunctive syllogism which ultimately terminates in it so that the connection becomes evident. Accordingly, the conditional premise is not subject to exception of its antecedent unless its antecedent is doubtful and the consequent is evident in implication and the connection is evident in itself or by what proves it. So it has become clear that the hypothetical and positional conditional syllogisms: the benefit in using them in syllogistic form is only when their antecedent is doubtful and their connection is evident in itself or by a conjunctive syllogism, so a conjunctive syllogism is necessary. As for the antecedent, it cannot be other than being made evident by conditional syllogisms or conjunctive ones: if it is made evident by a conditional, then inevitably it leads ultimately to conditional syllogisms whose premises are doubtful. And they are made evident by conjunctive syllogisms. And if it is made evident from the outset by conjunctive syllogisms, that is clearer; then all beneficial syllogisms whose use is in syllogistic form reduce to conjunctive ones. And it is not unlikely that they reduce to a conditional that does not need to be used in syllogistic form, but that is rare. If you say: regarding our statement about the conditional premise whose antecedent is a conjunctive syllogism, how is its antecedent made evident by a conjunctive syllogism? We say: it is itself a conjunctive syllogism; and our purpose is that the thing which is made evident by the conditional is related to a conjunctive syllogism in explanation, and the purpose is achieved in this form. And if it is not so, it is made evident by something else upon something that makes evident that the conditional of the consequent which is the conclusion from the premise which is the syllogism is not by way of making evident a syllogistic matter from a useful syllogism. But someone may say regarding the conditional that is in reductio [al-khalf]; we say that that does not have the antecedent evident regarding the implication of the consequent to the antecedent. And how can the antecedent be evident when its antecedent is what is intended to be refuted? And how can the implication of the consequent be evident? This is only made evident by a conjunctive syllogism. All this is known by reflection on syllogistic examples.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage is a critical examination of the use of conditional syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i) in syllogistic form. The author argues that a conditional premise (mutasila) used in an exceptive syllogism is only beneficial when its antecedent (muqaddam) is unknown to the hearer. If the antecedent is already known, the conditional adds no new knowledge — it is 'superfluous' (fadl). The author uses an example: 'Every human is a laughing being; every laughing being is an animal' — this does not teach that a human is an animal, because that is already known. The so-called 'First Teacher' (Aristotle) is said to have intended to illustrate the form, not to claim that conditional syllogisms always produce new knowledge. The author stresses that the antecedent must be doubtful; otherwise the conditional is not a genuine connected statement. He then analyzes the reduction of all syllogisms to conjunctive (iqtirani) syllogisms. If the antecedent of a conditional is proven by another syllogism, that process must end in a conjunctive syllogism. Conditional syllogisms that are useful ultimately depend on conjunctive ones. The passage anticipates an objection about conditional syllogisms used in reductio ad absurdum (qiyas khalf), and replies that even there, the implication between antecedent and consequent is not self-evident but is established by a conjunctive syllogism. The overall conclusion is that the foundation of logical proof is the conjunctive syllogism.

The Syllogism

The Compound Structure of Qiyas Khalf
Author defines qiyas khalf as a compound of two conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a qiyas khalf (reductio ad absurdum) by combining a conjunctive conditional syllogism with an exceptive conditional syllogism.

Core Idea
Qiyas khalf is a compound of two conditional syllogisms, not a single step.

Reductio ad absurdum is a powerful method in Islamic logic, used extensively in kalam and usul al-fiqh to prove a proposition by showing its negation leads to an absurdity. The author here clarifies that qiyas khalf is not a simple syllogism but a compound of two conditional syllogisms: first a conjunctive syllogism that derives a new conditional, then an exceptive syllogism that extracts the desired conclusion. Understanding this structure is key to correctly applying reductio arguments in theological and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two component syllogisms
The author states that qiyas khalf is composed of two conditional syllogisms: one conjunctive (iqtirani) and one exceptive (istithna'i). Recognize that the first syllogism produces a conditional conclusion that becomes the premise for the second.
2
Analyze the conjunctive syllogism
The conjunctive syllogism consists of a conditional minor premise (mutasila sughra) and a categorical major premise (hamliyya kubra) that shares the consequent of the conditional. From these, a new conditional is deduced: if the antecedent of the minor holds, then the predicate of the major applies to the consequent.
For example: If P then Q; every Q is R; therefore if P then R.
3
Analyze the exceptive syllogism
The conclusion of the conjunctive syllogism becomes the conditional premise of the exceptive syllogism. The exception (istithna') affirms or denies one part to deduce the other, typically leading to the original thesis by contradiction.
Standard forms: modus ponens or modus tollens on the derived conditional.
4
Combine the two into the overall proof
Recognize that the reductio argument is a chain: the first syllogism transforms the initial assumption into a conditional, and the second uses that conditional to derive the desired categorical conclusion. The compound nature means each step must be individually valid.
The author emphasizes that qiyas khalf is 'in reality' a compound, not a simple single syllogism.
5
Construct an Islamic example
Apply the structure to a theological claim. For instance, to prove that the world is temporally originated (hadith), assume it is eternal (qadim). Derive that if it were eternal, it would have no need for a creator; but everything temporally originated requires a creator; therefore if the world is eternal, then it does not require a creator, which is absurd. This yields the original claim that the world is temporally originated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في القياس الخلف ]
أما القياس الخلف / فإنه بالحقيقة قياس مركب من قياسين شرطيين؛ [۲۳۹ظ] أحدهما قياس اقتراني مركب من متّصلة صغرى وحملية كبرى تشارك تالي المتّصلة، وثانيهما قياس استثنائي مقدّمته الشرطية متصلة هي نتيجة القياس
Chapter: On the Reductio Syllogism. As for the reductio syllogism (qiyas khalf), it is in reality a syllogism composed of two conditional syllogisms: one of them is a conjunctive syllogism composed of a conditional minor premise and a categorical major premise that shares the consequent of the conditional; and the second is an exceptive syllogism whose conditional premise is the conclusion of the syllogism.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage opens a new section (fasl) on the qiyas khalf, or reductio ad absurdum. The author defines it as a compound syllogism consisting of two conditional syllogisms (qiyasayn shartiyayn). The first is a conjunctive syllogism (qiyas iqtirani) made up of a conditional minor premise (mutasila sughra) — i.e., a conditional statement serving as the minor premise — and a categorical major premise (hamliyya kubra) that shares (i.e., predicates something of) the consequent (tali) of that conditional. The second is an exceptive syllogism (qiyas istithna'i) whose conditional premise (muqaddimatuhu al-shartiyya al-mutasila) is the conclusion of the first syllogism. This means that the reductio proves a proposition by assuming its negation and deriving an absurdity, then using that derivation to affirm the original. The structure: from a conditional (if P then Q) and a categorical (every Q is R) we deduce a new conditional (if P then R). Then through an exceptive syllogism, we affirm the opposite of P or the negation of the antecedent, typically leading to the original claim. The passage is concise and serves as a definitional statement, but the full elaboration likely follows in subsequent lines.

Key Takeaway
Qiyas khalf is a compound syllogism: first a conjunctive conditional syllogism produces a conditional that then serves as the premise for an exceptive syllogism, yielding the proof by contradiction.

Structure and Objection of Qiyas al-Khalf
Author describes the customary way reductio is used and then presents an objection against it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the logical structure of a reductio (qiyas al-khalf) and identify the objection raised against it in this passage.

Core Idea
The reductio is a conditional syllogism whose validity hinges on the necessary implication being a genuine contradiction.

In this passage, the author explains how reductio arguments are customarily presented: a categorical syllogism is set up, but the conclusion is left unstated, and instead the contradictory of the consequent is asserted as impossible. He then raises a critical objection: the necessary implication might not be a true contradiction or might not be necessary. This lesson will help you dissect both the structure and the critique, sharpening your ability to evaluate reductio arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the reductio structure
Find the categorical syllogism used (e.g., every J is B, every B is A, so every J is A). Note that the conclusion is not stated; instead, the contradictory of the consequent is declared impossible.
The author calls this a 'categorical syllogism' (qiyas iqtirani) used to produce the consequent.
2
Analyze the exception step (istithna')
Observe how the impossibility of the consequent ('this is impossible') functions as an exception of the contradictory of the consequent, leading to the required conclusion.
This turns the argument into a conditional syllogism: If A then B; not B; therefore not A.
3
Examine the terminology debate
Distinguish between 'khalf' (with fatha, meaning contradiction) and 'khulf' (with dhammah, meaning breaking a promise). The author insists on the former, rejecting the latter as linguistically incorrect for this context.
This clarifies the technical term and avoids confusion with ordinary language.
4
Trace the objection
The objection states that the necessary implication (lazim) may either not be a genuine contradiction (khalf) or may not be necessary if the contradictory of the result is true. Consider how this challenges the reductio's validity.
The text ends before completing the objection; the implication is that the reductio might rely on a false premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم العادة جرت في استعمال الخلف بأن يستعمل ذلك القياس الاقتراني. ثمَّ يترك النتيجة فلا يذكر بل يذكر تاليها ثم يذكر ما هو بالحقيقة استثناء لنقيض تاليها وهو تالي نتيجة القياس الاقتراني ليلزم منه المطلوب؛ فيقال مثلا إن كان ليس كل «ج ب كاذبا فكل ج ب صادق وكل «ب أ» فكل «ج أ»، وهذا محال فليس كل ج ب صادقًا. فقوله هذا محال معناه أنه ليس كل ج أ) وهو استثناء نقيض التالي. ثمَّ هذا القياس إنما يسمى بالخلف لكونه مستلزما للمحال وهو الخلف. ويقال أيضًا إنه إنما يسمى به لكون الطلب فيه بالخلف من الخلف. وأما قول من قال: إنه قياس الخلف بضم الخاء فذلك لا يصح إلا وأن يكون الخلف بمعنى الخلف لغة، وذلك في حيز المنع فإن الخلف بضم الخاء إنما يكون في المواعيد فقط. وقد يورد على هذا القياس من الشبه أيضًا؛ وذلك من وجوه [1] منها أن يقال: هذا اللازم لا يكون خلفا أو لا يكون لازما لصدق النقيض وهو نقيض النتيجة كما مرّ فإنه إذا كان لازمًا خلف كان الخلف من لوازم الصادقتين. ولو أمكن هذا فلا يمكن أن وهو
Then the custom is to use the reductio such that the categorical syllogism is used. Then the result is left unmentioned, but its consequent is mentioned, then what is in reality an exception of the contradictory of that consequent (which is the consequent of the result of the categorical syllogism) is mentioned, to elicit the sought conclusion. So it is said, for example: 'If it is not that every J is B is false, then every J is B is true, and every B is A, so every J is A, and this is impossible, so not every J is B is true.' So his saying 'this is impossible' means that it is not that every J is A, and it is an exception of the contradictory of the consequent. Then this syllogism is called 'khalf' because it entails an impossibility which is a contradiction (khalf). It is also said that it is so called because the demand in it is by way of contradicting (khalf). As for the one who says that it is 'qiyas al-khalf' with a dhammah on the kha' (i.e., pronouncing 'khulf'), that is not valid except if 'khalf' means 'promise' in language, and that is objectionable, because 'khulf' with dhammah is only in promises. And there is also an objection against this syllogism based on ambiguities, from several aspects. One of them is that it is said: 'This necessary implication is either not a contradiction (khalf) or not necessary because of the truth of the contradictory which is the contradictory of the result as mentioned.' For if the necessary implication were a contradiction, the contradiction would be among the necessary implications of two true statements. And if this were possible, it cannot be that ... (incomplete).
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage first describes the customary way of presenting the reductio argument: the categorical syllogism is set up but the conclusion is left unstated; instead, the contradictory of the consequent is asserted as impossible, which in effect is an exception to the conditional. An example is given. Then there is a discussion of the naming: 'khalf' with fatha vs. 'khulf' with dhammah. Then an objection is raised: the implication might not be a genuine contradiction, or the contradiction might arise from two true premises, which would be problematic. The text ends mid-sentence.

Two Methods of Reductio Ad Absurdum and Their Weaknesses
Author describes two methods of reductio ad absurdum and criticizes their flaws.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two methods of reductio ad absurdum described in the passage and explain the logical flaw in each method as critiqued by the author.

Core Idea
Reductio arguments are not automatically sound; the source of contradiction must be checked.

This passage critiques two methods of proving a reductio ad absurdum argument. The first method claims that the major premise is necessary, but the author denies this because the absurdity might come from the whole combination rather than a part. The second method adds unnecessary complexity without fixing the first flaw. Understanding this critique helps you evaluate the validity of reductio arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two methods
Read the passage carefully and note that the author first describes a method where someone claims that the consequent leads to an absurdity, and then identifies the absurdity as the minor premise. Then the author criticizes this method in two ways: 'the first' (الأول) and 'the second' (الثاني). Locate these sections.
The two methods are not explicitly named; they are referred to as 'each of these two ways'.
2
Analyze the first method's claim and flaw
The first method claims that the major premise's truth is necessary. The author's objection is that the absurdity may result from the whole conjunction (التأليف) as a whole, not from any individual premise. Therefore, the major premise is not necessarily true; it is possible that the absurdity is entailed by the whole combination.
Consider an example: if both premises together lead to absurdity, each premise individually may still be true.
3
Analyze the second method's additional flaw
The second method includes everything the first method includes, plus unnecessary and elaborate embellishments (التكلُّف في التطويل من غير حاجة). It is thus even more problematic because it compounds the first error with needless complexity.
The author does not detail the second method's structure, only criticizes its redundancy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقد ادعوا الضرورة في صدقها. ومنهم من تعرض لبيانه بعد بيان أن التالي يلزم منه ومن حق محال فقال هذا المحال إما أن يكون عين الصغرى أو عين الكبرى أو عين نفس التأليف لكن ليس عين نفس التأليف بدليل الخلف في غير هذا الموضع، ولا عين الكبرى؛ فإن الحق لا يفضي إلى الباطل فهو إذن عين الصغرى التي هي التالي، فيكون التالي محالا . ثم كل واحد من هذين الطريقين لا يكون كما ينبغي؛ أما الأول فلأن الكبرى التي ادعوا الضرورة في صدقها ممنوعة لاحتمال أن يكون المستلزم للمحال المجموع من حيث هو المجموع ليس ما يكون في ضمنه من الأجزاء. وأما الثاني فلأنه مشتمل على ما عليه الأول / وعلى غيره كذلك، وهو التكلُّف في التطويل من غير حاجة.
They claimed necessity in its truth. And among them is one who undertook to explain it after clarifying that the consequent follows from it and from a true (premise) an absurdity, so he said: This absurdity is either the minor premise itself, or the major premise itself, or the composition itself. But it is not the composition itself, by the evidence of reductio in another place, nor is it the major premise, for truth does not lead to falsehood. So it is therefore the minor premise, which is the consequent, so the consequent is absurd. Then each of these two ways is not as it should be; as for the first, because the major premise whose truth they claimed as necessary is denied, since it is possible that what entails the absurdity is the whole as a whole, not what is within it of parts. As for the second, because it includes what the first includes, and other things as well, and it is an unnecessary elaborate embellishment.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage critiques two methods of proving a reductio ad absurdum argument. The author first describes a method where someone attempts to prove the necessity of a premise by showing that if it were false, an absurdity would result. The argument claims that the absurdity must be either the minor premise, the major premise, or the composition of the two, and then eliminates the composition and major premise, concluding that the minor premise must be absurd, hence the consequent is impossible. The author then points out weaknesses in two ways this argument is presented. The first way claims necessity in the truth of the major premise, but this is denied because it is possible that the absurdity follows from the whole combination rather than from any individual part. The second way is even more flawed as it includes all the problems of the first plus additional useless embellishment. The author shows that these methods are not rigorous.

Key Terms
الخلف
الصغرى
الكبرى
التأليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the first method of reductio problematic?
A) The minor premise cannot be the absurdity.
B) The major premise claimed as necessary may not be necessary because the absurdity may arise from the whole combination, not a part.
C) The method uses an invalid figure of syllogism.
D) The method is too short and lacks detail.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a reductio argument depends on whether the contradiction stems solely from the denied proposition, not from the conjunction of premises.

The Syllogism
intermediate
6 min
Structure and Naming of Reductio (Khalf)
Author explains the standard usage of reductio as a categorical syllogism and its nomenclature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of a reductio argument as a categorical syllogism and explain why it is called 'khalf'.

Core Idea
Reductio proves through absurdity, omitting the conclusion and invoking its contradictory.

In classical logic, reductio ad absurdum (khalf) is essential for establishing a claim by showing its negation leads to contradiction. The passage details the standard procedure: a categorical syllogism where the conclusion is left unstated, and instead the contradictory of its consequent is used as an exception. Understanding this form helps recognize the logical backbone of many arguments in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the goal of reductio
Recognize that the aim is to prove a proposition by assuming its negation and deriving an absurdity (khalf). The absurdity can be a contradiction or a known falsehood.
2
Recognize the underlying categorical syllogism
The author states that reductio uses the conjunctive (categorical) syllogism (qiyas iqtirani). In the example, the categorical syllogism is: every B is A, and every J is B, therefore every J is A.
3
Understand the omission of the conclusion
The conclusion of the categorical syllogism is not explicitly stated. Instead, its consequent is mentioned, and then the contradictory of that consequent is taken as an exception to derive the desired result.
This step is crucial: the absurdity is shown by contradicting the consequent.
4
Parse the example
Follow the given example: Assume 'every J is B' (negation of desired 'not every J is B'), derive 'every J is A' via the categorical syllogism, note that this is absurd, so the assumption is false, hence 'not every J is B' is proven.
The absurdity 'every J is A' contradicts a known fact (e.g., some J are not A).
5
Note the etymology
The argument is called 'khalf' because it entails an absurdity (khalf). The pronunciation is with fatha, not damma, as 'khulf' with damma means breaking promises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم العادة جرت في استعمال الخلف بأن يستعمل ذلك القياس الاقتراني. ثمَّ يترك النتيجة فلا يذكر بل يذكر تاليها ثم يذكر ما هو بالحقيقة استثناء لنقيض تاليها وهو تالي نتيجة القياس الاقتراني ليلزم منه المطلوب؛ فيقال مثلا إن كان ليس كل «ج ب كاذبا فكل ج ب صادق وكل «ب أ» فكل «ج أ»، وهذا محال فليس كل ج ب صادقًا. فقوله هذا محال معناه أنه ليس كل ج أ) وهو استثناء نقيض التالي. ثمَّ هذا القياس إنما يسمى بالخلف لكونه مستلزما للمحال وهو الخلف. ويقال أيضًا إنه إنما يسمى به لكون الطلب فيه بالخلف من الخلف. وأما قول من قال: إنه قياس الخلف بضم الخاء فذلك لا يصح إلا وأن يكون الخلف بمعنى الخلف لغة، وذلك في حيز المنع فإن الخلف بضم الخاء إنما يكون في المواعيد فقط.
Then the custom has been in the use of reductio to use that conjunctive syllogism. Then the conclusion is left aside; it is not mentioned, rather its consequent is mentioned, then what is in reality an exception of the contradictory of its consequent is mentioned, and that is the consequent of the conclusion of the conjunctive syllogism, so that the required follows from it. For example, it is said: If it is not that every J is B false, then every J is B true, and every B is A, so every J is A, and this is absurd, so not every J is B true. So his statement 'this is absurd' means that it is not every J is A, and it is an exception of the contradictory of the consequent. Then this syllogism is called reductio (khalf) because it entails an absurdity (khalf). And it is also said that it is called by this because the demand in it is by way of substitution (khalf) from substitution. As for the saying of one who says: It is 'qiyas al-khulf' with a damma on the kha', that is not correct unless khulf means 'substitution' in language, and that is debatable, for khulf with a damma is only in promises.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage explains the standard procedure for a reductio ad absurdum argument. The author describes it as a conjunctive (categorical) syllogism where the conclusion is not explicitly stated; instead, the consequent of the conclusion is mentioned, and then the contradictory of that consequent is taken as an exception to derive the desired result. An example is given using variables: if it is not false that every J is B, then (by conversion?) actually the example is: 'If it is not that every J is B is false, then every J is B is true, and every B is A, so every J is A. This is absurd, so not every J is B is true.' The absurdity is the statement 'every J is A' which contradicts something known. The author clarifies the etymology: it is called 'khalf' because it entails an absurdity (khalf). Some pronounce it 'khulf' but that is incorrect in this context because 'khulf' with a damma refers to breaking promises, not logical contradiction.

Key Terms
الخلف (al-khalf): A syllogism that proves a proposition by deriving a contradiction from its negation; named after the absurdity (khalf) it produces.
القَياس الاقْتِراني (al-qiyas al-iqtirani): A categorical syllogism where the premises are conjoined without a conditional. [Note: In later texts, qiyas iqtirani is contrasted with istithna'i (conditional syllogism).]
نقيض التالي (naqid al-tali): The contradictory of the consequent in a conditional statement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the standard structure of a reductio syllogism?
A) The conclusion is stated first, then the premises.
B) The conclusion is omitted, and its consequent is mentioned, then the contradictory of that consequent is used as an exception.
C) The minor premise is denied, and the major premise is affirmed.
D) The syllogism is conditional, not categorical.

Key Takeaway
Reductio (khalf) is a categorical syllogism where the conclusion is omitted and its contradictory is used as an exception to derive the required proposition by contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Consider the argument: 'If the Qur'an were created, it would be possible for it to be corrupted. But the Qur'an is preserved. Therefore, the Qur'an is not created.' Identify the categorical syllogism underlying this reductio and state the assumed premise that leads to absurdity.
?

The Syllogism

First Objection: Contradiction Not Following
First objection: necessary consequence may not be a contradiction or may follow from premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical flaw in a reductio when the supposed contradiction does not follow from the premises alone.

Core Idea
A reductio's contradiction must follow from the contradictory alone, not from the premises, or it is useless.

The first objection to reductio ad absurdum challenges whether the supposed contradiction actually follows from the premises. If the contradiction is already entailed by the premises, the reductio becomes circular and fails to prove anything. Let us examine the objector's reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Locate the reductio's structure
Identify the two premises (الصادقتين) and the conclusion in the target reductio. The objector focuses on what necessarily follows from them.
2
Understand the objection's claim
The objector says the necessary consequence (اللازم) may not be a contradiction at all, or if it is, it follows from the premises themselves, not from the assumption of the contradictory.
The passage states: 'this necessary consequence is not a contradiction or is not necessary for the truth of the contradictory.'
3
Analyse the consequence of circularity
If the contradiction were a necessary concomitant of the premises, then the reductio would merely restate what is already implied, making it trivial and useless.
The text says: 'if it were a necessary contradiction, the contradiction would be a necessary concomitant of the two true (premises). And if this were possible, it would not be possible for the reductio syllogism to be useful.'
4
Assess the validity of the objection
The objection highlights that a successful reductio must derive a contradiction from the negation of the conclusion plus only one premise (or without additional premises), otherwise the argument is circular.
Later logicians responded by specifying that the reductio assumes only the negation of the conclusion, not both premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يورد على هذا القياس من الشبه أيضًا؛ وذلك من وجوه [1] منها أن يقال: هذا اللازم لا يكون خلفا أو لا يكون لازما لصدق النقيض وهو نقيض النتيجة كما مرّ فإنه إذا كان لازمًا خلف كان الخلف من لوازم الصادقتين. ولو أمكن هذا فلا يمكن أن يكون قياس الخلف مفيدًا. وكيف يكون مفيدًا وإنه لا يلزم منه ما يكون منافيًا لصدق المقدمتين . [ب] ومنها أن يقال: هذا وإن كان خلفا فلا يكون لازما لصدق النقيض بل يكون لازما لصدق النقيض مع الغير وهو المقدمة الصادقة كما مر . ولو كان لازما لصدق النقيض مع الغير لكان يلزم من كذبه كذب ذلك المجموع؛ وهو صدق النقيض مع الغير. ولا يلزم من كذب المجموع كذب كل واحد من جزئيه في نفس الأمر فإنه يمكن أن يكون كل واحد من جزئيه صادقا وهو كاذب. ألا ترى أنه يصدق أن يقال: كل إنسان (۱) متنفس ويصدق أيضًا كل إنسان مستنشق، ولا يصدق أن يقال كل إنسان متنفّس مع كونه مستنشقا [ت] ومنها أن يقال: لا يلزم من كذب النتيجة كذب كل واحدة من المقدمتين في البعض من الصور؛ وذلك من جملة ما يقدح في هذا القياس. وإنما قلناه؛ وذلك لأنه يصح أن يقال كل كاتب بالفعل إنسان، ولا شيء من الإنسان دائم الحركة من حيث هو الإنسان فلا شيء من الكاتب بالفعل دائم الحركة من حيث هو كاتب بالفعل؛ فإذن لا يلزم من كون هذه النتيجة خلفًا كذب كل واحدة من المقدمتين.
And there are also objections raised against this syllogism from fallacies; and that from [several] aspects: [1] One is that it is said: This necessary consequence is not a contradiction or is not necessary for the truth of the contradictory, which is the contradictory of the conclusion as mentioned. For if it were a necessary contradiction, the contradiction would be a necessary concomitant of the two true (premises). And if this were possible, it would not be possible for the reductio syllogism to be useful. How can it be useful when it does not entail something that contradicts the truth of the two premises? [ب] Another is that it is said: This, even if it is a contradiction, is not necessary for the truth of the contradictory alone, but is necessary for the truth of the contradictory together with something else, namely the true premise as mentioned. And if it were necessary for the truth of the contradictory together with something else, then from its falsity would follow the falsity of that whole; that is, the truth of the contradictory together with the other. And from the falsity of the whole, it does not follow that each of its parts is false in actual fact, for it is possible that each of its two parts is true while the whole is false. Do you not see that it is true to say: every human is breathing, and also true: every human is inhaling, but it is not true to say: every human is breathing while being inhaling? [ت] Another is that it is said: From the falsity of the conclusion, it does not follow that each of the two premises is false in some forms; and that is among the things that invalidate this syllogism. And we have said that; for it is valid to say: every actual writer is human, and no human is perpetually moving insofar as he is human, so no actual writer is perpetually moving insofar as he is an actual writer; so then from this conclusion being a contradiction, it does not follow that each of the two premises is false.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage presents three objections to the validity of reductio ad absurdum. The first objection [1] argues that the necessary consequence (the supposed contradiction) may not actually be a contradiction, or if it is, it follows necessarily from the two premises being true. If that were the case, the reductio would be useless because it would not generate anything that conflicts with the truth of the premises themselves. The second objection [ب] concedes that a contradiction follows, but not from the contradictory alone; rather it follows from the contradictory combined with some other true premise. If the combination is false, it does not imply each part is false. An example is given: 'Every human is breathing' and 'Every human is inhaling' are each true, but the conjunction 'Every human is breathing while inhaling' is false (since not all humans are inhaling at all times? Actually the example is problematic but the point stands: a false conjunction can have true parts). The third objection [ت] notes that even if the conclusion is false (a contradiction), it does not entail that each premise is false. An example: Every actual writer is human, and no human is perpetually moving as human (i.e., in respect of humanity), so no actual writer is perpetually moving as actual writer. This conclusion may be false? The example is meant to show a case where the conclusion is false but each premise is true? Actually it's a syllogism that produces a false conclusion? The author uses it to show that a false conclusion does not necessarily mean both premises are false.

Key Terms
اللازم
النقيض
الصادقتين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first objection to reductio ad absurdum, why does the reductio become useless if the necessary consequence follows from the premises themselves (الصادقتين)?
A) Because the contradiction is not actually a contradiction.
B) Because the reductio would be circular: the contradiction is already entailed by the premises.
C) Because the premises are false.
D) Because the conclusion is true.

Key Takeaway
The first objection warns that a reductio argument is only useful if the contradiction follows from the assumption of the contradictory, not from the premises alone.

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Second Objection: Contradiction from a Conjunction
Second objection: contradiction follows from a conjunction, so falsity does not distribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a contradiction derived from a conjunction does not disprove any single component of that conjunction.

Core Idea
A false conjunction can have true parts; a contradiction from a combination does not refute the contradictory alone.

The second objection concedes that a contradiction does arise, but argues it follows from the combination of the contradictory and another true premise, not from the contradictory alone. Since the falsity of a conjunction does not imply the falsity of its conjuncts, the reductio cannot isolate the contradictory as false.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's concession
The objector allows that a contradiction (خلف) occurs in the reductio, but argues it is not necessary for the truth of the contradictory alone.
2
Locate the combination
The contradiction follows from the truth of the contradictory together with another true premise (المقدمة الصادقة). This is a conjunction.
The text says: 'it is necessary for the truth of the contradictory together with something else, namely the true premise.'
3
Apply the logical principle of conjunction
If a conjunction is false, it does not follow that each conjunct is false. The objector illustrates with an example: 'every human breathes' and 'every human inhales' are each true, but the conjunction 'every human breathes while inhaling' is false.
The passage: 'it is possible that each of its two parts is true while the whole is false.'
4
Implication for reductio
Therefore, the falsity of the conjunction (the contradiction) does not entail that the contradictory (the assumption to be disproved) is false. The reductio cannot proceed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[ب] ومنها أن يقال: هذا وإن كان خلفا فلا يكون لازما لصدق النقيض بل يكون لازما لصدق النقيض مع الغير وهو المقدمة الصادقة كما مر . ولو كان لازما لصدق النقيض مع الغير لكان يلزم من كذبه كذب ذلك المجموع؛ وهو صدق النقيض مع الغير. ولا يلزم من كذب المجموع كذب كل واحد من جزئيه في نفس الأمر فإنه يمكن أن يكون كل واحد من جزئيه صادقا وهو كاذب. ألا ترى أنه يصدق أن يقال: كل إنسان (۱) متنفس ويصدق أيضًا كل إنسان مستنشق، ولا يصدق أن يقال كل إنسان متنفّس مع كونه مستنشقا [ت] ومنها أن يقال: لا يلزم من كذب النتيجة كذب كل واحدة من المقدمتين في البعض من الصور؛ وذلك من جملة ما يقدح في هذا القياس. وإنما قلناه؛ وذلك لأنه يصح أن يقال كل كاتب بالفعل إنسان، ولا شيء من الإنسان دائم الحركة من حيث هو الإنسان فلا شيء من الكاتب بالفعل دائم الحركة من حيث هو كاتب بالفعل؛ فإذن لا يلزم من كون هذه النتيجة خلفًا كذب كل واحدة من المقدمتين.

7 min
Third Objection: False Conclusion with True Premises
Third objection: false conclusion does not imply false premises; example given.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a case where a false conclusion (contradiction) arises from true premises, thereby challenging the reductio's assumption.

Core Idea
A false conclusion can be derived from true premises, so a contradiction in a reductio does not guarantee a false premise.

The third objection provides a concrete syllogism to show that even if the conclusion is a contradiction (false), the premises may still be true. This undermines the fundamental assumption of reductio ad absurdum that a contradiction must indicate a false premise.

Guided Path
1
Examine the example syllogism
The objector presents: 'Every actual writer is human. No human is perpetually moving as human. Therefore, no actual writer is perpetually moving as actual writer.'
Identify the premises and conclusion explicitly.
2
Evaluate the truth of premises and conclusion
The objector claims the premises are true, but the conclusion is false (a contradiction). In a valid syllogism, if premises are true, conclusion must be true. Here, the conclusion being false suggests the argument is invalid or the premises are not actually true.
The passage states: 'from this conclusion being a contradiction, it does not follow that each of the two premises is false.'
3
Understand the logical point
This example is meant to show that a false conclusion does not necessarily imply false premises; the fault could lie in the logical form of the argument.
The objector uses this to argue that reductio cannot automatically attribute falsity to premises.
4
Apply to reductio ad absurdum
In a reductio, if we derive a contradiction from assuming the negation of the conclusion and other true premises, we conclude the negation is false. But this objection shows that the contradiction might arise from the logical structure rather than from the assumption.
The reductio assumes the argument is valid; if it is not, the objection holds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يورد على هذا القياس من الشبه أيضًا؛ وذلك من وجوه [1] منها أن يقال: هذا اللازم لا يكون خلفا أو لا يكون لازما لصدق النقيض وهو نقيض النتيجة كما مرّ فإنه إذا كان لازمًا خلف كان الخلف من لوازم الصادقتين. ولو أمكن هذا فلا يمكن أن يكون قياس الخلف مفيدًا. وكيف يكون مفيدًا وإنه لا يلزم منه ما يكون منافيًا لصدق المقدمتين . [ب] ومنها أن يقال: هذا وإن كان خلفا فلا يكون لازما لصدق النقيض بل يكون لازما لصدق النقيض مع الغير وهو المقدمة الصادقة كما مر . ولو كان لازما لصدق النقيض مع الغير لكان يلزم من كذبه كذب ذلك المجموع؛ وهو صدق النقيض مع الغير. ولا يلزم من كذب المجموع كذب كل واحد من جزئيه في نفس الأمر فإنه يمكن أن يكون كل واحد من جزئيه صادقا وهو كاذب. ألا ترى أنه يصدق أن يقال: كل إنسان (۱) متنفس ويصدق أيضًا كل إنسان مستنشق، ولا يصدق أن يقال كل إنسان متنفّس مع كونه مستنشقا [ت] ومنها أن يقال: لا يلزم من كذب النتيجة كذب كل واحدة من المقدمتين في البعض من الصور؛ وذلك من جملة ما يقدح في هذا القياس. وإنما قلناه؛ وذلك لأنه يصح أن يقال كل كاتب بالفعل إنسان، ولا شيء من الإنسان دائم الحركة من حيث هو الإنسان فلا شيء من الكاتب بالفعل دائم الحركة من حيث هو كاتب بالفعل؛ فإذن لا يلزم من كون هذه النتيجة خلفًا كذب كل واحدة من المقدمتين.
And there are also objections raised against this syllogism from fallacies; and that from [several] aspects: [1] One is that it is said: This necessary consequence is not a contradiction or is not necessary for the truth of the contradictory, which is the contradictory of the conclusion as mentioned. For if it were a necessary contradiction, the contradiction would be a necessary concomitant of the two true (premises). And if this were possible, it would not be possible for the reductio syllogism to be useful. How can it be useful when it does not entail something that contradicts the truth of the two premises? [ب] Another is that it is said: This, even if it is a contradiction, is not necessary for the truth of the contradictory alone, but is necessary for the truth of the contradictory together with something else, namely the true premise as mentioned. And if it were necessary for the truth of the contradictory together with something else, then from its falsity would follow the falsity of that whole; that is, the truth of the contradictory together with the other. And from the falsity of the whole, it does not follow that each of its parts is false in actual fact, for it is possible that each of its two parts is true while the whole is false. Do you not see that it is true to say: every human is breathing, and also true: every human is inhaling, but it is not true to say: every human is breathing while being inhaling? [ت] Another is that it is said: From the falsity of the conclusion, it does not follow that each of the two premises is false in some forms; and that is among the things that invalidate this syllogism. And we have said that; for it is valid to say: every actual writer is human, and no human is perpetually moving insofar as he is human, so no actual writer is perpetually moving insofar as he is an actual writer; so then from this conclusion being a contradiction, it does not follow that each of the two premises is false.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage presents three objections to the validity of reductio ad absurdum. The first objection [1] argues that the necessary consequence (the supposed contradiction) may not actually be a contradiction, or if it is, it follows necessarily from the two premises being true. If that were the case, the reductio would be useless because it would not generate anything that conflicts with the truth of the premises themselves. The second objection [ب] concedes that a contradiction follows, but not from the contradictory alone; rather it follows from the contradictory combined with some other true premise. If the combination is false, it does not imply each part is false. An example is given: 'Every human is breathing' and 'Every human is inhaling' are each true, but the conjunction 'Every human is breathing while inhaling' is false (since not all humans are inhaling at all times? Actually the example is problematic but the point stands: a false conjunction can have true parts). The third objection [ت] notes that even if the conclusion is false (a contradiction), it does not entail that each premise is false. An example: Every actual writer is human, and no human is perpetually moving as human (i.e., in respect of humanity), so no actual writer is perpetually moving as actual writer. This conclusion may be false? The example is meant to show a case where the conclusion is false but each premise is true? Actually it's a syllogism that produces a false conclusion? The author uses it to show that a false conclusion does not necessarily mean both premises are false.

Key Terms
اللازم
النقيض
الصادقتين

Checkpoint
In the third objection, what does the example of 'every actual writer is human' and 'no human is perpetually moving as human' aim to demonstrate?
A) That the conclusion is false but the premises are true.
B) That the premises are false.
C) That the reductio is always valid.
D) That the conclusion is true.

Key Takeaway
The third objection demonstrates that a false conclusion does not automatically imply false premises; the logical form must be examined before concluding.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Analyse the syllogism in the objection: 'Every actual writer is human; no human is perpetually moving as human; therefore no actual writer is perpetually moving as actual writer.' Is this syllogism valid? If it is valid and the premises are true, is the conclusion necessarily true? If not, why might the objector consider the conclusion false? Discuss the implications for reductio ad absurdum.

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Response to the First Objection on Reductio
Author refutes the first objection by showing that the contradiction follows from the premises themselves, not from an external premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's refutation of the objection against reductio and evaluate its validity.

Core Idea
Reductio works because the contradiction arises in actual fact, not under the assumption.

This lesson addresses a common objection to reductio ad absurdum in Islamic logic. The objector claims that if we assume the premises true, the consequence is true, so no contradiction arises. The author refutes this by distinguishing between the assumption (taqdir) and actual fact (fi nafs al-amr). Understanding this distinction is crucial for validating reductio arguments in kalam and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector claims that under the assumption that both premises are true, the necessary consequence (the supposed contradiction) is true, not contradictory. Thus reductio fails.
2
Locate the author's key distinction
The author distinguishes between 'on the assumption of truth' (taqdir) and 'in actual fact' (fi nafs al-amr). On the assumption, the consequence is true; in actual fact, it is contradictory.
3
Follow the conditional reasoning
If both premises are true in actual fact, they entail a contradiction (false). Anything that entails falsehood cannot itself be true. Therefore the premises are not both true in actual fact.
This is modus tollens: If P then Q; not Q; therefore not P.
4
Verify the conclusion
The reductio shows that the conjunction of premises is false, so at least one premise is false. This validates the reductio: assuming premises true leads to contradiction, so they cannot all be true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب [أ] فنقول في الأول : اللازم من صدق المقدمتين لا يكون خلفا على تقدير صدق المقدمتين. وكيف وأنه صادق على ذلك التقدير بل يكون خلفًا في نفس الأمر فيكون صدق المقدمتين مستلزما لما لا يكون صادقًا في نفس الأمر، وكل ما يكون مستلزما لما لا يكون صادقا في نفس الأمر / فذلك لا يكون صادقًا في نفس الأمر ؛ فكذلك صدق المقدمتين في نفس الأمر. وانتفاء صدقهما لا يمكن أن يكون انتفاء صدق المجموع دون صدق أحد جزأيه؛ فإن المجموع من الصادقتين صادق لا محالة، ولا بانتفاء صدق ما عدا (۱) النسخة- إنسان، صح هامش.
As for the answer [to the first objection], we say regarding the first: The necessary consequence from the truth of the two premises is not a contradiction on the assumption of the truth of the two premises. How could it be, when it is true on that assumption? Rather it is a contradiction in actual fact, so the truth of the two premises entails what is not true in actual fact. And everything that entails what is not true in actual fact, that itself is not true in actual fact. So likewise, the truth of the two premises is not true in actual fact. And the negation of their truth cannot be the negation of the truth of the whole without (negating) the truth of one of its two parts; for the whole composed of two truths is necessarily true. And it is not by the negation of the truth of what is other than (…)
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage provides a response to the first objection against reductio. The objection claimed that the necessary consequence (the contradiction) would follow from the two true premises, making the reductio useless. The author refutes this by distinguishing between assuming the premises true and actual fact. On the assumption that both premises are true, the consequence is not a contradiction because it is true. But in actual fact, the consequence is a contradiction, meaning that the truth of the premises entails something false in actual fact. Anything that entails something false in actual fact cannot itself be true in actual fact. Therefore, the premises cannot both be true in actual fact. The author further argues that if the conjunction of the premises is false, it cannot be that both are true, because a conjunction of two truths is necessarily true. Thus, at least one premise is false. The argument is meant to show that reductio is valid: assuming the premises true leads to a contradiction, so they cannot all be true. The response is cut off, but the core point is made.

Key Terms
تقدير (taqdir)
خلف (khalf)
صدق (sidq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the objection that under the assumption of true premises the consequence is true, not a contradiction?
A) He agrees and rejects reductio.
B) He distinguishes between the assumption of truth and actual fact, arguing that in actual fact the consequence is contradictory.
C) He claims the premises are always false.
D) He changes the subject.

Key Takeaway
The validity of reductio rests on the distinction between assumption and actual fact: the contradiction emerges in actual fact, not under the assumption.

The Syllogism

Defending Qiyas al-Khalf from Objections
Author defends qiyas al-khalf against three objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the three objections against qiyas al-khalf and explain how the author refutes each one using logical analysis.

Core Idea
Qiyas al-Khalf is valid; objections often mistake the composite of premises for individual premises.

In this passage, the author defends qiyas al-khalf (reductio ad absurdum) against three objections that question its utility and validity. Each objection attempts to show that the reductio does not lead to a necessary contradiction of the premises themselves. The author systematically dismantles these objections by clarifying the logical relationship between the premises, their composite, and the contradictory of the desired conclusion. Understanding this dialectical exchange is crucial for correctly applying reductio arguments in Islamic theological and juristic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first objection
The objector claims that the reductio entails the truth of the contradictory only together with the true premise, so its falsity does not require the falsity of each part. The example: 'Every human is breathing' and 'Every human is inhaling' are true individually but their conjunction 'Every human is breathing while inhaling' is false.
The objector thinks the composite's falsehood can coexist with both parts being true.
2
Analyze the author's first reply
The author argues that if the two premises are true, their composite is necessarily true. The falsity of the composite can only arise from the falsity of the contradictory, not from the other premise. The objection's example is denied because each proposition is true only at a certain time, so the conjunction is also true at that time.
The author insists that the composite of true propositions is true, so falsehood must come from the assumed contradictory.
3
Identify the second objection
The objector provides a syllogism: 'Every actual writer is a human; nothing of the human is perpetually moving insofar as he is human; so nothing of the actual writer is perpetually moving insofar as he is an actual writer.' The conclusion is false but the premises may not both be false.
The objector claims that here false conclusion does not imply false premises.
4
Analyze the author's second reply
The author applies the same logic: falsity of conclusion implies falsity of the composite of premises. The example is erroneous because the correct conclusion is 'nothing of the actual writer is perpetually moving insofar as he is human', not 'as an actual writer'.
The author corrects the logical derivation to show the objection is based on a mistaken inference.
5
Synthesize the defense
The author consistently shows that the reductio's validity depends on the composite of premises being necessarily true when premises are true, and falsehood of the conclusion forces the falsehood of the contradictory of the desired result, thus proving the desired conclusion.
All three objections are refuted by the same underlying principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يكون قياس الخلف مفيدًا. وكيف يكون مفيدًا وإنه لا يلزم منه ما يكون منافيًا لصدق المقدمتين. [ب] ومنها أن يقال: هذا وإن كان خلفا فلا يكون لازما لصدق النقيض بل يكون لازما لصدق النقيض مع الغير وهو المقدمة الصادقة كما مر. ولو كان لازما لصدق النقيض مع الغير لكان يلزم من كذبه كذب ذلك المجموع؛ وهو صدق النقيض مع الغير. ولا يلزم من كذب المجموع كذب كل واحد من جزئيه في نفس الأمر فإنه يمكن أن يكون كل واحد من جزئيه صادقا وهو كاذب. ألا ترى أنه يصدق أن يقال: كل إنسان متنفس ويصدق أيضًا كل إنسان مستنشق، ولا يصدق أن يقال كل إنسان متنفّس مع كونه مستنشقا. [ت] ومنها أن يقال: لا يلزم من كذب النتيجة كذب كل واحدة من المقدمتين في البعض من الصور؛ وذلك من جملة ما يقدح في هذا القياس. وإنما قلناه؛ وذلك لأنه يصح أن يقال كل كاتب بالفعل إنسان، ولا شيء من الإنسان دائم الحركة من حيث هو الإنسان فلا شيء من الكاتب بالفعل دائم الحركة من حيث هو كاتب بالفعل؛ فإذن لا يلزم من كون هذه النتيجة خلفًا كذب كل واحدة من المقدمتين.
وأما الجواب [أ] فنقول في الأول: اللازم من صدق المقدمتين لا يكون خلفا على تقدير صدق المقدمتين. وكيف وأنه صادق على ذلك التقدير بل يكون خلفًا في نفس الأمر فيكون صدق المقدمتين مستلزما لما لا يكون صادقًا في نفس الأمر، وكل ما يكون مستلزما لما لا يكون صادقا في نفس الأمر فذلك لا يكون صادقًا في نفس الأمر؛ فكذلك صدق المقدمتين في نفس الأمر. وانتفاء صدقهما لا يمكن أن يكون انتفاء صدق المجموع دون صدق أحد جزأيه؛ فإن المجموع من الصادقتين صادق لا محالة، ولا بانتفاء صدق ما عدا النقيض؛ إذ هو صادق بالضرورة؛ والكلام فيه فيكون بانتفاء صدق النقيض إذن وهو المطلوب. ولا يقال المجموع المركب من الصادقتين لا يلزم أن يكون صادقا؛ إذ المجموع عبارة عن تلك الأجزاء فلو كان كل واحد منها صادقًا كان المجموع صادقًا وإلا لكان مركبًا منها ومن الغير؛ وذلك باطل وإلا لكان المركب من الثلاثة مركبًا منها، ومن الغير أيضًا إلى غير النهاية. [ب] وفي الثاني نقول بمثل ما قلناه في الأول فإنه إذا لزم من كذبه كذب المجموع لزم من كذبه كذب النقيض كذلك لما مر. وأما قوله ألا ترى أنه يصدق كل إنسان متنفّس إلى آخره، فذلك في حيز المنع لما أن قولنا كل إنسان متنفس لا يكون صادقًا إلا وأن يقال كل إنسان متنفس في وقت من الأوقات. وكذلك قولنا كل إنسان مستنشق. ولو كان كذلك فكل واحد منهما لا يكون صادقًا إلا وأن يكون المجموع صادقًا فيصدق أن يقال كل إنسان متنفّس مع كونه مستنشقا؛ إذ هو في وقت وذلك في وقت. [ث] وفي الثالث نقول: إنه في حيز المنع أيضًا فإنه يلزم من كذب النتيجة كذب مجموع المقدمتين لما مر، وإن لم يلزم منه كذب كل واحدة من المقدمتين. وأما المثال الذي مرَّ ذكره فذلك خطأ فإنه لا يلزم من ذلك المركب قولنا لا شيء من الكاتب بالفعل دائم الحركة من حيث هو كاتب بالفعل بل يلزم منه قولنا لا شيء من الكاتب بالفعل دائم الحركة من حيث هو الإنسان.
The syllogism by reductio is useful. How is it useful when it does not entail something that contradicts the truth of the two premises? [B] Among them is that it is said: This, even if it is a reductio, does not entail the truth of the contradictory, but rather entails the truth of the contradictory along with something else, namely the true premise as mentioned. And if it entailed the truth of the contradictory along with something else, then its falsity would require the falsity of that composite; that is, the truth of the contradictory along with the other. And the falsity of the composite does not require the falsity of each of its two parts in reality, for it is possible that each of its two parts is true while the composite is false. Do you not see that it is true to say: Every human is breathing, and also true to say: Every human is inhaling, but it is not true to say: Every human is breathing while being inhaling. [T] Among them is that it is said: The falsity of the conclusion does not require the falsity of each of the two premises in some forms; and that is among the things that undermine this syllogism. And we only said it; for it is correct to say: Every actual writer is a human, and nothing of the human is perpetually moving insofar as he is human, so nothing of the actual writer is perpetually moving insofar as he is an actual writer; thus the falsity of this conclusion as a reductio does not require the falsity of each of the two premises. As for the answer [A] we say concerning the first: The entailment from the truth of the two premises is not a reductio on the assumption of the truth of the two premises. How so? For it is true on that assumption, rather it is a reductio in reality, so the truth of the two premises entails what is not true in reality, and everything that entails what is not true in reality is itself not true in reality; likewise the truth of the two premises in reality. And the negation of their truth cannot be the negation of the truth of the composite without the negation of the truth of one of its two parts; for the composite of two true propositions is necessarily true, nor by the negation of the truth of anything other than the contradictory, for it is necessarily true; and the discussion is about it, so it is by the negation of the truth of the contradictory then, and that is what is required. And it is not said that the composite of the two true propositions does not necessarily have to be true; for the composite is an expression of those parts, so if each of them is true, then the composite is true, otherwise it would be composed of them and something else; and that is false, otherwise the composite of three would be composed of them and something else ad infinitum. [B] And concerning the second we say similar to what we said concerning the first: For if the falsity of the composite follows from its falsity, then the falsity of the contradictory follows from its falsity as mentioned. As for his saying: Do you not see that it is true that every human is breathing etc., that is in the realm of denial, because our saying 'Every human is breathing' is not true unless one says 'Every human is breathing at some time'. And likewise our saying 'Every human is inhaling'. And if that were the case, then each of them is not true unless the composite is true, so it is true to say 'Every human is breathing while being inhaling', for it is at a time and that is at a time. [Th] And concerning the third we say: It is also in the realm of denial, for the falsity of the conclusion requires the falsity of the composite of the two premises as mentioned, even if it does not require the falsity of each of the two premises. As for the example mentioned, that is an error, for that composite does not entail our saying 'Nothing of the actual writer is perpetually moving insofar as he is an actual writer', rather it entails our saying 'Nothing of the actual writer is perpetually moving insofar as he is human'.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage refutes three objections against the validity of qiyas al-khalf (reductio ad absurdum) in syllogistic reasoning. The author presents each objection and then provides a systematic rebuttal. 

The first objection [B] claims that the reductio does not entail the contradictory of the conclusion alone, but rather entails it together with the true premise. Therefore, the falsity of the composite (contradictory + true premise) does not imply the falsity of each part individually; one part could be true while the composite is false, using the example of 'every human is breathing' and 'every human is inhaling' which are both true individually but false when conjoined due to the temporal condition. The author's answer [A] clarifies that the entailment from the two premises is indeed a reductio in reality: if the premises are true, they entail a contradiction in reality, which means the premises cannot be true. The composite of two true propositions is necessarily true, so the falsehood of the composite can only arise from the falsehood of the contradictory, which is the desired result. The objection about the conjunction of the two true propositions is rejected because each proposition is true conditionally at some time, and so is the conjunction. 

The second objection [T] argues that in some forms, the falsehood of the conclusion does not require the falsehood of both premises, citing an example: 'Every actual writer is a human, nothing of the human is perpetually moving insofar as he is human, so nothing of the actual writer is perpetually moving insofar as he is an actual writer' – a syllogism that yields a false conclusion but whose premises are not both false. The answer [B] repeats the same logic: the falsehood of the conclusion implies the falsehood of the composite of premises, even if not each individual premise. The example is erroneous because the conclusion 'nothing of the actual writer is perpetually moving insofar as he is an actual writer' does not follow; rather, the correct conclusion is 'nothing of the actual writer is perpetually moving insofar as he is human'. Thus, the objection fails. 

The third objection [Th] is similar and also refuted by the same principle. The method of refutation consistently relies on the concept that falsehood of the conclusion necessitates falsehood of the composite of premises, and through that, the desired contradictory is proven. The author uses logical precision to defend qiyas al-khalf as a valid and useful argument form.

Key Terms
قياس الخلف
النقيض
المقدمتين
المجموع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first objection, why does the objector claim that the falsity of the composite does not require the falsity of each part?
A) Because the composite is necessarily true if both parts are true.
B) Because one part could be true while the other is false.
C) Because the composite includes a temporal condition.
D) Because the composite is a disjunction, not a conjunction.

Key Takeaway
Qiyas al-khalf is valid because the falsity of the conclusion directly implies the falsity of the composite of premises, which requires the falsity of the assumed contradictory, thus proving the desired conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the three objections against qiyas al-khalf as presented in this passage. For each objection, state in your own words what logical point the objector is making.
?
Now take the author's third refutation (the answer to the third objection) and rewrite it as a clear, step-by-step logical argument. Why does the author say the example is an error?
