Reductio and Modality: Why It Only Proves Truth, Not Necessity
Author argues that reductio does not give the determinate modality of the conclusion, only its truth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the difference between proving a proposition's truth and proving its modality through reductio, and evaluate when a reductio argument is sufficient for its intended conclusion.

Core Idea
Reductio yields truth, not modality—be careful what you refute.

In this passage, the author tackles a subtle epistemological question: does reductio (qiyas al-khalf) give us the modal force of the conclusion, or only its truth? Drawing on the distinction between the proposition actually refuted and the intended conclusion, he argues that reductio only establishes truth, and even then only if the conclusion's terms are fixed. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and its implications for modal logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's main claim
Find the sentence in the translation where he states 'The truth is that it does not yield it; rather it yields the truth of the conclusion...'. What is he saying reductio does NOT give?
2
Understand why modality is not given
The author says that even if reductio refutes the contradictory of a proposition with the same subject and predicate, it does not follow that this proposition is exactly the conclusion. He introduces the concept of 'more general' (a‘amm). Why might the refuted proposition be broader?
The refuted proposition could entail the conclusion, but not be identical to it.
3
Consider the hypothetical example
The author mentions a 'conventional' conclusion (عرفية). What scenario does this illustrate? Why might the reductio have refuted a different proposition than the intended conclusion?
4
Apply the lesson to a real argument
Think of a common reductio in Kalam, e.g., the proof that an infinite regress is impossible. Does that argument prove the necessary truth of a First Cause, or only that an infinite regress is false? How does the author's point affect the strength of such arguments?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عرفت هذا فنقول إن هذا القياس وهو قياس الخلف من جملة ما فيه من الكلام أنه هل يكون مفيدًا جهة النتيجة على التعيين أم لا . والحق أنه لا يفيد بل يفيد صدق النتيجة إذا كانت النتيجة معينة في القياس؛ وذلك لأنه وإن قام على إبطال نقيض قضية موضوعها الأصغر ومحمولها الأكبر فلا يلزم أن يكون المركب من ذلك الموضوع، ومن ذلك المحمول نتيجة القياس الذي مرَّ ذكره. وكيف وقد كان من الممكن أن تكون النتيجة هي التي يلزمها هذا المركب حتى يكون هذا المركَّب أعم من النتيجة، وقد قام الخلف على إبطال نقائض القضايا التي ليس شيء منها نتيجة القياس الذي مرَّ ذكره كما إذا قدرنا النتيجة مثلا عرفية
When you have known this, we say: This syllogism, namely the reductio syllogism, is among matters about which there is discussion: whether it yields the modality of the conclusion determinately or not. And the truth is that it does not yield it; rather it yields the truth of the conclusion if the conclusion is determinate in the syllogism. That is because even if it is based on refuting the contradictory of a proposition whose subject is the minor term and predicate the major term, it does not necessarily follow that the composite of that subject and that predicate is the conclusion of the syllogism that was mentioned. Indeed, it is possible that the conclusion is that which this composite entails, so that this composite is more general than the conclusion, and the reductio has been based on refuting the contradictories of propositions none of which is the conclusion of the mentioned syllogism, as if we suppose for example the conclusion is conventional...
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage addresses an epistemological issue regarding the reductio syllogism: does it provide the determinate modality (جهة النتيجة) of the conclusion, such as necessity or contingency, or does it only establish the truth of the conclusion? The author states the correct view (الحق) is that reductio does not give the modality; it only gives the truth (صدق) of the conclusion, provided the conclusion is already determinate in the syllogism (i.e., its terms are fixed). The reasoning is that even if reductio refutes the contradictory of a proposition composed of the same subject and predicate as the desired conclusion, that proposition might not be exactly the conclusion but something more general (أعم) that entails the conclusion. In such a case, the reductio has actually refuted the contradictories of other propositions, not the specific conclusion. This is a subtle point about the logical form of reductio: the argument must be carefully set up so that the proposition refuted is indeed the contradictory of the intended conclusion; otherwise, the reductio only proves some other proposition that is logically related. The author hints that if the conclusion is 'conventional' (عرفية), the problem becomes more apparent. This passage is characteristic of advanced logical commentary that scrutinizes the epistemic value of proof methods.

أعم

Reductio ad absurdum establishes truth, not modal strength; the proposition refuted may be more general than the intended conclusion.

Reducing Qiyas al-Khalf to the First Figure
Author explains reduction of qiyas al-khalf to first figure by treating the contradictory as a premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how the contradictory of a particular negative can serve as minor or major in a qiyas al-khalf and reduce it to a first-figure direct syllogism.

Core Idea
Qiyas al-khalf and direct syllogism are mutually reducible via careful term placement.

The passage begins by detailing how the contradictory of a particular negative proposition can be positioned as the minor or major premise. By applying the reduction rules, any qiyas al-khalf can be transformed into a direct syllogism in the first figure. This demonstrates the deep unity between the two syllogistic forms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition to be proven
Suppose the intended conclusion is a particular negative, e.g., 'Some S is not P'. Its contradictory is 'All S are P'.
The contradictory will be used as one premise in the qiyas al-khalf.
2
Decide whether the contradictory serves as minor or major
The author says the contradictory can be either the minor or the major. If it is the minor, the major must be universal affirmative. If it is the major, the minor must be particular affirmative.
This choice affects the resulting direct syllogism.
3
Apply the reduction to the first figure
When reducing, the syllogism always returns to the first figure. For example, if contradictory is minor (universal affirmative) and major is universal affirmative, the first figure yields a universal affirmative conclusion, which then converts to the desired particular negative.
Conversion rules are used after the direct syllogism.
4
Recognize the mutual reducibility
The author concludes that qiyas al-khalf and qiyas al-mustaqim are mutually reducible; one can always be transformed into the other.
This shows their logical equivalence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصغرى، ويمكن أن يكون نقيضه الكبرى كذلك فإن كان هو الصغرى يجب أن تكون كبراه موجبة كلية، وإن كان هو الكبرى يجب أن تكون صغراه موجبة جزئية. وعند الرد إلى الاستقامة كيف كان يرجع إلى الشكل الأول، وينتج ما ينعكس إلى المطلوب. وأما السالب الجزئي فنقيضه يمكن أن يكون هو الصغرى، ويمكن أن يكون هو الكبرى أيضًا : فإن كان هو الصغرى يمكن أن تكون كبراه موجبة كلية، ويمكن أن تكون موجبة جزئية. وكذلك يمكن أن تكون سالبة كلية. وعند الرد إلى الاستقامة يرجع إلى هذا الشكل بعينه، وينتج المطلوب إن كانت كبراه موجبة، وينتج السالبة الكلية المستلزمة للسالبة الجزئية التي هو المطلوب إن كانت سالبة كلية. وإن كان هو الكبرى يمكن أن تكون صغراه موجبة كلية أو سالبة كلية يرجع إلى هذا الشكل عند الرد إلى الاستقامة، وإلى الشكل الأول أيضًا لكن ينتج سالبة كلية تنعكس إلى نفسها في الكمية المستلزمة للمطلوب. وإن كانت سالبة كلية فعند الرد يرجع أيضًا إلى هذا الشكل، وينتج المطلوب بعينه فنعلم أن قياس الخلف وقياس المستقيم يشتركان في أن كل واحد منهما يرجع إلى الآخر؛ إذا عرفت هذا فنقول إن هذا القياس وهو قياس الخلف من جملة ما فيه من الكلام أنه هل يكون مفيدًا جهة النتيجة على التعيين أم لا . والحق أنه لا يفيد بل يفيد صدق النتيجة إذا كانت النتيجة معينة في القياس؛ وذلك لأنه وإن قام على إبطال نقيض قضية موضوعها الأصغر ومحمولها الأكبر فلا يلزم أن يكون المركب من ذلك الموضوع، ومن ذلك المحمول نتيجة القياس الذي مرَّ ذكره. وكيف وقد كان من الممكن أن تكون النتيجة هي التي يلزمها هذا المركب حتى يكون هذا المركَّب أعم من النتيجة، وقد قام الخلف على إبطال نقائض القضايا التي ليس شيء منها نتيجة القياس الذي مرَّ ذكره كما إذا قدرنا النتيجة مثلا عرفية خاصة، وأقمنا الدليل على صدقها وهو قياس الخلف فمتى صدقت هي صدقت العامة منها وهي العرفية العامة لاستلزام صدق الخاص صدق العام من غير عكس، ومتى صدقت العرفية العامة صدقت العامة منها وهي المطلقة العامة. وكذلك العامة من المطلقة العامة وهي الممكنة العامة. والخلف كما يساعد على إبطال ذلك النقيض فإنه يساعد على إبطال نقائض هذه القضايا بأسرها لا محالة : فإذا قام على إبطال نقيض الممكنة العامة أو المطلقة العامة أو العرفية العامة لم يلزم منه أن تكون النتيجة ممكنة عامة للقياس الذي مرَّ ذكره. وكذلك لا يلزم أن تكون مطلقة عامة أو عرفية عامة بل يمكن أن تكون ما هو الأخص منها. ولا يلزم من صدق العام صدق الخاص فلا يلزم من صدق هذه القضية أي قضية كانت من القضايا المذكورة صدق النتيجة. ولو كان كذلك فالخلف متى ساعد على إبطال نقيض قضية يمكن أن تكون تلك القضية نتيجة القياس الذي مرَّ ذكره فيمكن أن لا تكون بل قضية هي أخص منها وحينئذ يلزم أن لا يفيد قياس الخلف جهة النتيجة على التعيين بل إذا تعين أن نتيجة هذا القياس هي هذه القضية، فإنه يدل على صدقها بالطريق الذي سبق ذكره.
the minor premise, and it is possible that its contradictory is the major as well. If it is the minor, its major must be universal affirmative; if it is the major, its minor must be particular affirmative. And when reducing to the direct [syllogism], whichever way, it returns to the first figure, and produces a conclusion that converts to the desired [conclusion]. As for the particular negative, its contradictory can be the minor, and can also be the major. If it is the minor, its major can be universal affirmative, or particular affirmative, and also can be universal negative. And when reducing to the direct [syllogism], it returns to this same figure, and produces the desired [conclusion] if its major is affirmative, and produces the universal negative which entails the particular negative that is the desired [conclusion] if its major is universal negative. And if it is the major, its minor can be universal affirmative or universal negative; it returns to this figure when reducing to the direct [syllogism], and also to the first figure, but it produces a universal negative which converts to itself in quantity entailing the desired [conclusion]. And if it is universal negative, then when reducing it also returns to this figure, and produces the desired conclusion exactly. So we know that qiyas al-khalf and the direct syllogism share that each one of them reduces to the other. If you know this, we say that this syllogism—that is, qiyas al-khalf—among the matters discussed about it is whether it gives the modality of the conclusion determinately or not. The truth is that it does not give it, rather it gives the truth of the conclusion if the conclusion is determinate in the syllogism. This is because even if it is based on refuting the contradictory of a proposition whose subject is the minor term and predicate the major term, it does not follow that the compound of that subject and that predicate is the conclusion of the syllogism mentioned. How so? Since it is possible that the conclusion is that which is entailed by this compound so that this compound is more general than the conclusion, and qiyas al-khalf has been based on refuting the contradictories of propositions none of which is the conclusion of the syllogism mentioned. For example, if we suppose the conclusion is, say, a particular 'urfiyya, and we establish the proof of its truth—that is, qiyas al-khalf—then when it is true, the general one among them is true, which is the general 'urfiyya, because the truth of the particular entails the truth of the general, without converse. And when the general 'urfiyya is true, the general one among them—the general mutlaqa—is true. Similarly the general of the general mutlaqa, which is the general mumkina. And al-khalf, just as it supports refuting that contradictory, it necessarily supports refuting the contradictories of all these propositions. So if it is based on refuting the contradictory of the general mumkina, or the general mutlaqa, or the general 'urfiyya, it does not follow from that that the conclusion is a general mumkina for the syllogism mentioned. Similarly it is not necessary that it be a general mutlaqa or general 'urfiyya; rather it can be what is more specific than them. And the truth of the general does not entail the truth of the particular, so the truth of this proposition—whichever of the propositions mentioned—does not entail the truth of the conclusion. And if that were the case, then when al-khalf supports refuting the contradictory of a proposition that could be the conclusion of the syllogism mentioned, it is possible that it is not, but rather a proposition more specific than it. And then it follows that qiyas al-khalf does not benefit the modality of the conclusion determinately. Rather, if it is determined that the conclusion of this syllogism is this proposition, then it indicates its truth by the method previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of qiyas al-khalf (reductio ad absurdum) and its relationship with qiyas al-mustaqim (direct syllogism). The author begins by examining cases where the contradictory of a premise can serve as the minor or major, and how reduction to the first figure works. He then asserts that qiyas al-khalf and direct syllogism are mutually reducible. The main point is whether qiyas al-khalf determines the modality of the conclusion (e.g., necessary, absolute, possible). The author argues that it does not; it only proves the truth of a determinate conclusion, not its modal strength. He illustrates this by considering a conclusion of a specific 'urfiyya (customary) proposition: the reductio might refute the contradictory of a more general proposition, but the actual conclusion could be a specific one, and truth of the general does not imply truth of the specific. Therefore, qiyas al-khalf cannot uniquely fix the modality.

Key Terms

قياس المستقيم
السالب الجزئي

D) Particular negative

Key Takeaway
Qiyas al-khalf can always be transformed into a direct first-figure syllogism, demonstrating their logical equivalence.

Why Qiyas al-Khalf Does Not Determine Modality
Author examines whether qiyas al-khalf yields determinate modal conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why qiyas al-khalf does not determine the modality of the conclusion and identify the fallacy of assuming it does.

Core Idea
Qiyas al-khalf proves truth, not modal strength.

After establishing the reducibility, the author turns to a subtle point: does qiyas al-khalf specify the modality of the conclusion? He argues that it does not; it only confirms the truth of a statement if that statement is already assumed to be the conclusion. The reductio can refute the contradictory of a general proposition while the actual conclusion is more specific, leading to modal ambiguity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem of modality
The author asks whether qiyas al-khalf yields the modality of the conclusion determinately (e.g., does it prove the conclusion is necessary, absolute, or possible?).
Modality is a key topic in Islamic logic, especially in demonstration (burhan).
2
Recognize that the reductio refutes a contradictory of some proposition
The reductio refutes the contradictory of a proposition P. But P may not be the conclusion itself; it could be a more general proposition that entails the conclusion.
For example, P might be a general customary proposition, while the conclusion is a specific one.
3
Analyze the hierarchy of propositions
The author gives an example: a particular 'urfiyya (customary) proposition entails a general 'urfiyya, which entails a general mutlaqa, which entails a general mumkina. The reductio might refute the contradictory of any of these, but the conclusion could be the most specific.
Truth of the general does not imply truth of the specific.
4
Conclude the indeterminacy
Therefore, qiyas al-khalf does not uniquely determine the modality of the conclusion. It only proves the truth of the conclusion if the conclusion is already specified.
The reductio is a truth-preserving method, not a modal specification tool.

الصغرى، ويمكن أن يكون نقيضه الكبرى كذلك فإن كان هو الصغرى يجب أن تكون كبراه موجبة كلية، وإن كان هو الكبرى يجب أن تكون صغراه موجبة جزئية. وعند الرد إلى الاستقامة كيف كان يرجع إلى الشكل الأول، وينتج ما ينعكس إلى المطلوب. وأما السالب الجزئي فنقيضه يمكن أن يكون هو الصغرى، ويمكن أن يكون هو الكبرى أيضًا : فإن كان هو الصغرى يمكن أن تكون كبراه موجبة كلية، ويمكن أن تكون موجبة جزئية. وكذلك يمكن أن تكون سالبة كلية. وعند الرد إلى الاستقامة يرجع إلى هذا الشكل بعينه، وينتج المطلوب إن كانت كبراه موجبة، وينتج السالبة الكلية المستلزمة للسالبة الجزئية التي هو المطلوب إن كانت سالبة كلية. وإن كان هو الكبرى يمكن أن تكون صغراه موجبة كلية أو سالبة كلية يرجع إلى هذا الشكل عند الرد إلى الاستقامة، وإلى الشكل الأول أيضًا لكن ينتج سالبة كلية تنعكس إلى نفسها في الكمية المستلزمة للمطلوب. وإن كانت سالبة كلية فعند الرد يرجع أيضًا إلى هذا الشكل، وينتج المطلوب بعينه فنعلم أن قياس الخلف وقياس المستقيم يشتركان في أن كل واحد منهما يرجع إلى الآخر؛ إذا عرفت هذا فنقول إن هذا القياس وهو قياس الخلف من جملة ما فيه من الكلام أنه هل يكون مفيدًا جهة النتيجة على التعيين أم لا . والحق أنه لا يفيد بل يفيد صدق النتيجة إذا كانت النتيجة معينة في القياس؛ وذلك لأنه وإن قام على إبطال نقيض قضية موضوعها الأصغر ومحمولها الأكبر الخلف على إبطال نقائض القضايا التي ليس شيء منها نتيجة القياس الذي مرَّ ذكره كما إذا قدرنا النتيجة مثلا عرفية خاصة، وأقمنا الدليل على صدقها وهو قياس الخلف فمتى صدقت هي صدقت العامة منها وهي العرفية العامة لاستلزام صدق الخاص صدق العام من غير عكس، ومتى صدقت العرفية العامة صدقت العامة منها وهي المطلقة العامة. وكذلك العامة من المطلقة العامة وهي الممكنة العامة. والخلف كما يساعد على إبطال ذلك النقيض Necessity of Universal Affirmative in Syllogism
Author's argument that syllogisms require a universal affirmative premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why every valid syllogism requires a universal affirmative premise.

Core Idea
No syllogism is complete without a universal affirmative premise.

This passage asserts a fundamental condition for any valid syllogism: it must contain a universal affirmative premise. The author justifies this by analyzing how a conclusion can be proven: either through entailment (luzum) or opposition (inad), or through a simple term. In each case, a universal affirmative is necessary for the conditional proof (istithna') that links the terms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Read the opening sentence: 'As for the syllogism, it is not complete except by containing the meaning of universality and affirmation.' This is the central thesis.
2
Analyze the two modes of proof
The author says the desired conclusion is either shown as a necessary consequent (lazim) of something or as opposite (mu'anid) to it. In both cases, the proof proceeds via conditional proof (istithna').
When the conclusion is entailed by something, its contradictory is equivalent to the necessary consequent, leading to a conditional syllogism.
3
Consider the case without entailment or opposition
If the conclusion is not proven through entailment or opposition, the thing from which it is proven is either a compound or a simple term. If simple, positing or removing it yields the conclusion, again via conditional proof.
The text breaks off here, but the underlying logic is that all valid syllogisms reduce to conditional proofs requiring a universal affirmative major premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في أن القياس لا يتم إلا بتضمنه معنى الكلية والإيجاب] أما القياس فإنه لا يتم إلا بتضمنه معنى الكلية والإيجاب؛ وذلك لأن المطلوب إما يتبين على سبيل أنه لازم عن شيء أو معاند له، فيكون نقيضه في قوة اللازم فيكون سبيل بيانه سبيل الاستثناء، فإن تبين عن شيء لا على سبيل اللزوم أو العناد له فلا يخلو من أن يكون ذلك الشيء مركبا تركيبا جزئيا أو لا يكون بل لا جزء فيه فهو في حكم المفرد يلزم عنه شيء كما يلزم عن مفرد لوضعه أو رفعه؛ فيكون على سبيل الاستثناء أيضًا، فإن كان مركبا
Section: [On that the syllogism is not complete except by containing the meaning of universality and affirmation.] As for the syllogism, it is not complete except by containing the meaning of universality and affirmation; that is because the desired [conclusion] is either made evident as something entailed by something or opposed to it, so its contradictory is in the position of the necessary, so its method of making evident is the method of conditional proof (istithnā'). If it is made evident by something not by way of entailment or opposition, then that thing is either a compound with partial composition or not, and if it has no part, it is in the ruling of a simple term; something follows from it as from a simple term by its positing or removal, so it is also by way of conditional proof. If it is compound...
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage opens a new section stating a fundamental condition for any syllogism: it must contain a universal affirmative proposition. The author argues that the desired conclusion is either shown to be a necessary consequence of something (luzum) or opposed to it (inad). In the first case, the contradictory of the original proposition is in the position of a necessary consequent, so the proof proceeds by conditional proof (istithna'). Alternatively, if the conclusion is not shown through entailment or opposition, the thing from which it is made evident is either a compound or a simple. If it is simple (like a single term), then the relation is also like the conditional proof because positing or removing that term yields the conclusion. The passage breaks off as it begins discussing the case of a compound premise. The key idea is that every syllogism must involve some universal affirmative statement because the conditional proof requires a universal major premise linking the middle term to the predicate.

Reductio Does Not Determine Modality
Author explains the application of khalaf to modal propositions and resulting modality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a reductio proof does not directly determine the modality of the conclusion, and identify asymmetrical relationships between particular and universal modal propositions.

Core Idea
Refuting a universal contradictory does not force the conclusion to be universal—it may be more specific.

Reductio ad absurdum (qiyas al-khalf) is a powerful proof method, but it has subtle limitations when applied to modal propositions. In this lesson, we examine how the truth of a particular (e.g., a specific modal claim) implies the truth of a more general modal proposition, but not vice versa. This asymmetry means that a successful reductio refuting a universal contradictory cannot guarantee that the conclusion matches that universal modality. Understanding this nuance prevents overconfidence in modal proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modal hierarchy
The passage introduces a hierarchy: particular (خاص) → universal conventional (عرفية عامة) → universal absolute (مطلقة عامة) → universal possible (ممكنة عامة). Note that the particular is the most specific and the universal possible the most general.
This hierarchy is one-way: truth flows from particular to general, but not back.
2
Trace the implication relation
Understand that truth of the particular entails truth of the universal conventional, which entails the universal absolute, which entails the universal possible. This is a chain of implications, but the converse does not hold.
3
Apply reductio to a modal contradictory
Suppose we use reductio to refute the contradictory of a universal possible. The passage cautions that the conclusion (the proposition we prove) may not itself be universal possible; it could be a more specific proposition (e.g., universal absolute or conventional).
The refutation only shows that the contradictory is false, not that the proven proposition has the same modality as the refuted one.
4
Explain why the reductio does not fix modality
Because truth of a general universal does not entail truth of a particular (special case), proving a general proposition does not prove the specific one needed as a conclusion. The reductio may establish a proposition that is actually a stronger (more specific) claim than what was initially targeted.

خاصة، وأقمنا الدليل على صدقها وهو قياس الخلف فمتى صدقت هي صدقت العامة منها وهي العرفية العامة لاستلزام صدق الخاص صدق العام من غير عكس، ومتى صدقت العرفية العامة صدقت العامة منها وهي المطلقة العامة. وكذلك العامة من المطلقة العامة وهي الممكنة العامة. والخلف كما يساعد على إبطال ذلك النقيض فإنه يساعد على إبطال نقائض هذه القضايا بأسرها لا محالة : فإذا قام على إبطال نقيض الممكنة العامة أو المطلقة العامة أو العرفية العامة لم يلزم منه أن تكون النتيجة ممكنة عامة للقياس الذي مرَّ ذكره. وكذلك لا يلزم أن تكون مطلقة عامة أو عرفية عامة بل يمكن أن تكون ما هو الأخص منها. ولا يلزم من صدق العام صدق الخاص فلا يلزم من صدق هذه القضية أي قضية كانت من القضايا المذكورة صدق النتيجة. ولو كان كذلك فالخلف متى ساعد على إبطال نقيض قضية يمكن أن تكون تلك القضية نتيجة القياس الذي مرَّ ذكره فيمكن أن لا تكون بل قضية هي أخص منها وحينئذ يلزم أن لا يفيد قياس الخلف جهة النتيجة على التعيين بل إذا تعين أن نتيجة هذا القياس هي هذه القضية، فإنه يدل على صدقها بالطريق الذي سبق ذكره.
Specifically, we have provided proof of its truth by means of the reductio. Whenever the particular is true, the universal is true, namely the universal conventional, because the truth of the particular entails the truth of the universal without the converse. And whenever the universal conventional is true, the universal absolute is true. Similarly, the universal from the universal absolute is the universal possible. And the reductio, just as it helps in refuting that contradictory, so it inevitably helps in refuting the contradictories of all these propositions. If it is used to refute the contradictory of the universal possible or the universal absolute or the universal conventional, it does not necessitate that the conclusion is a universal possible for the syllogism mentioned earlier. Similarly, it does not necessitate that it be universal absolute or universal conventional, but rather it can be more specific than them. And the truth of the universal does not entail the truth of the particular, so the truth of any of these propositions does not entail the truth of the conclusion. And if that were the case, then whenever the reductio helps in refuting the contradictory of a proposition, that proposition could be the conclusion of the syllogism mentioned earlier, but it is possible that it is not, but rather a proposition more specific than it. Then it would follow that the reductio argument does not determine the modality of the conclusion with specificity; rather, if it is determined that the conclusion of this syllogism is indeed this proposition, then it indicates its truth by the method already mentioned.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage discusses the implications of using reductio ad absurdum (qiyas al-khalf) in modal logic. The author explains that when we use reductio to prove a proposition, the truth of the particular (e.g., a specific modal claim) implies the truth of a more general proposition (e.g., 'urfiyyah 'ammah, then mutlaqah 'ammah, then mumkinah 'ammah). However, the converse does not hold. The key point is that the reductio method does not guarantee that the conclusion will have the same modality as the refuted contradictory. For instance, if we refute the contradictory of a universal possible, the conclusion need not be universal possible; it could be a more specific proposition. The author stresses that the truth of a general proposition does not entail the truth of a particular one, so the reductio does not determine the exact modality of the conclusion. The method merely shows that the conclusion is true if the reductio succeeds, but its modality may be narrower than expected. This is an important caution against overgeneralizing the results of reductio proofs.

العرفية العامة
المطلقة العامة
الممكنة العامة

C) It can be more specific than the universal possible.
D) It must be a particular proposition.

Key Takeaway
A reductio proof does not automatically impose a universal modality on the conclusion; the conclusion may be a more specific proposition, so one must not overgeneralize the result.

The Syllogism
advanced
8 min
The Universal Affirmative Core of Every Syllogism
Author proves that every syllogism must involve a universal affirmative sense and explains relational types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why every valid syllogism requires a universal affirmative relational core and distinguish between necessary consequence and predication relations.

Core Idea
Every syllogism relies on a universal affirmative relation to link subject and predicate.

Syllogisms are the engine of logical proof in Islamic sciences, from kalam to fiqh. But what exactly makes a syllogism work? This passage dives deep into the foundational requirement: a syllogism is only valid if it contains the meaning of universality (kulliyyah) and affirmation (ijab). The author systematically shows that all syllogisms reduce to a conditional structure where a predication relation binds the terms together necessarily.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Thesis
Locate the opening claim: the syllogism is not complete except by including universality and affirmation. This is the central assertion the author defends.
The thesis is stated at the very beginning of the passage.
2
Analyze the Two Cases of Establishing a Conclusion
The author considers whether a conclusion is established as a necessary consequent of something or opposed to it. If so, it falls under conditional syllogism (istithna'i). If not, the establishing thing is either simple or compound.
This breakdown leads to the recognition that all proof paths involve some form of conditional reasoning.
3
Distinguish the Two Relational Types
The author contrasts two kinds of relations between intelligible meanings: mere necessary consequence (luzum) and predication/positing (haml wa wad'). Only the latter provides a relation that links subject and predicate directly.
Mere consequence without predication cannot establish the needed connection between the terms.
4
Understand Why Predication Must Involve Both Terms
The relation must be with both the subject and the predicate. If it only involves one term, no connection between the two arises. This ensures the syllogism's conditional nature.
The author says the relation must be 'with both terms together' (مع الطرفين جميعا).
5
Apply the Conclusion to a Familiar Syllogism
Consider the classic kalam proof: 'Everything temporally originated has a cause; the world is temporally originated; therefore, the world has a cause.' The major premise is universal affirmative, providing the necessary predication relation that links 'world' and 'has a cause'.
This demonstrates why universality and affirmation are indispensable for a valid syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في أن القياس لا يتم إلا بتضمنه معنى الكلية والإيجاب] أما القياس فإنه لا يتم إلا بتضمنه معنى الكلية والإيجاب؛ وذلك لأن المطلوب إما يتبين على سبيل أنه لازم عن شيء أو معاند له، فيكون نقيضه في قوة اللازم فيكون سبيل بيانه سبيل الاستثناء، فإن تبين عن شيء لا على سبيل اللزوم أو العناد له فلا يخلو من أن يكون ذلك الشيء مركبا تركيبا جزئيا أو لا يكون بل لا جزء فيه فهو في حكم المفرد يلزم عنه شيء كما يلزم عن مفرد لوضعه أو رفعه؛ فيكون على سبيل الاستثناء أيضًا، فإن كان مركبا تركيباً جزئياً ويلزم عنه شيء لا على سبيل وضع واستثناء فلا بد وأن يكون للذي يتبين به نسبة أجزاء إلى هذا الشيء. ولا يمكن أن تكون تلك النسبة إلا على أحد الوجهين : إما نسبته إلى جملة هذا القول لا إلى أجزائه ويكون إذا عقلت تلك النسبة ووضع ذلك الشيء لزم هو سواء كان ذلك الوضع إيجابًا أو سلباً أو إلزاما أو رفعا بعد أن يجعل حكمًا. وهذا القسم أيضًا من القياس الاستثنائي وإما نسبته إلى أجزاء هذا القول المطلوب واحدًا فواحدًا؛ وذلك لأن النسبة التي لذلك الشيء إما أن تكون إلى الجملة أو إلى أجزاء الجملة: فإنه إذا لم تكن نسبة ذلك الشيء إلى جملة هذا القول ولا إلى أجزائه حتى تكون تلك النسبة توجب جميع الأجزاء. ثمّ يجب أن يتصل في الذهن أحد الحكمين بالآخر أعني المطلوب بما يطلب به. وإن كان الشيء إذا حضر في الذهن لزم أن يحضر في الذهن شيء آخر فتبين أن بينهما علاقة ما. وكل علاقة بين معنيين معقولين إما أن تكون علاقة لزوم ليس على ما يكون بحمل ووضع، وإما أن تكون على سبيل حمل ووضع؛ فإن كان الثالث الذي له نسبة إلى أجزاء المطلوب فيتبين به المطلوب إنما نسبته إلى أجزاء المطلوب على سبيل نسبة التلازم من غير حمل ووضع فإنما يحضر منه في الذهن إما أن يدل على وجود الموضوع أو المحمول أو هما جميعا دون الدلالة على سبيل النسبة التي بينهما فيجب إذن أن تكون العلاقة فيما نحن فيه علاقة حمل ووضع. ثم يجب أن تكون هذه العلاقة مع الطرفين جميعا. فإنه إذا كان مع أحد الطرفين فقط لا يوجب وجوده لطرف أو عدمه لطرف لزوم علاقة ما بين الطرفين؛ فالقياس شرطي على ما عرف. فإذن يجب أن تكون العلاقة مع الطرفين معا وتكون تلك العلاقة إذا عقلت له معهما صار الحمل أيضًا كالملزوم والمطلوب كاللازم.
Chapter: On the fact that the syllogism is not complete except by including the meaning of universality and affirmation. As for the syllogism, it is not complete except by including the meaning of universality and affirmation. That is because the desired conclusion is either established as a necessary consequent of something or as opposed to it; then its contradictory is in the position of the consequent, so its method of demonstration is the method of the conditional syllogism. If it is established from something not by way of consequence or opposition, then that thing must be either a compound of partial composition or not; if it has no parts, it is like a simple term, and something follows from it just as something follows from a simple term by positing or removing it, so it is also by way of conditional syllogism. If it is a compound of partial composition and something follows from it not by way of positing and conditional, then it is necessary that there be a relation of parts to this thing. That relation can only be in one of two ways: either its relation to the whole proposition not to its parts, and when that relation is understood and that thing is posited, it follows, whether that positing is affirmative or negative, or imposition or removal after it has been made a judgment. This kind is also from the conditional syllogism. Or its relation to the parts of this desired proposition one by one. This is because the relation of that thing is either to the whole or to the parts of the proposition: for if the relation of that thing is not to the whole proposition nor to its parts such that that relation necessitates all the parts, then it is necessary that one of the two judgments connect in the mind with the other, namely the desired conclusion and what is sought by it. And if a thing, when present in the mind, necessitates the presence of another thing in the mind, then it becomes clear that there is some relation between them. Every relation between two intelligible meanings is either a relation of necessary consequence not by way of predication and positing, or it is by way of predication and positing. If the third term which has a relation to the parts of the desired conclusion is such that its relation to the parts of the desired conclusion is a relation of mutual consequence without predication and positing, then what is present from it in the mind is either an indication of the existence of the subject or the predicate or both, without indicating the relation between them. Therefore, the relation in our discussion must be one of predication and positing. Then this relation must be with both terms together. For if it is only with one of the two terms, its existence or non-existence for the term does not necessitate a relation between the two terms. So the syllogism is conditional as is known. Therefore, the relation must be with both terms together, and when that relation is understood for him with them, the predicate becomes like the implied consequence and the desired conclusion becomes like the consequent.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical argument about the foundational requirement of syllogism: it must include the meaning of universality (kulliyyah) and affirmation (ijab). The author begins by stating this thesis and then justifies it by analyzing how a conclusion (matlub) is established. If the conclusion is a necessary consequent of something or opposed to it, its contradictory is like a consequent, and the proof proceeds via conditional syllogism (istithna'i). If not, the establishing thing is either simple or compound. Simple things give conditional syllogism as well. For compounds, we need a relation of parts to the establishing thing. This relation is either to the whole proposition or to its parts individually. The author distinguishes two types of relations between intelligibles: one of mere necessary consequence (luzum) without predication, and one of predication and positing (haml wa wad'). For a syllogism to yield a conclusion, the relation must be of the latter type, and it must involve both the subject and predicate terms. If the relation is only with one term, it does not necessitate a connection. The author concludes that the syllogism is effectively conditional (sharti) because the relation must be a relation of predication that links the terms universally and affirmatively. This is a subtle discussion that ties the nature of syllogistic inference to the universal affirmative proposition.

الكلية
الإيجاب
الاستثنائي
اللزوم
الحمل والوضع
الشرطي

B) A relation of predication and positing (haml wa wad') involving both terms.
C) A relation of subalternation between universal and particular.
D) A relation of contradiction between affirmative and negative.

Key Takeaway
Every valid syllogism is ultimately a conditional structure where a universal affirmative predication relation links the subject and predicate necessarily.

The Syllogism

The Middle Term's Required Relation: Predication over Concomitance
Author analyzes the necessary relation of the middle term to extremes in qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the relation of predication (haml wa wad') from mere necessary concomitance (luzum) and explain why the middle term must be predicated of both extremes for a valid categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
The middle term must bind extremes via predication, not mere implication.

In this passage, the author delves into the fundamental relation that makes a syllogism (qiyas) work. He distinguishes two types of relations: one relating the middle term to the whole conclusion (as in conditional syllogisms) and one relating it to the parts (subject and predicate) individually. He argues that for a categorical syllogism, the relation must be one of predication (haml) and position (wad'), and it must involve both extremes. This insight is crucial for constructing and evaluating any categorical proof in Islamic sciences, from kalam to fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of relations
The author says the relation of a term to a statement can be either to the whole statement (conditional syllogisms) or to its parts one by one (categorical syllogisms). Find where he makes this distinction.
The phrase 'إما نسبته إلى جملة هذا القول لا إلى أجزائه' vs 'وإما نسبته إلى أجزاء هذا القول المطلوب واحدًا فواحدًا'.
2
Determine which relation is required for a categorical syllogism
He states that for our purpose (the third term in categorical qiyas), the relation must be 'على سبيل حمل ووضع' (by way of predication and position). Trace his reasoning: he rejects mere necessary concomitance without predication because that only indicates existence of subject/predicate, not the relation between them.
Look for 'فإنه إذا لم تكن نسبة ذلك الشيء إلى جملة هذا القول ... فتبين أن بينهما علاقة ما' up to 'فيجب إذن أن تكون العلاقة فيما نحن فيه علاقة حمل ووضع'.
3
Understand why the relation must involve both extremes
He argues that if the middle term relates to only one extreme, no connection between the extremes follows. The middle term must be with both extremes together. Verify: 'ثم يجب أن تكون هذه العلاقة مع الطرفين جميعا' and the following sentences.
Consider a counterexample: if 'A is B' and 'C is B' but B relates to A and C separately, we don't get 'A is C' unless B is predicated of both.
4
Classify the middle term: single or compound
He divides the third term (middle term) into single (مفرد) or not single. If compound, it may have the force of a single or require multiple relations. Note his examples with letters (أ, ب, ج, د) for agreeing and differing relations.
The examples: 'أب' and 'ج', and 'كل ما هو ب و ج معا فهو د' for agreeing; 'أب وليس ج' and 'كل ما هو ب وليس ج فهو د' for differing.
5
Apply to an Islamic science example
Take a kalam proof: 'The world is temporally originated. Everything temporally originated requires a cause. Therefore the world requires a cause.' Here 'temporally originated' is the middle term, predicated of 'world' in the minor and 'requires a cause' in the major. Check that it relates via predication and involves both extremes.
This shows the abstract principle concretely.

الأجزاء. ثمّ يجب أن يتصل في الذهن أحد الحكمين بالآخر أعني المطلوب بما يطلب به. وإن كان الشيء إذا حضر في الذهن لزم أن يحضر في الذهن شيء آخر فتبين أن بينهما علاقة ما. وكل علاقة بين معنيين معقولين إما أن ووضع فإنما يحضر منه في الذهن إما أن يدل على وجود الموضوع أو المحمول أو هما جميعا دون الدلالة على سبيل النسبة التي بينهما فيجب إذن أن تكون العلاقة فيما نحن فيه علاقة حمل ووضع. ثم يجب أن تكون هذه العلاقة مع الطرفين جميعا. فإنه إذا كان مع أحد الطرفين فقط لا يوجب وجوده لطرف أو عدمه لطرف لزوم علاقة ما بين الطرفين؛ فالقياس شرطي على ما عرف. فإذن يجب أن تكون العلاقة مع الطرفين معا وتكون تلك العلاقة إذا عقلت له معهما صار الحمل أيضًا كالملزوم والمطلوب كاللازم. والثالث هو الذي جمع الطرفين بخاصة لا محالة تلك الخاصة توجب جمع الطرفين اللذين للمطلوب. فلما كانت لها إلى هذا الطرف نسبة كذا وإلى ذلك الطرف نسبة كذا وجب أن يكون بين الطرفين نسبة كذا في أي مادة اتفقت لأن تلك الخاصة في صورة المقدّمة أعني كيفيتها وكميتها وجهتها لا في مادتها لزم دائما أن يصح اللازم فلا يحتاج إلى أن يبتدأ كل وقت بوضع شرطي واستثناء بل يقتصر على تلك الخاصة؛ وإن في الحقيقة عند النفس [٢٤٣,] شرطي واستثناء. وكان من الحملي من هذه الجهة / أيضًا ما يتم في القوة بالشرطي. وأما الأقسام التي تقع دون ذلك فلا يلزمها مثل صورة ذلك اللزوم لزوما قياسيا بل عسى مثل العكس وكذب النقيض وليس كلامنا فيه. ثمّ الثالث فيما تكون نسبته إلى أجزاء المطلوب كما مر لا يخلو من أن يكون شيئًا مفردًا لفظا ومعنى، أو غير مفرد : فإن كان غير مفرد فإما أن يكون في قوة مفرد، أو لا يكون بل يكون أجزاؤه متباينة ولا يتصل منها ما قوته قوة مفرد؛ فإن كان في قوة مفرد فحكمه حكم المفرد فإن وضع لا في قوة مفرد على أنه شيآن أو أشياء متباينة فإما أن يكون لكل واحد منها نسبة إلى كلي الطرفين أو لجملتها أو لبعضها نسبة إلى طرف ولبعضها إلى طرف آخر ؛ فإن كان لكل واحد منها نسبة إلى كلي الطرفين ويتم جمعهما بنسبة واحدة كان القياس الواحد تاما بواحد. ويكون الباقي إما فصلا وإما قياسا آخر وإن كان إنما يتم جمعهما لجميع النسب كلها فيكون حملها هو المتوسط وهو من جهة ما هو حمله کمعنی واحد مثال هذا والنسب متفقة «أب» و «ج» وكل ما هو «ب» و (ج) معا فهو «د». ومثاله والنسب مختلفة «أب» وليس «ج» وكل ما هو «ب» وليس (ج) فهو (د)، ولسنا نبين هنا شرائط الإنتاج بل أحوال هذا المتوسط؛ إذ النسب كما مرَّ. والشرائط كذلك والمقدمة من حيث هي المقدمة لا تكون إلا
A partial composition and something follows from it not by way of position and exception, so there must be for that by which a relation of parts to this thing is clarified. That relation can only be in one of two ways: either its relation to the whole of this statement and not to its parts, and when that relation is understood and that thing is posited, it follows, whether that position is affirmative, negative, necessitating, or removing after it is made a judgment. And this division is also of the conditional syllogism. Or its relation to the parts of this requested statement one by one; because the relation of that thing is either to the whole or to the parts of the whole: for if the relation of that thing is not to the whole of this statement nor to its parts such that that relation necessitates all the parts. Then one of the two judgments must be connected in the mind to the other, i.e., the requested with what it is requested by. And if when a thing is present in the mind another thing necessarily becomes present in the mind, it is shown that there is some relation between them. Every relation between two intelligible meanings is either a relation of necessary concomitance not by way of predication and position, or it is by way of predication and position. For if the third (term) has a relation to the parts of the requested and by it the requested is clarified, its relation to the parts of the requested is by way of necessary concomitance without predication and position, then from it there is present in the mind either an indication of the existence of the subject or the predicate or both without indication of the relation between them. Thus the relation in our case must be a relation of predication and position. Then this relation must be with both extremes. For if it is with only one of the two extremes, its existence for one extreme or its non-existence for the other does not necessitate a relation between the extremes. So the syllogism is conditional as known. Therefore the relation must be with both extremes together, and that relation, when understood for it with them, makes the predication also like the necessary condition and the requested like the necessary consequent. The third (term) is that which combines both extremes necessarily; that specific property necessitates the combination of the two extremes of the requested. Since it has such a relation to this extreme and such a relation to that extreme, there must be such a relation between the two extremes in any matter, because that specific property is in the form of the premise – i.e., its quality, quantity, and modality – not in its matter; so the consequent always holds. There is no need to begin each time with a conditional and exception, but one suffices with that specific property. And in reality, in the mind, it is [٢٤٣,] conditional and exception. And from a categorical syllogism from this aspect, sometimes it is completed in potency by the conditional. As for the divisions that fall short of this, they do not necessitate that form of that consequent as a syllogistic consequent, but perhaps like conversion and falsity of the contradictory, and our discussion is not about them. Then the third, regarding what its relation to the parts of the requested is as before: it is either a single thing in word and meaning, or not single. If it is not single, it is either in potency of a single, or not, but rather its parts are disparate and none of them has the potency of a single. If it is in potency of a single, its ruling is the ruling of a single. If it is posited not in potency of a single, as two or disparate things, then either each one has a relation to the whole of both extremes, or their total has, or some of them have a relation to one extreme and others to the other extreme. If each one has a relation to the whole of both extremes and their combination is completed by one relation, then the single syllogism is complete by one. And the remainder is either a division or another syllogism. And if their combination is completed only by all the relations together, then their predication is the middle term, and from the aspect of its being a predication it is like a single meaning. An example of this with agreeing relations: 'ab' and 'j', and everything that is 'b' and 'j' together is 'd'. And an example with differing relations: 'ab' and not 'j', and everything that is 'b' and not 'j' is 'd'. And we are not explaining here the conditions of production but the states of this middle term; for the relations are as before. And the conditions are likewise, and the premise as such is not except...
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage is an advanced analysis of the syllogism (qiyas), focusing on the nature of the middle term and the required relation it must have with the extremes (subject and predicate of the conclusion). The author begins by distinguishing two types of relations: either the middle term relates to the whole conclusion (as in conditional syllogisms where the relation is between propositions) or to its parts (the subject and predicate directly). He argues that for a valid categorical syllogism, the relation must be one of predication (haml wa wad'), meaning the middle term is predicated of each extreme in a premise. He then emphasizes that this predication relation must involve both extremes simultaneously; otherwise, no necessary connection is established. The author further refines this by discussing the middle term itself: it can be a single term or a compound. If compound, it may function as a single term in potency or require multiple relations. He gives abstract examples using letters (أ, ب, ج, د) to illustrate cases where the middle term combines the extremes through agreeing or differing relations. The passage concludes that a full discussion of production conditions is not intended here, only the states of the middle term.

C) Any relation that connects the two premises, regardless of type.

Key Takeaway
A valid categorical syllogism requires the middle term to be predicated of both extremes, not merely associated via concomitance.

The Third Type of Middle Term: Necessitation via Specific Property
Author defines the third type of middle term via specific property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the third type of middle term and explain how its specific property (khāṣṣa) necessitates a conclusion without repeating a conditional.

Core Idea
The third middle term's specific property (form) makes syllogistic entailment automatic, independent of matter.

This passage defines the third type of middle term (mutawassit) in syllogistic theory. The author explains that a specific property (khāṣṣa) belonging to the form of the premise—its quality, quantity, and modality—guarantees the conclusion without the need to reiterate a conditional and its exception each time. In reality, the entailment is implicitly conditional in the mind. We will unpack how this works and why it differs from weaker inferences like conversion (ʿaks).

Guided Path
1
Identify the third type of middle term
Find the description of a middle term that combines the two extremes (subject and predicate of the conclusion) by a specific property (khāṣṣa).
This is distinct from middle terms based on matter or other aspects.
2
Understand the role of the specific property
Note that the specific property is in the form (ṣūra) of the premise—its quality, quantity, and modality—not its matter (mādda). This formal character ensures the entailment holds for any content.
Form includes affirmative/negative, universal/particular, necessary/contingent.
3
See why the entailment is automatic
Because the specific property has a fixed relation to each extreme, the relation between extremes follows necessarily, without needing to check the material content.
The author says 'in any matter it agrees upon' (fī ayyi mādda ittafaqat).
4
Contrast with conditional reasoning
The passage states that while in the mind this is a conditional (sharṭī wa istithnā'), we do not need to state it explicitly each time; the specific property suffices.
This highlights the efficiency of the third type.
5
Distinguish from weaker inferences
Recognize that conversion (ʿaks) and falsity of the contradictory (kazb al-naqīḍ) are not syllogistic entailments and are not part of this discussion.
The author excludes them to focus strictly on syllogistic necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث هو الذي جمع الطرفين بخاصة لا محالة تلك الخاصة توجب جمع الطرفين اللذين للمطلوب. فلما كانت لها إلى هذا الطرف نسبة كذا وإلى ذلك الطرف نسبة كذا وجب أن يكون بين الطرفين نسبة كذا في أي مادة اتفقت لأن تلك الخاصة في صورة المقدّمة أعني كيفيتها وكميتها وجهتها لا في مادتها لزم دائما أن يصح اللازم فلا يحتاج إلى أن يبتدأ كل وقت بوضع شرطي واستثناء بل يقتصر على تلك الخاصة؛ وإن في الحقيقة عند النفس [٢٤٣,] شرطي واستثناء. وكان من الحملي من هذه الجهة / أيضًا ما يتم في القوة بالشرطي. وأما الأقسام التي تقع دون ذلك فلا يلزمها مثل صورة ذلك اللزوم لزوما قياسيا بل عسى مثل العكس وكذب النقيض وليس كلامنا فيه.
The third is that which combines the two extremes by a specific property; inevitably that specific property necessitates the combination of the two extremes that are required. Since it has such a relation to this extreme and such a relation to that extreme, it must be that there is such a relation between the two extremes in any matter it agrees upon, because that specific property is in the form of the premise—namely its quality, quantity, and modality—not in its matter. Thus the necessary consequence always holds, so there is no need to begin each time by positing a conditional and an exception; rather one is content with that specific property, even though in reality, in the mind [٢٤٣,] it is a conditional and an exception. And part of the categorical [syllogism] from this perspective also is what is completed in potency by the conditional. As for the divisions that fall below that, they do not require such a form of entailment in a syllogistic manner, but rather such as conversion and falsity of the contradictory, and we are not discussing that.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage defines the third type of middle term (mutawassit) in syllogistic theory. The author states that this third type combines both extremes of the conclusion through a specific property (khāṣṣa). That property necessarily brings together the subject and predicate of the desired conclusion. Because this property has a fixed relation to each extreme, the relation between the extremes becomes necessary in any material content, since the property concerns the form (quality, quantity, modality) of the premise, not its matter. Therefore, the conclusion follows automatically without having to reiterate a conditional and its exception each time; the specific property suffices, even though formally it can be reduced to a conditional reasoning in the mind. The author then notes that some categorical syllogisms are completed in potency by a conditional. Finally, he distinguishes this case from weaker forms of inference like conversion (ʿaks) and falsity of the contradictory (kazb al-naqīḍ), which do not produce a syllogistic entailment and are not the subject of this discussion.

Key Takeaway

The Simple and Compound Middle Term
Author categorizes the middle term as simple or compound

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a middle term as simple (mufrad) or compound (ghayr mufrad) and determine if a compound term is effectively simple (fī quwwat mufrad).

Core Idea
The middle term's structure determines whether a syllogism can be built.

In logic, the middle term is the glue that connects the extremes. But not every expression can serve as a proper middle. The author provides a detailed classification: a middle term can be simple or compound. If compound, it may function as a simple term if its parts combine into a single meaning. Understanding this classification is essential for constructing valid syllogisms in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term in a given syllogism
Look at the conclusion and find the term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. This is the middle term (hadd al-awsat).
2
Check if the middle term is a single word with a single meaning (mufrad)
If it is a simple term like 'insān' (human) or 'ālam' (world), it is simple.
3
If not simple, determine if it is compound but effectively simple (fī quwwat mufrad)
A compound term like 'Creator of the heavens and the earth' is composite in wording but refers to one entity; it functions as a simple term in reasoning.
4
Distinguish compound terms that are not effectively simple
Terms with disparate parts that do not combine into a single meaning, like 'body and soul', cannot serve as a unified middle term.
5
Apply the classification to a real kalam example
In the proof from contingency, the middle term 'possible existent' (mumkin al-wujūd) is simple. In arguments using multiple attributes, the middle may be compound but effectively simple if the attributes co-refer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثمّ الثالث فيما تكون نسبته إلى أجزاء المطلوب كما مر لا يخلو من أن يكون شيئًا مفردًا لفظا ومعنى، أو غير مفرد : فإن كان غير مفرد فإما أن يكون في قوة مفرد، أو لا يكون بل يكون أجزاؤه متباينة ولا يتصل منها ما قوته قوة مفرد؛ فإن كان في قوة مفرد فحكمه حكم المفرد فإن وضع لا في قوة مفرد على أنه شيآن أو أشياء متباينة فإما أن يكون لكل واحد منها نسبة إلى كلي الطرفين أو لجملتها أو لبعضها نسبة إلى طرف ولبعضها إلى طرف آخر ؛ فإن كان لكل واحد منها نسبة إلى كلي الطرفين ويتم جمعهما بنسبة واحدة كان القياس الواحد تاما بواحد. ويكون الباقي إما فصلا وإما قياسا آخر وإن كان إنما يتم جمعهما لجميع النسب كلها فيكون حملها هو المتوسط وهو من جهة ما هو حمله کمعنی واحد مثال هذا والنسب متفقة «أب» و «ج» وكل ما هو «ب» و (ج) معا فهو «د». ومثاله والنسب مختلفة «أب» وليس «ج» وكل ما هو «ب» وليس (ج) فهو (د)، ولسنا نبين هنا شرائط الإنتاج بل أحوال هذا المتوسط؛ إذ النسب كما مرَّ. والشرائط كذلك والمقدمة من حيث هي المقدمة لا تكون إلا حملية. اللهم إلا أن يوجد من حيث هي أجزاء من الأمور كما إذا قلت قولنا كل «أب» محصورة. وأما من حيث مقدّمة منفصلة فلا تكون لها نسبة واحدة إلى كل واحد من الطرفين؛ فلا يجب أن تنفصل نسبتها، ولا تكون حالها حال المتوسط الذي فيه تركيب. وقد دخل في هذا البيان ما لا يعتبر الأجزاء فيه البتة بل الجملة؛ فيكون حينئذ نسبة شي واحد وهي أجزاء. وأما القسم الثالث وهو الذي تكون النسب فيه متفرّقة فمن البين أنه لا يجب أن يلزم منه لازم البتة. وذلك لأن نسبة الأول إلى الثاني مثلا نسبة حمل أو وضع، ونسبة الثاني إلى الرابع كذلك، وليس بين الثالث والرابع نسبة فلا يلزم منه شيء؛ إذ الأشياء كلها بهذه الصفة. وبالجملة فالنسبة بين طرفي المطلوب وبين ما هو المتوسط بينهما من اللوازم مفرداً كان ذلك المتوسط أو مركبا، فإذا لم يكن كذلك لم يكن التأليف مفيدًا. والنسبة قد تكون بالفعل، وقد تكون بالقوة، فيجب أن يكون لمحمول المطلوب إلى المتوسط نسبة كلية ، وللموضوع نسبة إيجابية حتى تكون على أشكال قياسية وهي ثلاثة نحو الاقترانية والاستثنائية والخلفية، ثم الاستثنائية إنما يتم أمرها بالاقترانية. وكذلك الخلفية، فالمطلوب لا يتم إلا بهذه الأشكال؛ وهذه الأشكال بالشكل الأول. وقد قيل بعد هذا القول في التعليم الأول / إن كل قياس يتم بكلي [٢٤٣ظ] وبموجب؛ ولا ينتج كلي إلا عن كليتين، وأما الجزئي فقد ينتج عن كليتين وعن كلي وجزئي (١). وبهذا يتبين أنه لا بد من مقدمة كلية ومما هو موجب بالفعل أو بالقوة كالممكن والمطلق الصرف على ما عرف. (۱) أنالوطيقا الأولى لأرسطو طاليس، ص ٢٧٣، ٢٧٤.
Then the third [type] regarding its relation to the parts of the desired [conclusion] as previously: it is either something simple in word and meaning, or not simple. If not simple, then either it is in potency of a simple, or it is not, but rather its parts are disparate and nothing among them that is in potency of a simple is connected; if it is in potency of a simple, then its judgment is the judgment of a simple. If it is posited not in potency of a simple as two things or disparate things, then either each one of them has a relation to the whole of the two extremes, or their totality, or some of them have a relation to one extreme and others to another extreme. If each one has a relation to the whole of the two extremes and their combination is completed by one relation, then the one syllogism is complete by one [middle]. And the remainder is either a division or another syllogism. And if their combination is completed by all the relations together, then their predication is the middle term, and it is, from the perspective of its predication, as a single meaning. An example of this with agreeing relations: 'A is B and C' and 'Everything that is B and C together is D'. And an example with different relations: 'A is B and not C' and 'Everything that is B and not C is D'. And we are not explaining here the conditions of production but rather the states of this middle term, since the relations are as before, and the conditions are likewise. And the premise, as a premise, is only categorical. Except that it may be found as parts of things, as when you say: 'Every A is B, limited.' But as a disjunctive premise, it does not have a single relation to each of the two extremes; so its relation must not be separated, and its state is not the state of a middle term that has composition. And included in this explanation is that which does not consider the parts at all but the whole; in that case there is a relation of one thing, and they are parts. As for the third division, in which the relations are disparate, it is evident that no necessary consequence follows from it at all. This is because the relation of the first to the second, for example, is a relation of predication or positing, and the relation of the second to the fourth likewise, but there is no relation between the third and the fourth, so nothing follows from it; since all things are of this description. In summary, there must be a relation between the two extremes of the desired [conclusion] and that which is the middle term between them among the necessary consequences, whether that middle term is simple or compound. If it is not so, then the composition is not useful. The relation may be actual or potential. So there must be for the predicate of the desired [conclusion] a universal relation to the middle term, and for the subject an affirmative relation, so that it may be in the syllogistic figures, which are three: conjunctive, exceptive, and suppositional. Then the exceptive is only completed by the conjunctive, and likewise the suppositional. So the desired [conclusion] is only completed by these figures; and these figures are by the first figure. And it has been said after this statement in the First Teaching: 'Every syllogism is completed by a universal and an affirmative; and a universal does not produce except from two universals; as for a particular, it may produce from two universals and from a universal and a particular.' By this it is made clear that there must be a universal premise and something affirmative actually or potentially, like the possible and the absolute unqualified, as is known.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed classification of the middle term (mutawassit) based on its relation to the parts of the conclusion. The third type of relation (the one that connects parts) is examined: the middle can be simple (mufrad) in word and meaning, or compound (ghayr mufrad). If compound, it may be effectively simple (in potency of a simple) or not. In each case, the author explains how the syllogism is formed. When the middle is compound but not effectively simple, there are subcases: either each part relates to both extremes, or some to one extreme and others to the other. If each part relates to both extremes and the combination is completed by one relation, then one syllogism suffices; the remainder is either a division (fasl) or another syllogism. If the combination needs all relations together, then the predication is the middle term considered as a single meaning. The author then states that premises, as premises, are only categorical (ḥamliyya), except when considered as parts of compound statements. A disjunctive premise (munfaṣila) does not have a single relation to each extreme and thus cannot serve as a middle term with composition. The author then contrasts this with the case where the whole rather than parts is considered. He then discusses the third division where relations are disparate, where no necessary conclusion follows. Finally, he summarizes that for a useful composition, there must be a relation between the extremes and the middle term, whether simple or compound. He then introduces the three figures: conjunctive (iqtirānī), exceptive (istithnā'ī), and suppositional (khalfī), noting that the exceptive and suppositional are completed by the conjunctive, and all depend on the first figure. The passage ends with a quotation from Aristotle's First Teaching emphasizing the need for universal and affirmative premises.

غير مفرد
في قوة مفرد
حملية

A middle term that is effectively simple functions just like a simple term in producing a syllogism.

The Syllogism

When the Middle Term is Compound but Functionally Simple
Author illustrates compound middle with agreeing and different relations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a compound middle term is effectively simple and distinguish between agreeing and different relations in its parts.

Core Idea
Compound middle terms can act as a single unit if their parts combine into one meaning.

The previous lesson introduced the classification. Now we dive deeper into the subcase when the middle term is compound but not effectively simple: its parts may relate to the extremes in different ways. The author illustrates with examples using logical variables, distinguishing between agreeing and different relations. Understanding these patterns helps in constructing complex syllogisms where the middle term has multiple components.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parts of a compound middle term
If the middle term is compound, break it into its components. For example, 'the universe is created and temporal' has two parts: 'created' and 'temporal'.
2
Determine the relation of each part to the extremes
Does each part relate to both extremes (the subject and predicate of the conclusion), or only to one? The passage describes several possibilities.
3
Identify agreeing vs. different relations
Agreeing: both parts are predicated of the subject (e.g., 'A is B and C'). Different: one part is predicated and the other negated (e.g., 'A is B and not C').
4
Formulate the corresponding universal premise
For agreeing: 'Everything that is B and C is D.' For different: 'Everything that is B and not C is D.'
5
Construct a valid syllogism using these components
Combine with the subject to conclude that A is D. In agreeing case: A is B and C; everything that is B and C is D; so A is D. In different case: A is B and not C; everything that is B and not C is D; so A is D.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثمّ الثالث فيما تكون نسبته إلى أجزاء المطلوب كما مر لا يخلو من أن يكون شيئًا مفردًا لفظا ومعنى، أو غير مفرد : فإن كان غير مفرد فإما أن يكون في قوة مفرد، أو لا يكون بل يكون أجزاؤه متباينة ولا يتصل منها ما قوته قوة مفرد؛ فإن كان في قوة مفرد فحكمه حكم المفرد فإن وضع لا في قوة مفرد على أنه شيآن أو أشياء متباينة فإما أن يكون لكل واحد منها نسبة إلى كلي الطرفين أو لجملتها أو لبعضها نسبة إلى طرف ولبعضها إلى طرف آخر ؛ فإن كان لكل واحد منها نسبة إلى كلي الطرفين ويتم جمعهما بنسبة واحدة كان القياس الواحد تاما بواحد. ويكون الباقي إما فصلا وإما قياسا آخر وإن كان إنما يتم جمعهما لجميع النسب كلها فيكون حملها هو المتوسط وهو من جهة ما هو حمله کمعنی واحد مثال هذا والنسب متفقة «أب» و «ج» وكل ما هو «ب» و (ج) معا فهو «د». ومثاله والنسب مختلفة «أب» وليس «ج» وكل ما هو «ب» وليس (ج) فهو (د)، ولسنا نبين هنا شرائط الإنتاج بل أحوال هذا المتوسط؛ إذ النسب كما مرَّ. والشرائط كذلك والمقدمة من حيث هي المقدمة لا تكون إلا حملية. اللهم إلا أن يوجد من حيث هي أجزاء من الأمور كما إذا قلت قولنا كل «أب» محصورة. وأما من حيث مقدّمة منفصلة فلا تكون لها نسبة واحدة إلى كل واحد من الطرفين؛ فلا يجب أن تنفصل نسبتها، ولا تكون حالها حال المتوسط الذي فيه تركيب. وقد دخل في هذا البيان ما لا يعتبر الأجزاء فيه البتة بل الجملة؛ فيكون حينئذ نسبة شي واحد وهي أجزاء. وأما القسم الثالث وهو الذي تكون النسب فيه متفرّقة فمن البين أنه لا يجب أن يلزم منه لازم البتة. وذلك لأن نسبة الأول إلى الثاني مثلا نسبة حمل أو وضع، ونسبة الثاني إلى الرابع كذلك، وليس بين الثالث والرابع نسبة فلا يلزم منه شيء؛ إذ الأشياء كلها بهذه الصفة. وبالجملة فالنسبة بين طرفي المطلوب وبين ما هو المتوسط بينهما من اللوازم مفرداً كان ذلك المتوسط أو مركبا، فإذا لم يكن كذلك لم يكن التأليف مفيدًا. والنسبة قد تكون بالفعل، وقد تكون بالقوة، فيجب أن يكون لمحمول المطلوب إلى المتوسط نسبة كلية ، وللموضوع نسبة إيجابية حتى تكون على أشكال قياسية وهي ثلاثة نحو الاقترانية والاستثنائية والخلفية، ثم الاستثنائية إنما يتم أمرها بالاقترانية. وكذلك الخلفية، فالمطلوب لا يتم إلا بهذه الأشكال؛ وهذه الأشكال بالشكل الأول. وقد قيل بعد هذا القول في التعليم الأول / إن كل قياس يتم بكلي [٢٤٣ظ] وبموجب؛ ولا ينتج كلي إلا عن كليتين، وأما الجزئي فقد ينتج عن كليتين وعن كلي وجزئي (١). وبهذا يتبين أنه لا بد من مقدمة كلية ومما هو موجب بالفعل أو بالقوة كالممكن والمطلق الصرف على ما عرف. (۱) أنالوطيقا الأولى لأرسطو طاليس، ص ٢٧٣، ٢٧٤.
Then the third [type] regarding its relation to the parts of the desired [conclusion] as previously: it is either something simple in word and meaning, or not simple. If not simple, then either it is in potency of a simple, or it is not, but rather its parts are disparate and nothing among them that is in potency of a simple is connected; if it is in potency of a simple, then its judgment is the judgment of a simple. If it is posited not in potency of a simple as two things or disparate things, then either each one of them has a relation to the whole of the two extremes, or their totality, or some of them have a relation to one extreme and others to another extreme. If each one has a relation to the whole of the two extremes and their combination is completed by one relation, then the one syllogism is complete by one [middle]. And the remainder is either a division or another syllogism. And if their combination is completed by all the relations together, then their predication is the middle term, and it is, from the perspective of its predication, as a single meaning. An example of this with agreeing relations: 'A is B and C' and 'Everything that is B and C together is D'. And an example with different relations: 'A is B and not C' and 'Everything that is B and not C is D'. And we are not explaining here the conditions of production but rather the states of this middle term, since the relations are as before, and the conditions are likewise. And the premise, as a premise, is only categorical. Except that it may be found as parts of things, as when you say: 'Every A is B, limited.' But as a disjunctive premise, it does not have a single relation to each of the two extremes; so its relation must not be separated, and its state is not the state of a middle term that has composition. And included in this explanation is that which does not consider the parts at all but the whole; in that case there is a relation of one thing, and they are parts. As for the third division, in which the relations are disparate, it is evident that no necessary consequence follows from it at all. This is because the relation of the first to the second, for example, is a relation of predication or positing, and the relation of the second to the fourth likewise, but there is no relation between the third and the fourth, so nothing follows from it; since all things are of this description. In summary, there must be a relation between the two extremes of the desired [conclusion] and that which is the middle term between them among the necessary consequences, whether that middle term is simple or compound. If it is not so, then the composition is not useful. The relation may be actual or potential. So there must be for the predicate of the desired [conclusion] a universal relation to the middle term, and for the subject an affirmative relation, so that it may be in the syllogistic figures, which are three: conjunctive, exceptive, and suppositional. Then the exceptive is only completed by the conjunctive, and likewise the suppositional. So the desired [conclusion] is only completed by these figures; and these figures are by the first figure. And it has been said after this statement in the First Teaching: 'Every syllogism is completed by a universal and an affirmative; and a universal does not produce except from two universals; as for a particular, it may produce from two universals and from a universal and a particular.' By this it is made clear that there must be a universal premise and something affirmative actually or potentially, like the possible and the absolute unqualified, as is known.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed classification of the middle term (mutawassit) based on its relation to the parts of the conclusion. The third type of relation (the one that connects parts) is examined: the middle can be simple (mufrad) in word and meaning, or compound (ghayr mufrad). If compound, it may be effectively simple (in potency of a simple) or not. In each case, the author explains how the syllogism is formed. When the middle is compound but not effectively simple, there are subcases: either each part relates to both extremes, or some to one extreme and others to the other. If each part relates to both extremes and the combination is completed by one relation, then one syllogism suffices; the remainder is either a division (fasl) or another syllogism. If the combination needs all relations together, then the predication is the middle term considered as a single meaning. The author then states that premises, as premises, are only categorical (ḥamliyya), except when considered as parts of compound statements. A disjunctive premise (munfaṣila) does not have a single relation to each extreme and thus cannot serve as a middle term with composition. The author then contrasts this with the case where the whole rather than parts is considered. He then discusses the third division where relations are disparate, where no necessary conclusion follows. Finally, he summarizes that for a useful composition, there must be a relation between the extremes and the middle term, whether simple or compound. He then introduces the three figures: conjunctive (iqtirānī), exceptive (istithnā'ī), and suppositional (khalfī), noting that the exceptive and suppositional are completed by the conjunctive, and all depend on the first figure. The passage ends with a quotation from Aristotle's First Teaching emphasizing the need for universal and affirmative premises.

مفرد
غير مفرد
في قوة مفرد
نسب متفقة
نسب مختلفة

Checkpoint
In the passage, what is the example of a compound middle term with different relations?
A) 'A is B and C'
B) 'A is B and not C'
C) 'A is either B or C'
D) 'A is not B and not C'

Key Takeaway
Compound middle terms can produce valid syllogisms when their parts have either agreeing or different relations to the extremes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Express the following argument in logical variable notation: 'The Quran is miraculous (mu'jiz) and divine revelation. Everything that is miraculous and divine revelation is from Allah. Therefore, the Quran is from Allah.' Identify whether the relations are agreeing or different.
?
Create a different-relation syllogism using a hadith-based example: 'The believer is patient and not despairing. Everyone who is patient and not despairing is among the righteous. So the believer is among the righteous.'

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Necessity of Universal Affirmative Premises for Syllogisms
Author states conditions for syllogistic production and the three figures

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify that a productive syllogism requires a universal premise and an affirmative relation, and explain the role of the first figure.

Core Idea
Every valid syllogism requires a universal and affirmative premise.

After discussing the middle term, the author turns to essential conditions for syllogistic production. He emphasizes that premises must be categorical, not disjunctive, and that the predicate of the conclusion must have a universal relation to the middle term, while the subject must have an affirmative relation. He then introduces the three figures (conjunctive, exceptive, suppositional) and notes that exceptive and suppositional depend on the conjunctive, which in turn depends on the first figure. This culminates in the Aristotelian principle that every syllogism requires a universal and affirmative premise.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definition of a categorical premise (ḥamliyya)
A premise that makes a simple assertion of inclusion or exclusion. The author says premises as such are only categorical.
2
Understand why disjunctive premises (munfaṣila) are unsuitable
A disjunctive premise does not have a single relation to each extreme, so it cannot serve as a middle term with composition.
3
Identify the requirement for the predicate and subject
The predicate of the conclusion must have a universal relation to the middle term (that is, the middle must be universalized). The subject must have an affirmative relation.
4
Recognize the three figures: conjunctive (iqtirāniyya), exceptive (istithnā'iyya), suppositional (khalfiyya)
The conjunctive is the basic categorical syllogism; exceptive is conditional modus ponens or tollens; suppositional is reductio ad absurdum. The latter two depend on the conjunctive.
5
Apply the principle: every syllogism requires a universal and affirmative premise
The author quotes Aristotle: a universal conclusion needs two universal premises; a particular can come from two universals or a universal and a particular. The premises must be affirmative actually or potentially.
