The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
The Two Essential Premises: Universal and Affirmative
Author states the necessity of universal and affirmative premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given syllogism satisfies the necessary conditions of having a universal premise and an affirmative premise.

Core Idea
No syllogism yields a necessary conclusion without a universal and an affirmative premise.

Imagine a syllogism where the major premise is particular or negative. Can it still produce a certain conclusion? The author, drawing from Aristotle, lays down a fundamental principle: every productive syllogism must include a universal premise and an affirmative premise. This lesson unpacks that principle and shows you how to apply it when evaluating arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two required premise types
Recall that a universal premise (kulli) refers to all individuals of a kind (e.g., 'every intoxicant'), while an affirmative premise (mujib) asserts a predicate of a subject (e.g., 'is haram'). The passage states that the predicate of the conclusion must have a universal relation to the middle term, and the subject must have an affirmative relation.
Affirmative includes positive propositions; negative propositions do not count.
2
Locate the author's explicit claim
Find the sentence: 'it is necessary that the predicate of the sought [conclusion] has a universal relation to the middle, and the subject has a positive relation.' This is the core condition. Then note the citation of Aristotle: 'every syllogism is perfected by a universal and an affirmative [premise]'.
3
Test with a concrete Islamic example
Take the fiqh syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is haram. Khamr is an intoxicant. Therefore, khamr is haram.' The major premise ('Every intoxicant is haram') is universal affirmative. The minor ('Khamr is an intoxicant') is particular affirmative. The condition holds. Now try a flawed version: 'Some intoxicants are haram. Khamr is an intoxicant. Therefore, khamr is haram.' The major is particular, not universal — the condition fails, so the conclusion is not necessary.
The passage also notes that a particular conclusion can come from two universals or a universal and a particular, but the condition on the universal and affirmative premises still applies to the structure.
4
Consider potential and actual affirmatives
The author adds that the affirmative premise can be actual or potential, such as the possible (al-mumkin) or the absolutely unconditional (al-mutlaq al-sirf). This means even modal premises can satisfy the affirmative condition as long as they are not negative.
This nuance is important for arguments dealing with contingency and necessity, common in kalam.

حملية. اللهم إلا أن يوجد من حيث هي أجزاء من الأمور كما إذا قلت قولنا كل «أب» محصورة. وأما من حيث مقدّمة منفصلة فلا تكون لها نسبة واحدة إلى كل واحد من الطرفين؛ فلا يجب أن تنفصل نسبتها، ولا تكون حالها حال المتوسط الذي فيه تركيب. وقد دخل في هذا البيان ما لا يعتبر الأجزاء فيه البتة بل الجملة؛ فيكون حينئذ نسبة شي واحد وهي أجزاء. وأما القسم الثالث وهو الذي تكون النسب فيه متفرّقة فمن البين أنه لا يجب أن يلزم منه لازم البتة. وذلك لأن نسبة الأول إلى الثاني مثلا نسبة حمل أو وضع، ونسبة الثاني إلى الرابع كذلك، وليس بين الثالث والرابع نسبة فلا يلزم منه شيء؛ إذ الأشياء كلها بهذه الصفة. وبالجملة فالنسبة بين طرفي المطلوب وبين ما هو المتوسط بينهما من اللوازم مفرداً كان ذلك المتوسط أو مركبا، فإذا لم يكن كذلك لم يكن التأليف مفيدًا. والنسبة قد تكون بالفعل ، وقد تكون بالقوة، فيجب أن يكون لمحمول المطلوب إلى المتوسط نسبة كلية ، وللموضوع نسبة إيجابية حتى تكون على أشكال قياسية وهي ثلاثة نحو الاقترانية والاستثنائية والخلفية، ثم الاستثنائية إنما يتم أمرها بالاقترانية. وكذلك الخلفية، فالمطلوب لا يتم إلا بهذه الأشكال؛ وهذه الأشكال بالشكل الأول. وقد قيل بعد هذا القول في التعليم الأول / إن كل قياس يتم بكلي وبموجب؛ ولا ينتج كلي إلا عن كليتين، وأما الجزئي فقد ينتج عن كليتين وعن كلي وجزئي (١). وبهذا يتبين أنه لا بد من مقدمة كلية ومما هو موجب بالفعل أو بالقوة كالممكن والمطلق الصرف على ما عرف.
(۱) أنالوطيقا الأولى لأرسطو طاليس، ص ٢٧٣، ٢٧٤.
[They are] categorical. Except when [the parts] exist as parts of matters, as when you say our saying 'every B' is quantified. But as a disjunctive premise, it does not have a single relation to each of the two sides; so its relation should not be separated, nor is its situation like the middle that has composition. And included in this explanation is that which does not consider the parts at all but the whole; then it is a relation of one thing and they are parts. As for the third division, which has scattered relations, it is clear that no necessary consequence follows from it at all. That is because the relation of the first to the second, for example, is a relation of predication or position, and the relation of the second to the fourth likewise, and there is no relation between the third and the fourth, so nothing follows from it; since all things are of this description. In sum, the relation between the two ends of the sought [conclusion] and what is the middle between them among the concomitants, whether that middle is simple or composite, if it is not like that, then the composition is not useful. And the relation may be actual or potential, so it is necessary that the predicate of the sought [conclusion] has a universal relation to the middle, and the subject has a positive relation, so that [the syllogisms] are upon the three syllogistic forms: the categorical, the conditional, and the reduction to the impossible. Then the conditional is perfected only by the categorical. Likewise the reduction to the impossible. So the sought [conclusion] is not completed except by these forms; and these forms [are reduced] to the first figure. And it has been said after this statement in the First Analytics: every syllogism is perfected by a universal and an affirmative [premise]; and a universal [conclusion] does not result except from two universal [premises]; but a particular [conclusion] may result from two universals or from a universal and a particular. And by this it is clear that a universal premise is necessary, and that which is affirmative actually or potentially, such as the possible and the absolutely unconditional, as is known.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage discusses the conditions necessary for a syllogism to be productive, focusing on the relations between premises and the forms of syllogism. The author begins by addressing a specific case: when a premise is disjunctive (manfasila), it does not have a single relation to both extremes, so its relational structure is different. Then the author describes three divisions of relations: one where the whole is considered, one where parts are considered, and one where relations are scattered; only the first two yield necessary conclusions. The key insight is that a productive syllogism requires a universal relation between the predicate of the conclusion and the middle term, and an affirmative relation between the subject and the middle. This yields the three standard forms: categorical (iqtirani), conditional (istithna'i), and reduction to the impossible (khalfi). The author notes that conditional and reduction syllogisms ultimately depend on the categorical. Finally, the author cites Aristotle's principle that every syllogism is perfected by a universal and an affirmative premise, and that a universal conclusion requires two universals, while a particular conclusion can come from two universals or one universal and one particular. This principle is then linked to the earlier discussion, emphasizing that a universal premise and an affirmative premise (actual or potential) are indispensable.

Key Takeaway
Every productive syllogism requires a universal premise and an affirmative premise (actual or potential).

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
The Three Syllogistic Forms and Their Interrelation
Author describes the three syllogistic forms and their interrelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between categorical, conditional, and reductio syllogisms, and explain why conditional and reductio depend on the categorical form.

Core Idea
All syllogisms resolve into the categorical form.

The author identifies three types of syllogism: categorical (iqtirani), conditional (istithna'i), and reduction to the impossible (khalfi). But he insists that the conditional and reductio are perfected only through the categorical. This lesson explores each form and their logical relationship, using examples from Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Define the three syllogistic forms
Categorical (iqtirani) consists of two categorical premises, e.g., 'All A are B; all B are C; therefore all A are C.' Conditional (istithna'i) has a conditional premise (if-then) and an exception, e.g., 'If A then B; A; therefore B.' Reductio (khalfi) proves a conclusion by showing its negation leads to a contradiction.
The passage also mentions them as 'the three syllogistic forms'.
2
Find the author's claim about dependence
Look at the sentence: 'Then the conditional is perfected only by the categorical. Likewise the reduction to the impossible.' This means that conditional and reductio arguments rely on an underlying categorical structure for their validity.
For example, the conditional syllogism 'If A then B; A; therefore B' can be recast as a categorical syllogism with a universal major premise.
3
Illustrate with a kalam example
Consider the categorical argument: 'Everything contingent has a cause; the world is contingent; therefore the world has a cause.' Its conditional form: 'If the world is contingent, then it has a cause; the world is contingent; therefore it has a cause.' The conditional premise implicitly contains a categorical universal. Reductio: 'Assume the world has no cause; then it would be eternal; but it is contingent (which implies temporality); contradiction; therefore the world has a cause.' The reductio works by showing that the negation of the conclusion leads to a contradiction, which is a categorical inconsistency.
The passage also states that 'these forms [are reduced] to the first figure', meaning all three can be transformed into a first-figure categorical syllogism.
4
Confirm the necessity of universal and affirmative premises (review)
Since conditional and reductio ultimately depend on categorical syllogisms, they too require the condition discussed in the previous lesson: a universal and an affirmative premise. This underscores the unity of the syllogistic theory.

The Syllogism

The Five Relations That Reduce Any Syllogism to Two Premises
Author argues that any syllogism reduces to exactly two premises via five relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which of the five relations underlies a given syllogism and explain why it reduces to exactly two premises.

Core Idea
Every syllogism, no matter how complex, collapses into two premises: a conditional and an exception.

In this passage, the author tackles a puzzle: if syllogisms can have many premises, how can they be said to have only two? He identifies five fundamental relations between the known premise and the unknown conclusion. Each of these relations forces the syllogism into two premises. Understanding these relations is the key to seeing the unity behind all syllogistic forms.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the claim
The author asserts that any syllogism whatsoever must have exactly two premises, no more and no less. This seems counterintuitive when we see multi-premise arguments, but he will show how they reduce.
This is a strong reductionist claim.
2
List the five relations
The known (معلوم) can relate to the sought (مطلوب) in five ways: (a) known implies sought, (b) contradictory of sought implies contradictory of known, (c) known is incompatible with contradictory of sought, (d) known is incompatible with sought itself, (e) known relates to each part of sought separately.
These relations correspond to different syllogistic forms.
3
Match relations to conditional types
For relations (a)-(d), the first premise is a conditional (conjunctive for a,b; disjunctive for c,d). The second premise is an exceptional premise (مقدمة استثنائية) that affirms one part. Together they yield the conclusion.
The exceptional premise asserts the occurrence of the known.
4
Examine the fifth relation
Relation (e) covers categorical syllogisms. The known serves as a middle term that has a positive or negative relation to each part of the conclusion. This also yields exactly two premises: one relating the middle to each term.
This is the foundation of the first-figure categorical syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في أن الأقيسة قد تكون مؤلفة من مقدمات كثيرة ] وأما الأقيسة فإنها قد تكون مؤلفة من مقدمات كثيرة؛ وإن كان القياس أي قياس كان لا بد من مقدمتين لا أزيد ولا أنقص. وقد مر من قبل أن المطلوب المجهول إنما يعلم من قبل حاصل معلوم، والعلم به ضروري وذلك الحاصل [أ] لا بد وأن تكون له نسبة إلى عين ذلك المطلوب، [ب] أو لنقيضه نسبة إلى نقيض ذلك المطلوب، [ت] أو لعينه نسبة إلى نقيض ذلك المطلوب، [ث] أو لنقيضه نسبة إلى عين ذلك، [ج] أو لعينه نسبة إلى كل جزء من جزأي المطلوب. وكيف كان يحصل لنا مؤلف من مقدمتين لا أزيد ولا أنقص. أما في القسم الأول فعلى أن ذلك الحاصل المعلوم ملزوم لذلك المجهول، وأما في الثاني فعلى أن نقيض ذلك المجهول ملزوم لنقيض ذلك المعلوم، وأما في الثالث فعلى ذلك المعلوم من جملة ما يمتنع اجتماعه مع نقيض ذلك المجهول. وأما في الرابع فعلى أن ذلك المعلوم من جملة ما يمتنع اجتماعه عين ذلك المجهول في الكذب وهي المقدمة الشرطية المتصلة في القسمين الأولين والمنفصلة في القسمين الآخرين. والمقدمة الأخرى في الأقسام الأربعة تدل على حصول ذلك المعلوم وهي المقدمة الاستثنائية : وحينئذ يلزم بالضرورة العلم بحصول المطلوب، فظهر أن القياس الاستثنائي مركب من مقدمتين لا غير. وأما في القسم الخامس وهو أن تكون لذلك المعلوم نسبة إلى كل واحد من جزأي المطلوب فلا بد فيه من أن تكون تلك النسبة بحيث توقع بين جزأي المطلوب نسبته تلك النسبة مطلوبة من القياس. وذلك إنما يكون بأن يؤخذ شيء واحد له إلى كل واحد من الطرفين نسبة إيجابية إن كان المطلوب نسبة إيجابية، أو نسبة إيجابية إلى أحدهما وسلبية إلى الآخر إن كان المطلوب به سلبية. ولو كان كذلك فلا بد وأن يحصل
Section: [On that syllogisms may be composed of many premises] As for syllogisms, they may be composed of many premises; yet any syllogism whatsoever must have two premises, no more and no less. And it has passed before that the unknown sought [conclusion] is only known from a known obtained [premise], and knowledge of it is necessary. And that obtained [premise] must have either [a] a relation to the very same sought [conclusion], or [b] a relation of its contradictory to the contradictory of that sought, or [c] a relation of the same [obtained] to the contradictory of the sought, or [d] a relation of its contradictory to the same sought, or [e] a relation of the same [obtained] to each part of the two parts of the sought. However it is, we obtain a composition of two premises, no more and no less. As for the first division, [it is] on [the basis] that that obtained known is an implicans of that unknown. As for the second, [it is] on [the basis] that the contradictory of that unknown is an implicans of the contradictory of that known. As for the third, [it is] on [the basis] that that known is among what is impossible to coexist with the contradictory of that unknown. As for the fourth, [it is] on [the basis] that that known is among what is impossible to coexist with the very same unknown in falsehood—and these are the conjunctive conditional premise in the first two divisions and the disjunctive [conditional] in the other two divisions. And the other premise in the four divisions indicates the occurrence of that known, and it is the exceptional premise. Then necessarily knowledge of the occurrence of the sought follows, so it is clear that the conditional syllogism is composed of two premises, no more. As for the fifth division, which is that the known has a relation to each one of the two parts of the sought, then it is necessary that the relation be such that it establishes between the two parts of the sought the relation that is sought from the syllogism. That is only by taking one thing that has to each of the two terminals a positive relation if the sought is positive, or a positive relation to one and a negative to the other if the sought is negative. And if it is like that, then it must be obtained...
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage addresses the apparent paradox that syllogisms can have many premises yet essentially require only two. The author first asserts that any syllogism, no matter how many premises it seems to have, fundamentally consists of exactly two premises. He then enumerates five ways in which the known premise (the premise we have) relates to the unknown conclusion that we seek. These five divisions correspond to the types of relations in a syllogism: (1) the known directly implies the unknown, (2) the contradictory of the unknown implies the contradictory of the known (contrapositive), (3) the known is incompatible with the contradictory of the unknown, (4) the known and the unknown are incompatible (both cannot be false), and (5) the known has a relation to each part of the conclusion separately (this underlies the categorical syllogism). For the first four divisions, the author explains that the syllogism consists of a conditional premise (conjunctive or disjunctive) and an exceptional premise (which asserts the occurrence of the known). Thus, there are always two premises. For the fifth division, which corresponds to categorical syllogisms, the known serves as a middle term that relates to the subject and predicate of the conclusion. The author breaks down the required relations (positive or negative) depending on whether the conclusion is affirmative or negative. The passage ends mid-sentence but clearly establishes the core principle: all syllogisms, regardless of appearance, reduce to two premises.

Key Terms
ملزوم
نقيض
شرطية متصلة
شرطية منفصلة
مقدمة استثنائية

Key Takeaway
All syllogisms, whether conditional or categorical, ultimately consist of exactly two premises: a conditional and an exception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take a real example from usul al-fiqh: 'Alcohol is prohibited because it intoxicates; Nabidh is intoxicating; so nabidh is prohibited.' Identify which of the five relations this syllogism falls under and show how it reduces to exactly two premises.
?
Examine the following complex argument: 'If it rains, the ground is wet. It is raining. Therefore the ground is wet.' Which of the five relations does this represent? Explain why it has exactly two premises.

Defining the Compound Syllogism
Author defines compound syllogism and its basic constraint.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a compound syllogism and distinguish it from a simple syllogism in Islamic logical texts.

Core Idea
A compound syllogism has two premises; each premise is itself a conclusion of prior syllogisms.

The author has established that every syllogism requires exactly two premises. But what happens when those premises themselves need proof? This lesson introduces the compound syllogism, where each premise can be the result of a prior syllogism. Understanding this is crucial for analyzing complex arguments in kalam and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Recall the basic syllogism structure
Every syllogism has exactly two premises, no more and no less. This is a foundational rule.
2
Understand the paradox of compound syllogisms
If a premise itself requires proof, it may be composed of multiple premises. But the overall argument still functions as a single syllogism with two premises.
The compound syllogism is still a single syllogism, not a chain.
3
Extract the definition
Find the exact definition: a compound syllogism is one where each of its two premises itself contains two or more premises.
Look for the phrase 'ولا يمتنع أن تكون كل واحدة من المقدمتين مشتملة على المقدمتين أو أكثر منهما'.
4
Check the constraint
Despite the internal complexity, the compound syllogism still produces a single conclusion from two premises.
The number of premises in the basic structure remains two.

فصل : في أن الأقيسة قد تكون مؤلفة من مقدمات كثيرة وأما الأقيسة فإنها قد تكون مؤلفة من مقدمات كثيرة؛ وإن كان القياس أي قياس كان لا بد من مقدمتين لا أزيد ولا أنقص. وقد مر من قبل أن المطلوب المجهول إنما يعلم من قبل حاصل معلوم، والعلم به ضروري وذلك الحاصل لا بد وأن تكون له نسبة إلى عين ذلك المطلوب، أو لنقيضه نسبة إلى نقيض ذلك المطلوب، أو لعينه نسبة إلى نقيض ذلك المطلوب، أو لنقيضه نسبة إلى عين ذلك، أو لعينه نسبة إلى كل جزء من جزأي المطلوب. وكيف كان يحصل لنا مؤلف من مقدمتين لا أزيد ولا أنقص. فإذن القياس المنتج بالذات بنتيجة واحدة لا يزيد على مقدمتين ولا ينقص عنهما. ولا يمتنع أن تكون كل واحدة من المقدمتين مشتملة على المقدمتين أو أكثر منهما؛ ويسمى ذلك القياس قياسًا مركباً. والقياسات المركبة قد تكون موصولة وهي التي تذكر فيها النتائج تارة لبيان كونها منتجة وتارة لبيان كونها مقدمة لما بعدها، وقد تكون مفصولة وهي التي لا تذكر فيها النتائج البتة. ومنهم من سمى الأولى مفصولة والثانية موصولة على العكس وذلك من جملة ما لا نزاع فيه. وأمثال هذه الأقيسة المركبة كثيرة؛ ذلك على ذلك القياسات المستعملة في أصول أقلوديس وغيرها. فنقول إن المقدمات قد تزيد على الاثنين لواحد من هذه الوجوه الثلاثة إما أن تكون تلك المقدمات ليست مقدمات القياس القريب بل مقدمات تنتج المقدمات التي هي أقرب، وإما أن تكون موردة على سبيل الاستقراء والتمثيل؛ فلا تكون مقدمات القياس نفسه بل مقدمات استقراء يتعرف بها صحة مقدمة من مقدمات القياس. وإما أن تكون لا على أحد هذين الوجهين؛ وذلك على وجوه منها أن يورد للحيلة وهي التي يراد بها إخفاء النتيجة من القياس لا إظهارها: فالمقدّمات الضرورية إذا أوردت صرفة بحدس ما ينساق إليه من النتيجة، وتعلم كيفية انسياقها إليه فعوشر في تسليمها.
Chapter: On that syllogisms may be composed of many premises. As for syllogisms, they may be composed of many premises, although any syllogism whatsoever must have two premises, no more and no less. It has preceded that the unknown sought is only known from an already known obtained thing, and knowledge of it is necessary. That obtained thing must have a relation either to the very thing sought, or to its contradictory in relation to the contradictory of that sought, or to the very thing in relation to the contradictory of that sought, or to its contradictory in relation to the very thing, or to the very thing in relation to each part of the two parts of the sought. However it is, we obtain a composition of two premises, no more and no less. So the syllogism that produces by itself a single conclusion does not exceed two premises nor fall short of them. And it is not impossible that each of the two premises itself contains two or more premises; that syllogism is called a compound syllogism. Compound syllogisms may be connected, in which the results are mentioned sometimes to show that they are productive and sometimes to show that they are a premise for what follows, and they may be disconnected, in which the results are not mentioned at all. Some call the first disconnected and the second connected, the opposite—this is among matters about which there is no dispute. Examples of such compound syllogisms are numerous, like those used in the Elements of Euclid and others. So we say that the premises may exceed two for one of three reasons: either those premises are not premises of the proximate syllogism but premises that produce the premises that are closer; or they are adduced by way of induction and analogy, so they are not premises of the syllogism itself but premises of induction by which the correctness of a premise of the syllogism is known; or they are not in either of these two ways, and this has forms, among them being adduced for trickery, by which concealment of the conclusion is intended, not its disclosure. For necessary premises, when adduced pure with a guess of what the conclusion leads to, and one learns how it leads to it, then it becomes difficult to accept them.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage explains how syllogisms can be composed of many premises while fundamentally requiring only two. The author first reiterates that every syllogism has exactly two premises. He recalls the earlier discussion about the relation between the known obtained thing and the sought unknown, showing that in all cases the syllogistic result is derived from two premises. Then he introduces the concept of the compound syllogism: a syllogism where each of the two premises itself contains two or more premises. He classifies compound syllogisms into two types: connected (mawsula), where the intermediate conclusions are stated either to show they are valid or to serve as premises for later steps, and disconnected (mafsula), where the intermediate conclusions are omitted entirely. He notes that some scholars reverse the names, but this is a terminological dispute. He gives Euclid's Elements as an example of using compound syllogisms. Finally, he lists three reasons why the total premises may exceed two: (1) the extra premises are not premises of the proximate syllogism but produce the premises that are closer (i.e., they are part of a prior syllogism); (2) they are adduced via induction (istiqra') or analogy (tamthil) to establish the truth of a premise; (3) they are used for trickery (hila), i.e., to conceal the conclusion rather than reveal it, making it difficult for the opponent to accept the necessary premises. This passage is an expansion of the basic syllogism theory, clarifying how complex arguments are built.

Key Terms
القياس المركب
مقدمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the defining feature of a compound syllogism?
A) It has more than two premises in its basic structure.
B) Each of its two premises is itself composed of two or more premises.
C) It uses induction instead of syllogism.
D) It conceals the conclusion to trick the opponent.

Key Takeaway
A compound syllogism is a single syllogism whose premises are themselves syllogistic conclusions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a compound syllogism in the following chain: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden. Every nabidh is an intoxicant. Therefore nabidh is forbidden.' But the premise 'Every nabidh is an intoxicant' is itself proven by a prior syllogism: 'All nabidh drinks are made from grapes; all grape-based intoxicants are intoxicants; therefore nabidh is intoxicant.' Explain how this forms a compound syllogism.

Types and Reasons for Compound Syllogisms
Author describes two types of compound syllogisms and three reasons for extra premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between connected and disconnected compound syllogisms and identify the three reasons for extra premises.

Core Idea
Compound syllogisms may be connected (explicit results) or disconnected (results omitted), with extra premises for proof, induction, or concealment.

After understanding the basic nature of compound syllogisms, the author now classifies them into two types based on whether intermediate conclusions are stated. He also explains why a compound syllogism may require more premises than a simple one. These distinctions are vital when analyzing complex arguments in Islamic jurisprudence and theology.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish connected vs disconnected
Connected syllogisms explicitly state intermediate conclusions; disconnected syllogisms omit them.
The author gives two purposes for stating intermediate results: to show they are valid or to serve as premises for later steps.
2
Note the terminological dispute
Some scholars reverse the names, but the author considers it a non-issue.
This caution helps avoid confusion when reading different texts.
3
Identify the three reasons for extra premises
Extra premises arise from: (1) premises that produce the proximate premises, (2) induction/analogy to support a premise, (3) trickery to conceal the conclusion.
Reason 3 is negative; it obscures the argument.
4
Recognize the purpose of each reason
Each reason explains why a compound syllogism might involve additional statements beyond the two basic premises.
Induction and analogy are tools to establish premises, not part of the syllogism itself.

The Four Figures of Conditional Conjunctive Syllogisms
Author enumerates the four figures of conditional conjunctive syllogisms with their conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four figures of conditional conjunctive syllogisms based on the relation of the extremes as described by the author.

Core Idea
Conditional conjunctive syllogisms have four figures defined by how the two extremes relate.

In this passage, the author describes the four figures of conditional conjunctive syllogisms. These figures determine valid patterns of inference when both premises are conditionals. Your task is to parse the text and recognize each figure's distinct pattern, building on your knowledge of categorical syllogism figures.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two extremes
In conditional conjunctive syllogisms, the two extremes are the antecedent and consequent of each conditional premise. The text refers to 'one of them' and 'the other' – these are the extremes.
The extremes are the propositions that are connected across the two premises.
2
Parse the four descriptions
The text gives four brief descriptions separated by 'أو' (or) and marked by 'كما في الشكل الأول/الثاني/الثالث/الرابع'. Read each description and note the relation between the two extremes.
Key phrases: 'يحصل الآخر له أو لا يحصل بل يسلب عنه' (first), 'يحصل لأحدهما أو لا يحصل للآخر' (second), 'يحصل كل واحد من الطرفين له أو يحصل أحدهما له ويسلب الآخر عنه' (third), 'يحصل أحدهما له أو يسلب عنه ويحصل هذا للآخر أو يسلب هو وعن الآخر' (fourth).
3
Map each description to a figure
Assign each description to its corresponding figure: first, second, third, fourth. Verify the pattern: first figure involves one extreme obtaining or being negated for the other; second figure involves it obtaining for one and being negated for the other; etc.
Compare with categorical syllogism figures where the middle term's position defines the figure.
4
Construct an example for a figure
Choose one figure (e.g., first) and create a simple conditional conjunctive syllogism using an Islamic example. For instance: 'If an act is commanded, then it is obligatory. If an act is obligatory, then doing it is rewarded.' This illustrates the first figure where the middle term 'obligatory' is affirmed in both premises.
This helps solidify the abstract description into concrete reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأحدهما، ويحصل الآخر له أو لا يحصل بل يسلب عنه كما في الشكل الأول، أو يحصل لأحدهما أو لا يحصل للآخر بل يسلب عنه كما في الشكل الثاني أو يحصل كل واحد من الطرفين له أو يحصل أحدهما له ويسلب الآخر عنه كما في الشكل الثالث، أو يحصل أحدهما له أو يسلب عنه ويحصل هذا للآخر أو يسلب هو وعن الآخر كما في الشكل الرابع: فهذه هي القياسات الاقترانية الشرطية.
For one of them, and the other obtains for it or does not obtain but is negated from it, as in the first figure; or it obtains for one of them or does not obtain for the other but is negated from it, as in the second figure; or each one of the two extremes obtains for it or one of them obtains for it and the other is negated from it, as in the third figure; or one of them obtains for it or is negated from it, and this obtains for the other or is negated from it and from the other, as in the fourth figure: these are the conditional conjunctive syllogisms.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage enumerates the four figures of conditional conjunctive syllogisms (al-qiyasāt al-iqtirāniyya al-sharṭiyya). Each figure is defined by the relation between the two extremes (the antecedent and consequent of the conditional premises). In the first figure, one extreme either obtains for the other or is negated. In the second, the extreme obtains for one or is negated from the other. In the third, both extremes either obtain or one is affirmed and the other negated. In the fourth, one extreme obtains or is negated for the first, and similarly for the second. This classification helps identify valid moods in conditional reasoning, analogous to the figures of categorical syllogisms.

Key Terms
القياسات الاقترانية الشرطية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One is affirmed and the other is negated
B) One obtains for the other or is negated
C) Both are affirmed
D) One is affirmed and the other is also affirmed

Key Takeaway
The four figures of conditional conjunctive syllogisms are defined by how the two extremes of the conditional premises relate, similar to the figures in categorical syllogisms.

Compound Syllogisms: Definition and Types
Author defines compound syllogism and distinguishes between connected and disconnected types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define a compound syllogism and distinguish between its connected and disconnected types based on whether intermediate conclusions are mentioned.

Core Idea
Compound syllogisms chain simple syllogisms; the types differ in explicitly stating intermediate conclusions.

In the previous lesson, you learned that a simple productive syllogism has exactly two premises. However, arguments in Islamic scholarship often involve multiple steps. The author now introduces the compound syllogism (qiyās murakkab), which expands this structure. This lesson will help you identify when an argument is compound and whether it is connected or disconnected.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definition of a simple syllogism
The author reaffirms that a simple productive syllogism has exactly two premises. This is the baseline for understanding the compound form.
2
Recognize the extension to compound
Understand that each premise may itself be a syllogism containing two or more premises, resulting in a compound syllogism. This is the key differentia.
The passage says 'it is not impossible' for each premise to contain multiple premises.
3
Distinguish connected vs. disconnected
In a connected (mawsūla) compound syllogism, intermediate conclusions (results) are explicitly mentioned, either to show they are productive or as premises for later steps. In disconnected (mafṣūla), these intermediate results are omitted entirely.
The passage explicitly defines both types.
4
Note the terminological inversion
Some scholars swap the names, calling the first type 'disconnected' and the second 'connected'. The author regards this as inconsequential and not a matter of dispute.
This is a minor lexical variation; focus on the conceptual definitions.
5
Apply the classification
Given a multi-step argument, determine if it is a compound syllogism and whether it is connected or disconnected. The key clue is the presence or absence of explicitly stated intermediate conclusions.
Practice with examples from fiqh or kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى هذا في الغير فإذن القياس المنتج بالذات بنتيجة واحدة لا يزيد على مقدمتين ولا ينقص عنهما. ولا يمتنع أن تكون كل واحدة من المقدمتين مشتملة على المقدمتين أو أكثر منهما؛ ويسمى ذلك القياس قياسًا مركباً . والقياسات المركَّبة قد تكون موصولة وهي التي تذكر فيها النتائج تارة لبيان كونها منتجة وتارة لبيان كونها مقدمة لما بعدها، وقد تكون مفصولة [٢٤٤ و] وهي التي لا تذكر فيها النتائج البتة. ومنهم من سمى الأولى مفصولة والثانية موصولة على العكس وذلك من جملة ما لا نزاع فيه.
And accordingly, regarding others, then the syllogism that produces by itself with one conclusion does not exceed two premises and does not fall short of them. And it is not impossible that each one of the two premises contains two or more premises; and that is called a compound syllogism. And compound syllogisms may be connected (mawsūla), which are those in which the results are mentioned sometimes to show that they are productive and sometimes to show that they are premises for what follows, and they may be disconnected (mafṣūla), which are those in which the results are not mentioned at all. And some of them call the first 'disconnected' and the second 'connected' inversely, and that is among things about which there is no dispute.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage defines the compound syllogism (qiyās murakkab). The author first establishes that a simple productive syllogism has exactly two premises. However, each premise may itself be composed of multiple premises, leading to a compound syllogism. Compound syllogisms are of two types: mawsūla (connected) where intermediate conclusions are explicitly stated, and mafṣūla (disconnected) where they are omitted. There is a terminological dispute: some scholars reverse the names, but the author considers it inconsequential. This classification is important for understanding how complex arguments are built from simple syllogisms.

Key Terms
قياس مركب
موصولة
مفصولة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a disconnected (mafṣūla) compound syllogism, what is done with the intermediate conclusions?
A) They are omitted entirely
B) They are mentioned explicitly
C) They are negated
D) They are replaced with new premises

Key Takeaway
A compound syllogism is a chain of simple syllogisms; the connected type explicitly states intermediate conclusions, while the disconnected type omits them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether the following argument is a connected or disconnected compound syllogism: 'All intoxicants are forbidden (established from the Qur'an). All khamr is an intoxicant. Therefore, all khamr is forbidden. Now, all nabidh is an intoxicant. Therefore, all nabidh is forbidden.' Explain your reasoning based on the presence or absence of intermediate conclusions.
?

(Do not worry about complete accuracy; focus on the structure of mentioning the intermediate conclusion.)

Three Reasons for Additional Premises: Deception, Embellishment, Clarification
Author explains three reasons for extra premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether an extra premise in a syllogism serves the purpose of deception, embellishment, or clarification.

Core Idea
Extra premises hide, beautify, or clarify — they don't change the core logic.

In the study of syllogisms, you will often encounter arguments with more than two premises. The author explains that these extra premises do not belong to the immediate syllogism but are added for specific rhetorical or inductive purposes. Understanding these purposes helps you distinguish between genuine logical premises and supportive or deceptive additions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
The author lists three reasons: premises that produce immediate premises, premises via induction/analogy, and a third category with three sub-types (ḥīla, zīna, īḍāḥ). Make sure you can distinguish these.
2
Understand the purpose of deception (al-ḥīla)
These premises are intended to hide the conclusion, not show it. The author says they make it difficult to accept the necessary premises due to hidden implications.
This occurs in dialectic and examination.
3
Understand embellishment (al-zīna)
These premises beautify speech without affecting the logical conclusion. Their presence or absence is irrelevant to the purpose.
4
Understand clarification (al-īḍāḥ)
These premises are for confirmation, such as parallels, linguistic divisions, or transitions. They support explanation.
5
Connect to dialectic
The author mentions that these types (especially deception) occur in dialectic, examination, and refined opinion. Recognize the contexts.

فنقول إن المقدّمات قد تزيد على الاثنين لواحد من هذه الوجوه الثلاثة إما أن تكون تلك المقدمات ليست مقدمات القياس القريب بل مقدمات تنتج المقدمات التي هي أقرب، وإما أن تكون موردة على سبيل الاستقراء والتمثيل؛ فلا تكون مقدمات القياس نفسه بل مقدمات استقراء يتعرف بها صحة مقدمة من مقدمات القياس. وإما أن تكون لا على أحد هذين الوجهين؛ وذلك على وجوه منها أن يورد للحيلة وهي التي يراد بها إخفاء النتيجة من القياس لا إظهارها : فالمقدّمات الضرورية إذا أوردت صرفة بحدس ما ينساق إليه من النتيجة، وتعلم كيفية انسياقها إليه فعوشر في تسليمها. فإذا خفي وجه انسياقها وظن بها أنها عديمة الجدوى وخصوصًا لاختلاط ما لا يجدي تركب المعاشرة في تسليمها وهذا في الجدلي والامتحان والرأي بالتدقيق ومنها أن يورد للزينة وهي المقدمات التي يحاول بها تحسين الكلام، وجودها وعدمها بالنسبة إلى المقصود بمنزله. ومنها أن يورد للإيضاح وهي المقدمات التي إيرادها للتقرير كالنظائر مثلا وكتقسيم اللفظ وكالانتقال من لفظ إلى لفظ ونحو ذلك مما يعرف في كتاب الجدل، فإن هذه كلها للاستظهار في الإبانة. وأما القياس القريب المنتج بالذات فمحال أن يكون مركباً مما هو أكبر من مقدمتين بل يجب أن يكون الأصغر بالقوة أو بالفعل داخلا تحت الحكم الكلي الذي هو الأكبر أو عليه، فالتكبير إذن إن وقع وليس بسبب الاستقراء وغيره من الوجوه المذكورة فهو بسبب تركيب القياس؛ وذلك تأليف قياس من مقدمتين كل واحدة منهما أو أحدهما مفتقرة إلى قياس يبينها فيتركب قياسان أحدهما على المقدمة والآخر على المطلوب إن كان المحتاج إلى البيان مقدمة واحدة، وثلاثة أقيسة إن كان المحتاج كل واحدة من المقدمتين ومقدمات المطلوب زوج لا محالة. وكذا المقدمات المنتجة لإحدى المقدمتين أو لكل واحدة منهما؛ والنتيجة أيضًا لكلتيهما زوج وجمع الزوج إلى الزوج زوج، فإذن مقدمات القياسات البسيطة والمركبة أزواج. فإن كان عددها فردًا فهناك إما زيادة وإما نقصان وإما عقم إن كان لا يتم بزيادة ولا يستوي بنقصان. أما الذي بنقصان فهو أن تسقط الكبرى لظهورها أو لأنه لو [٢٤٤ظ صرّح بها لظهر كذبها أو لأنها قد ظهرت بقياس ظهورا / يعني عن أن يجعل بعد ذلك مقدمة فتسقط وتؤتي بالمقدمة الأخرى؛ فتكون المقدمات المذكورة بالفعل ثلاثا، وتنتج المطلوب؛ إذ الصغرى لأحد هذه الأسباب الثلاثة. وإن كان على كل واحدة من مقدمتي القياس قياس يبعد أن يسقطا معا بل إن أسقط
So we say that the premises may exceed two for one of these three reasons: either those premises are not premises of the immediate syllogism but rather premises that produce premises that are closer; or they are presented on the way of induction and analogy, so they are not premises of the syllogism itself but premises of induction by which the correctness of one of the premises of the syllogism is recognized; or they are on neither of these two ways, and that occurs in various ways: among them, that they are presented for deception (al-ḥīla), which are intended to hide the conclusion of the syllogism, not to show it: for necessary premises, if they are presented purely with a conjecture of what follows from the conclusion, and one knows how it follows, then it becomes difficult to accept them. So if the way of its following is hidden and it is thought that they are useless, especially due to the mixture of what is useless, the composition of the association in accepting them, and this is in dialectic, examination, and refined opinion. Among them, that they are presented for embellishment (al-zīna), which are premises with which one tries to beautify speech; their existence or non-existence relative to the purpose is like. Among them, that they are presented for clarification (al-īḍāḥ), which are premises whose presentation is for confirmation, like parallels, division of lafẓ, and transition from one expression to another, and similar things known in the book of dialectic, for all these are for support in explanation. As for the immediate syllogism that produces by itself, it is impossible for it to be composed of more than two premises, rather the minor must be potentially or actually included under the universal judgment which is the major, or over it. So expansion (takbīr), then, if it occurs and not due to induction and other mentioned ways, it is due to the composition of the syllogism; and that is composing a syllogism from two premises each one of them or one of them needing a syllogism to clarify it, so two syllogisms are composed, one on the premise and the other on the desired conclusion, if that which needs clarification is one premise, and three syllogisms if each of the two premises needs, and the premises of the desired conclusion are necessarily a pair. Likewise, the premises producing one of the two premises or each one of them, and the result also for both of them is a pair, and adding a pair to a pair gives a pair. So then the premises of simple and compound syllogisms are pairs. If their number is odd, then there is either increase or decrease or sterility if it is not completed by increase nor equalized by decrease. As for that which is by decrease: it is that the major is dropped because of its obviousness, or because if it were stated its falsity would appear, or because it has appeared through a syllogism in a way that means that it cannot be made a premise afterwards, so it is dropped and the other premise is brought, so the actually mentioned premises are three, and they produce the desired conclusion, since the minor for one of these three reasons. And if for each of the two premises of the syllogism there is a syllogism, it is unlikely that both are dropped together, but rather if dropped
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage discusses why premises may exceed two in a syllogistic argument and then establishes that in simple and compound syllogisms the total number of premises is always even. The author lists three reasons for additional premises: 1) they serve as intermediate steps to produce the immediate premises, 2) they are presented inductively or analogically to establish the truth of a premise, and 3) they are introduced for rhetorical purposes: deception (ḥīla) to hide the conclusion, embellishment (zīna) to beautify speech, or clarification (īḍāḥ) to provide support. Then the author asserts that a simple productive syllogism cannot have more than two premises; if more are needed, it becomes compound. In compound syllogisms, each additional premise must be paired, so the total number of premises is always even. If an odd number appears, it is due to dropping a premise (usually the major) because it is obvious or would reveal falsity. The author illustrates this with an example where the major is dropped, leaving three premises. The passage ends with a discussion of the possibility of dropping both premises, which is unlikely.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the primary purpose of premises introduced for al-ḥīla?
A) To clarify the conclusion
B) To hide the conclusion
C) To beautify the speech
D) To provide induction

Key Takeaway
Extra premises in a syllogism serve rhetorical or inductive functions; they do not alter the fundamental bipartite nature of the immediate syllogism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find an example from a real Islamic scholarly debate (e.g., from al-Ghazālī's Tahāfut al-Falāsifa) and classify any extra premises as ḥīla, zīna, or īḍāḥ.
?
Construct a short syllogism on a fiqh issue (e.g., wine is forbidden) and then add a premise of clarification (e.g., 'wine is an intoxicant') and explain why it still does not affect the core syllogism.
